If you see a corrosive ulcer in a place that can tolerate a cauterization with fire, then make many cauterizations, small and large iron rods, according to what is suitable for the place where the corrosive ulcer is.
Then cauterize it on every side until you eradicate the entire corruption, and nothing of it remains deeply.
Then touch the same place for three days, and apply over the cauterized area a mixture of sulphur and oil, until all the scabs are eradicated and all corruption.
Then heal it with ointments that generate flesh.
If therefore you see that after three days flesh is born with healthy birth, in which there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not: then apply the cautery again over that which remains of the corrupted places.
The corroding is treated with sharp medicine whenever it becomes necessary. It itself rises in place of the cautery.
But indeed a cautery with fire is of quicker aid: and I have already spoken of its cauterization with sharp medicine in the division: therefore take it there when you need it.
On the method of cauterizing in pimples and warts.
Chapter 55.
It often happens that this disease occurs in the lower parts of the feet: and it is a coarse, foul thing, which hurts the foot.
And indeed the cautery for them is according to two methods: a cautery with fire, and a cautery with hot water.
As for the one with fire, it is like a hollow cautery of a bird's feather, which is made from iron, according to the quantity that the nail contains on all sides: and let its edges be subtle.
Then apply it ignited over the nail. Then move the hand with the cautery around the nail, until the cautery penetrates to the depth of the nail: and leave it for three days, until it begins to discharge pus. Then plaster it with woodland marshes, crushed with salt: and leave the plaster over it at night. For it will be eradicated from its own roots.
Then treat the place with a generating ointment of flesh, until it heals.
What if these are not turned and often happen on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet: then you will administer to them a cauterization with hot water: and it is, that you take a small tube made of bronze, or iron, or a feather quill: and apply it to the nail, or the scar. Then throw into the tube a small quantity of hot water: hold your hand and turn it with compression of your hand for a little while, so that the margin of the tube is impressed on the root of the nail, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the nail: and let the weak one wait a little upon the biting of the hot water for one hour.
Then release it, for the nail is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with them one after another, until you come to all of them that are on the body.
Then treat them after they have been eradicated, with what generates flesh from ointments.
On the method of cauterizing for the Tremor.
When there happens to someone a Tremor from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or another: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauterizations, or five on the vertebrae of the back, and between all the vertebrae a cauterization, and on his chest a cauterization, and on his stomach a cauterization.
For the Tremor is stilled with an oily cauterization, and it hastens health from cold sickness.
On the method of cauterizing for Albahtir (also known as Al Zahrawi or Albucasis
Chapter 57.
When there are expelled from the body of Albucasis foul things, that is, pimples, which are made from corrupted thick matter.
It is necessary therefore in the beginning when it appears, that they should be cauterized on every part of the head with a subtle cauterization of myrtle wood, whose ends have been ignited with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lens-shaped cauterization.
And when they are cauterized with an adamant, at the beginning of its expulsion according to this method: and it does not increase: and the excess is broken, making them, and it heals from them.
Nevertheless it is necessary that it be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Of the method of cauterizing in a flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
It often happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut at the wound, which occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or at the cauterization of a limb, and similar things to these: and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When therefore it happens to anyone, then put your hand quickly to the orifice of the artery, and place your index finger on it: and press it strongly, until the blood is stopped under your finger, and does not go out from it at all.
Then put small olive cauterizations on the fire, and a large number of them: and blow upon them, until they are ignited strongly.
Then take one of them, either small, or large, according to the wound and the place, where the artery is disrupted, and put the cauterization on the vein itself, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cauterization, until the blood is stopped.
Which if it is expelled when the finger is lifted from the orifice of the artery, and the cauterization is extinguished, then quickly take another cauterization, from the cauterizations which are prepared in the fire.
I will not cease to do that one thing after another until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you confuse the nerve which is there, and a malicious accident happens to the weak place.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to cut it off: especially when the artery is large, unless with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its ends are contracted, and the blood is cut off. Or let it be tied with a strong ligature thread. Or let medicines of the proper property be placed on it, which are to cut off blood, and be tightened with a decent constriction poultice.
He who administers the cutting off of it with a ligature, and tightening with a bandage, or application of cauterizing things, and similar, does not harm it deeply, except rarely.
If it happens to anyone and there is not a doctor present to him, nor medicine: then let him begin with the placing of the index finger on the mouth of the vein itself, as we have described, and a strong constriction of it, until the blood is stopped from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed from on top of it with water of vehement cold, always, until the blood coagulates and thickens, and is cut off.
And meanwhile, let him consider what it needs from a cautery or from medicine.
Of the Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in wounds, and their similar things.
We have described in the first chapter, every kind of illness for which it is suitable to combine with fire a cauterization and a burning medicine, and its opportunities, its causes, its instruments, and the forms of cauterizations.
And I have set forth chapters from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter by the same method: that the investigator should investigate what is being sought.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to learn, O sons, what in this chapter is that which is from the terror of what is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that there be a more vigorous caution, since in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an apostema, or opening of a cyst, or cure of an ulcer, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which are associated trembling, and fear, and death often occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest what is hesitant falls upon you, for sometimes men of such a kind fall to you in this art with kinds of weaknesses. For there is one who becomes angry with his own illness, and death is easy for him: on account of the vehemence of what he finds from his weakness and the length of his suffering. And an illness is from fear which signifies death.
And there is one who gives his attention to you, and says it to you, hoping for health, and his illness is destructive.
Therefore, it is not necessary that you consent to him who comes to you from those whose manner is such. And let your caution be more vigorous than your greed and your rapaciousness. And do not presume anything about it, unless after certain knowledge, which proves to you that which leads to successful treatment.
And that you use in the cure of all your weaknesses, prognosis and indication of that to which health is more suitable.
For in it is to you assistance for acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you, O sons, with His understanding, and may He not withhold from you guidance and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I have indeed arranged this Chapter by distinguishing the chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that may be alleviated for you what you want from it, and what you need.
On the cure of water that accumulates in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this weakness often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife tightly grasps the head of the child without gentleness.
And it also happens sometimes from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I have indeed not seen this illness in others than in children: and for every one of them whom I saw, death hastened: therefore it seemed to me that it was advisable to omit the operation in it.
And I have indeed seen one of them, a child, whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit up by himself, because of the size of his head: and the moisture increased, until he died.
And this moisture indeed either accumulates between the skin and the bone: or accumulates under the bone above the brain.
And the operation on it, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should make a single incision in the middle of the head, in width: and let the length of the incision be about two knots, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of a spatula.
If therefore the humidity is greatly increased and the apostema is larger, then put two fissures, according to this form.
And if the humidity is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the commissures of the head open on every side, and water is pressed out when you press it with your hand inward, and it is not hidden from you: then it is necessary that you find in the middle of the head three fissures, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract the whole humidity. Then bind the fissures with bandages and poultices. Then anoint it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then undo the bandage, and cure the wound with dressings and ointments.
And do not cease to bind the head with equality. And feed the weak one with all dry food of little humidity, until the member is comforted and healed.
And another method of incision is, that you consider where appears the magnitude of the apostema, and the collection of water.
For sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or in the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Make an incision therefore where it appears to you that the apostema and collection of water are, and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And beware, lest you cut the artery, and there occurs a flow of blood, and the weak one dies from that flow with an evacuation of humidity.
On the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter Two.
When there occurs to someone a cataract to the eyes, or to the breast, and it grows old, and the cure of medicines does not help: then the last cure is in that, an incision of these two arteries.
It is necessary therefore when one wishes to make an incision on them, that the weak head should be shaved. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then let the weak one constrict his neck, with any cloth of his. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are places which are behind the ears and depressed: and are rarely hidden, except in certain men.
Then mark with cauterization above both: and make an incision on both with a sharp spatula on both sides, the incision reaching to the mouth: and let it be according to the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the spatula under the artery, and make an incision upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined.
For when the vein is cut, blood comes out with a pulsating discharge, which jumps inward with a frequent leap.
If however the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the quantity of a space of three fingers. Then mark with cauterization, and probe until you reach the mouth.
And what is necessary to emit from blood, is six ounces, and perhaps you will emit more or less from it. All that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the weak person and the fullness of their artery.
Then consider the ulcer. Then if anything remains at the mouth from the abscess, then cut it, so that a hot aposteme does not occur: then constrict the ulcer with linen threads from a linen cloth.
Then treat it with an ointment, until it is healed.
On the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter 3.
When an old headache occurs to a man, or sharp catarrh due to acidic humidities and heat in the temples, or an old swelling, and things similar to them: and it is treated with modes of curative medicine, and it does not help: then we are experienced in these diseases in the extraction of arteries from the temples, or cauterization of them, as we will narrate.
And the mode of operation in extracting them, is that he should shave the weak hairs that are on the temples, and aim at the apparent artery in the temple. It itself is shown to you by its own pulse, and is rarely hidden except in few men, or by the swelling of cold.
What if it is hidden from you, then he should bind his head tightly with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or fumigate the place with hot water, until it appears to you with a clear appearance. Then take the spatula, which I formed a little before. Then scratch the skin with it with ease, until you penetrate to the artery. Then throw a hook into it, and pull it out upwards, until you pull it out of the skin, and make it emerge from the tunics that are under it on every side.
If the artery is delicate, then entwine it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the quantity by which its extremities are lengthened and contracted, and there does not occur a flow of blood. For if it is not cut and if it is not severed, the blood does not rise at all.
Then let out from the blood about six ounces, up to three.
If the artery is large, then you should tie it in two places with a double thread, strong, of silk: or of a chord of alhohod, so that putrefaction does not come to it before consolidation of the wound, and there occurs a flow of blood.
Then cut away the redundancy which is between the two ligaments. Do that at that hour or after another hour.
And if you use it, cauterize it with a cauterizer up to the mouth, with a razor-shaped cauterizer, until its extremities are cut and it will stay in place for the same operation.
And it is a better work, unless he who undergoes it has a fever or is of a hot complexion. For the cauterizer is one of those things which help to end moistures. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cotton, and powders well made should be put on top of it.
And after the dressing, it should be treated with dry medicines which make flesh grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If meanwhile while you are operating there occurs a flow of blood from the artery, then quickly cut it with a cauterizer or fill the place with cotton, and constrict your hand until the bleeding is stopped. What if none of those things are present to you, then put your finger on the place until the blood coagulates. And moisten the place with water of vehement cold, until the acute pain is stilled and constricted according to what is necessary.
And from what is lighter and easier than the extraction of the artery, it is to cauterize the vein with this razor-shaped cauterizer having two blades: let the sharpness be similar to the pumice stone. However they are much less sharp than the two razor blades. For if they are as sharp as the two razors, then cold will quickly seize both, and they do not cut the flesh with speed. When there is some thickness in them, heat of fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: when this work is done it is better than any work, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cauterium.
And let the space that is between two clamps be the quantity of the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with cauterization; and apply both heated cauteries, until they reach the mouth, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between the extremities may protrude. For they are not consolidated with this cauterization thoroughly.
Chapter 4.
On the treatment of a flow of tears from the eyes, with repeated ulcers.
When there are constant tears, and their flow is from the veins which are on the outside of the skull above the head: and no medicine can cure them with medical treatment, and you see the face of the weak already red, and he feels in his forehead a crawling, as is the crawling of ants: and his eyes are tired, moist, so that their lashes are already corroded by corrosion: and his eyelids are inflamed from the sharpness of tears: then the ancients have indicated for their cure this operation, and they have named it cure with Ense.
And it is that you should advise the weak person to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then cut on the forehead three equally distant fissures along the length of the forehead: and let the length of the fissure be about two fingers, and one fissure be equidistant to the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length a little from the movement of the arms, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries which are in both sides: and stretch out your hand from the continuation of the two jaws, and let the space of all incision be the quantity of three joined fingers: and have for yourself a piece of prepared sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you will dry the blood.
Then introduce a spatula of two sharp extremities, whose following shape is a moxa. From the incision which is on the temporal side, up to the second incision: and excavate with it the whole skin, which is between the two incisions, with a sifac, which is above the mouth. Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then extract this spatula, and introduce into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp cultellum, blunt on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its shape.
And put its sharp side towards the flesh joined with the skin, and its blunt side towards the mouth: and push it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to another fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed out equal, press out the places from the clots of coagulated blood.
Then put into every fissure a plaster made from old cotton, and put over them little balls soaked in wine, and oil, or vinegar. And see to it that hot apostema does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the ligament, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then treat it with royal ointment, and other things which are used to treat wounds: until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of sharp tears and discharges from the eyes from the interior of the head.
When there occur to someone sharp and acid discharges, corrosive, constant: and you see his eyes weakened, pale, and already their appearance is debilitated: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and in the depth of the head is sharp pain, laesing, and consequent sneezing: then conclude from these accidents, that these matters and discharges do not come, unless from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you should order the weak to scratch their own forehead. Then cut in it a single fissure in the middle of the forehead, or another one across its width, and start with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be up to the bone: and stretch your hand from the muscles of the temples, which move during mastication: until when the bone is laid bare: and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with a cotton thread, or with a linen thread. Then tie above with little pads: and soak the pads with wine and oil, so that hot apostema does not occur.
And when you untie it, and you see that the hot apostema has already begun to diminish: then it is necessary that you rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow in it.
Then treat it with a regimen of excitation that makes flesh grow: as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of rose oil: and prepare from them an plaster, and administer to the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter 6.
On the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or similar to stone, such as iron and glass: or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans: or it is a flowing thing, such as water and vinegar, and things like them: or some animal.
When therefore a stone falls into the ear, or a kind of stone from those that do not adhere, nor grow in the ear, expose the Ear to the sun: if therefore you see the stone, then instill into the Ear something from violet oil, or from sissamino. Then administer the extraction of it, and make sneeze with the condisi, and stop up the nostrils with a dressing at the time of sneezing, after you put in the opening of the ear fragments of cloth, or wool, and stretch the ear upwards.
For it often comes out with this treatment.
And if it does not come out, then administer the extraction of it with Gest, that is, with fine Pizigarolis: whose form is this.
If therefore it comes out with Gest, it is good.
And if not, then administer the extraction of it with a fine hook, bent at the end, of little doubling.
If therefore it does not come out by that, then make a tube of bronze, and introduce the extremity of the tube into the opening of the ear much, and stop up what is in the circumference of the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no passage except for the tube: then extract with your blowing attraction forcibly.
For it often comes out with what we have described. If however it does not come out in this manner: then take from helkalimbat, or from visco, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, a small amount: and put it at the extremity of the staff after you have wrapped around it a bandage decently placed: then introduce it into the opening of the ear with facility, after you have freed the ear from humidity.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have described, then proceed to the cutting, before there occurs an aposteme of heat, or a spasm.
And the manner of the cutting is, as in phlebotomy the weaker vein first in the head: and let out from him blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the weak one sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and find a small cutting in the root of the ear at its fat in the depressed place itself: and let the cutting be of a lunar figure, until you reach the stone: then remove it with that which is possible for you from instruments.
Then with speed and care treat the fissure until it is healed.
What if a thing falls into the ear is from one of the grains which grow and become inflamed then administer its extraction.
If it does not come out to you at the exit, then take a thin, subtle spatula of this form.
And administer with it the cutting off of that species of grains which fall into the ear and do not do that when you think that grain has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear until you put many a poultice: then extract them with forceps or with a subtle instrument or with suction as we have said, for the extraction of it will be alleviated.
In the water which enters into the ear, it is necessary that he uses a weak blowing with condensing at first: and now he has filled his ear with some old cottar: and it lies upon that ear in which there is water. If therefore it comes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, gentle, and heat them with fire for a little while, and gently insert one of them into the opening of his ear: and let him jump on his foot on that side, and strike with another stone above the stone which is in the ear. Therefore do not cease to do that with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out thus, that you take a parsnip or a juniper and insert one extremity of it into the ear: and burn the other extremity with fire until it is greatly charred. Then take another juniper and do that many times until all the water comes out: and extract it with a cannula according to what was previously said regarding the stone.
In the extraction of an animal that enters into it, if it is small in body, such as a frog, and similar to it, treat it as I have said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the sense: then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks: and its matter is easier than all those that are held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division, and in the treatment of distillations, and your treatment does not work: then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If, therefore, something appears there from worms, then extract it with forceps, or with subtle hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, whose form is this, narrow below, wide above: and introduce the subtle extremity into the ear according to the quantity that the weak part tolerates: then suck with a strong suction. Do it many times, until all the worms come out.
If, however, they do not obey you to exit, then stop up the surroundings of the cannula with wax, as I said regarding the stone.
What if they do not come out with what we have said: then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the infection of worms: and you will find it in the treatment of distillations.
And let your pouring be what you pour oil and medicines into the ear with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it of silver, or of copper, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let it be then that the pusher which is in the concavity of the cannula, is made from copper, bent sideways. And if you use it, take a rod and insert cotton into its extremity with a suitable insertion: then pour oil, or juice, or what you wish of these medicines into the cannula: and let it be in the ear: then introduce the rod with the cotton above, and express it with your hand with tempered compression, until the oil is pushed into the concavity of the hearing, and the weak part feels it inside: and what is poured into the ear, should already be warmed with fire for a short time: and beware lest there be something that distills into it cold mucus: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of an injury to the ear.
Sometimes some infants are born from their mothers' wombs not perforated.
And it has already happened to some men that an obstruction occurred in the channel of the ear from a wound or flesh grown in it.
And this obstruction is sometimes made in the deep opening of the ear, which sight does not reach: and sometimes it happens at the outer opening of the ear, and sight reaches it.
That which occurs in the deep ear, and which the gaze does not comprehend, is in many cases difficult to cure.
And in that which the sight reaches, you should place the ear of the infirm person in the sun, and look into it: then if you see the obstruction appearing, open it with a spatula, the shape of which is this which follows, let its extremity be where there is a small width, whose part is acute: and let the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not hurt the ear.
If the obstruction is from flesh grown in the ear's opening, then take it with a subtle hook, and cut it with gentle violence, until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is in the deep ear, then take a subtle and smooth wire, warm it with fire for a little while: then put it into the ear's opening.
If you perceive the obstruction with this, that the infirm person feels with a heaviness of hearing, try to open it with ease: and beware, lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then put into the ear a dressing according to the quantity of the width of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days, until you are secure about the consolidation of the wound.
Take a piece of linen cloth and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle powdered tragacanth over it.
If you see the ear has already formed a postule: then it is necessary to extract the linen cloth, and allow it to remain with another cloth, which you will have wrapped in a kerchief made with rose oil, until the hot postule subsides: then treat it until it is healed.
If a flow of blood should happen: then submerge a sponge or cloths in cold water and apply it to the ear: and use the remainder of the treatment which stops the flow of blood as described in the treatment of distillations.
On the treatment of eyelids with nightshade.
Chapter 8.
These are the nightshades that occur on the eyelids of the eye, when they are moist and when they are dry.
It is necessary therefore to pull them with forceps, or with a needle, and to cut them with scissors from their roots. Then apply powdered tragacanth to the places.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a subtle lentiform cauter.
And it is better to cauterize them after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
On the treatment of granulations of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter 9.
It often happens on the eyelids something which resembles a grain in its solidity and hardness, and because of that it is named a granule: and it is an aggregation of thick humidity on the upper and lower eyelid.
The operation therefore for them is, that you should examine whether the granule proceeds from what appears on the eyelid, and moves to every part, and its treatment is easy.
Find then an incision above it in width. Then scrape it from every part with ease, until it is hollow. Then hang it with a hook, and cut it off.
If however the cutting away of it is not prepared for you, unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a small incision, then it harms the weak one in something.
What if the incision is large: then bring it together with a suture: and treat it, until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For an ointment restores it, and fleshes it over.
And if a polyp is hanging towards the interior of the ear turned to the inner surface, then invert the eyelid, and hang the polyp with a hook: besides which you need an incision, and press it from every part.
What if the eyelid is lengthened by an incision, it does not harm it in anything.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the polyp, with salt water: and treat the place with that which fleshes over, until the weak one is healed.
On the treatment of eyelids.
Chapter 10.
Axirnath is a tumor that generates in the tissues of the upper eyelid: and it happens most to children: and it weighs on their eyes, and they are afflicted with discharges in the mornings, and they cannot look at the light of the sun, because tears flow to them, and because of this you see them always rubbing their faces, and sleeping on them, and their eyelids are humid under their eyebrows, which an inflammation has already covered: and a visible sign of Axirnath is to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the weak person puts their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare on it ligatures, and make from that thread a circle according to the quantity that the instrument contains on all sides. Then put it above it, and press with your fingers from all sides, so that the instrument gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that humidity with a spatula by making an incision through the width, and let not the incision be greater than what is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you cut the skin entirely, until you reach the instrument, for in many dispositions the instrument proceeds from the incision according to the shape of a piece of white fat.
Extract it then with a cloth, which you had inserted between your fingers to the outside, and you turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed: and beware, lest you put in the incision, so that you do not reach with the spatula to the eye, and harm it.
If however the instrument does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you put a little into the incision with ease, until the instrument proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and put them on the place, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who pound salt, and put it in a concavity of the incision, so that it dissolves what remains of that humidity.
Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however a hot abscess forms in the place, then treat it with cooling plasters, until it is healed.
Concerning the modes of relaxing the eye, that is, when the eyelid becomes soft, whence it happens that it protrudes as it should not.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow in the eyelids of the eye beyond the normal course under the natural hairs, and become long: then they themselves harm the eye, and cause various diseases to occur, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and a white spot, until it becomes the cause of destruction of the eye.
And the diseases of the eye are treated in four ways. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization. Or diseases are cured by Ataxmir through incision and suture. Or with a tube, as I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the weak one on your lap. Then turn back the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it is thus turned back, it is good. And if not: introduce the needle into it, where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid, where the hairs grow: and draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with the thread.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid under the additional hairs with the spatula going from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then extract the thread, and put under the eyelid a small cotton ball from cotton, or a piece of cloth: then mark above the eyelid with ink, which should be in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the figure be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid: because that already varies among men.
For there are some in whom it is necessary to make an incision from the eyelid a large quantity, according to the quantity which becomes soft from the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above the two lines that you marked, and begin at the larger tear duct up to the smaller tear duct, and let there be a single fissure near the natural hairs, just as is the thickness of the radius.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape out the whole thing. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, with a needle and thread of fine wool: and wipe away the blood, and make adhere what overflows from the threads above the two eyebrows, with certain soothing things, if you want to do that: and if not, do not trouble.
Then leave the suture and threads until three days, or four: afterwards cure it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is elevated at the time of the wound's sigillation and its closure: but the future one will be better.
This method of curing the Ataxmir was taught by the ancients.
However, there is concern for the weak, and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method for the Ataxmir is also: and it is that you mark on the eyelid a figure which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks, according to this shape which follows, then cut away what overflows with small forceps, according to this shape, with equality of the incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it does not suffice: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and bring together between your fingers the threads with equality.
Then elevate your hand to the level of elevation and elevate the skin over which the scar is marked, as we have described.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture: and cure it, until it heals.
If a tumor or an aposteme occurs at your incision or your fissure: but apply that aposteme with a plaster, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And when the eyelids also happen to convert their hairs.
It is necessary therefore that you use what we have said on the incision: but the suture of both is more artificial. On the treatment of ataxmia with canes doesn't translate well, a better translation would be: On the cure
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and split the fissure which is within, according to what I have described. Then skillfully make two canes, or two thin little wooden rods: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and the width of them is less than the width of a spatula: and you have already made an incision at the extremities of both from both parts where you hold the threads.
Then bring together both with subtlety over that which exceeds from the eyelid of the eye, and tie the two canes from both parts tightly, and leave it for some days. For the skin that is tied will die, and become black, and rot, until it falls off by itself.
If then it is delayed, then cut it with forceps. Then cure it, until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is elevated: and the hairs do not protrude.
And this is the form of the two canes, which you will make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter 12.
On the removal of painful piles from the eye with a needle.
The operation on it is, that you take a subtle needle, and insert into it a thread from fine silk, thinly. Then you will gather its ends, and tie each knot with a subtle knot very firmly: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then you will put on the Alinxotati another subtle thread under it briefly, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then the weak one will put his head on your lap, and it should be near the sun so that the operation may be declared: since it is a subtle operation, or a minor one.
Then you will insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you extract from above the natural pile.
Then you will pull the pile up to the Alinxotati: and insert into the Alinxotati that pile: if it is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then you will pull your hand with it, until the pile goes out with the Alinxotati from the eyelid with the natural pile.
If the Alinxotati is pulled and the pile does not go out with it: you will pull the Alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you put in it, until the Alinxotati goes out from the upper hole, and you see it with the sight of the eye: then you pull the Alinxotati, and the thread: and extract both, and close the eye: and keep it closed for two days, or three, until the pile adheres to its place, and flesh is born on top of it.
If the pile is short, then you will attach to it a long pile from the natural piles, and remove it with that: or leave it until it comes out after a space, and then remove it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental, therefore, occurs from the consolidation of a wound, or a cut, or a cauterization, and similar to those.
And the method of operation in it is, that you find that consolidation, and that you separate its lips, and you put between them a linen pad, and you bind it, until it heals.
And it is not proper to use in the cure of it things that dry and constrict, for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was; instead use in it things that soften, such as fenugreek: and apply with water, in which has been cooked althea, flaxseed: and anoint with dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some oil: and wrap with it a linen pad, and cure with it.
And the treasure of your cure is that you strive with all ingenuity, that it not be consolidated, according to the shape which it had at first.
And this operation that we have spoken of, is not anything but a certain correction of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return to what it was entirely.
On the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs on the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one occurs from a wound, or a tear, or a cauterization, and those similar things.
And the method of operation on it is that you take a needle, in which there is a double thread, and you insert it into the flesh, and you make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread passes through both extremities of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If then the figure of the eyelid returns according to what is proper, it is good.
And if not, take a rasp, and put it in the cleft, and shape the eyelid with it: and make two clefts on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two clefts be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is formed from them: until when they are gathered the figure of them becomes this figure, and it is the letter lambda of the Greek alphabet.
Then remove that flesh in quantity, which is its sharp lower width, from that which follows the eyelid. Then gather the separated parts: sew it with a thread of wool.
Then treat them with what we have said, concerning softening medicines and plasters, until they heal.
And if a fistula occurs from the cleft, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you find a flat cleft under the hair of the eyelids, also according to what has preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a plaster, according to what we have said.
And the summary of the discussion about the treatment of Alxatrati, when it is above or below, is that the operation runs in it according to what is prepared from the form of Alxatrati.
For it itself is of many diversities in form: and the prudent artisan prepares his skill with whatever method is possible for him, so that he makes the figure return to its natural form, or near it, and the infirmity itself, according to what is convenient for him from the operation and instruments in multiple arrangements.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesions of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many men, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore you should insert the extremity of a probe under the eyelid and elevate it upwards; or extend it with a hook: then cut the adhesion with a subtle spatula, which is not sharp in its incision, but rather blunt for a short time: so that the weak place is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed, and cuts the eye with its sharpness: and your incision should be as if you were excavating the adhesion with ease, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Pour then salty water into the eye, and wash it with water: or distill into it a distilled product made from dissolved zinc oxide and water.
Then separate between the eyelid and eye with a linen thread: and put over the eye a woolen cloth soaked in the white of the egg: and after the third day use the aforementioned ointment, and the consolidations, until it is healed.
Of the incision of the nail and the prominence of the flesh of tears.
Chapter 16.
Know that the nail is in two modes. Or it is nerve-like: and is similar to a hard, subtle callus. Or it is not nerve-like, similar to the humidity of frozen white substance: which when touched with iron, or a hook tries to seize it, it is cut off, and the hook does not stick to it.
And both types do not begin except from the larger tear duct, until which it advances gradually, until it covers the pupil, and prevents light and movement of the eye.
And the method of operating on it is that the weak person should place their head in your lap. Then open their eyes, and elevate the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with the claw of a pair of pincers, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a thread from the hairs of a horse, or an ox, or a cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle for a short while, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scratch the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were sewing it with the thread up to its very back.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a subtle spatula, or with small forceps: and distinguish the natural flesh of the tear duct, so that there does not occur from the cutting a continual flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail is extended with pincers alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then scratch it with a blunt spatula, whose shape is this.
And beware, lest the spatula touch the horny tunic, and a rupture occur in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the cutting, throw into the eye something of ground salt, or made from ginger, and distinguish it until another day. Then treat it with what is necessary, until it is healed.
For if the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a claw be fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines, and healed as I have described in the division.
For if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the weak person, and take a subtle, blunt, and not very sharp spatula, according to this shape. And scratch the nail from above with subtlety. What if you see that it is rarefied and shattered, and the file impresses on the eye, then distill from your hour in the eye, either with lapis or with rubie, or with tritum salt: and cover the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation over it, until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye to the contrary: then cease it, and cure the abscess, until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation over it with scraping, until it is healed.
The flesh of the tear duct, if it harms the eye with great annoyance, should be suspended with a hook, and a portion of it cut away: and do not make the incision ultimate, so that the flow of tears is not interrupted.
Then pour into the tear duct either rubie, or made from lapis, until it is healed.
On the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter 17.
There is born in certain eyes of men a red flesh, either composite or superficial, until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps converts the eyelids to the outside: and it is like the rose called balaustine.
And the operation for it is also that the weak one places his head on your lap. Then open his eyes, and collect all that flesh with hooks converging to it: and hold it with one forceps, or tongs.
Then cut the flesh at first, until the whole of it is finished with the incision. And beware of the eye, so that you do not harm it during the operation: and let your incision be what you cut with, or with the spatula, which we described in the incision of the nail: or with small forceps, with which the tissue is collected, according to the form that will come after this chapter.
When the incision is completed and the entire white cataract is removed, then fill the eye with powdered salt, or instill into it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines, and put on the eye a poultice of defrored cotton with the whites of eggs, so that it may be secured from hot apostema.
If something of the cataract remains and hot blood dominates you, and a hot apostema threatens, then leave the eye and cure it with that which settles the apostema.
Then repeat the treatment over it until it is healed.
And do likewise with the flesh that grows in the eye from this condition.
However, it is necessary that you delegate the cure of every weak eye to its natural or accidental disposition, operating with iron: and do not insert your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
On the collection of pus from the eye.
Chapter 18.
Pus are red veins that gather on the eye and prevent its proper function, and weaken the eye over time.
It is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which there is pus, is strong and does not have any other disease besides the pus, then collect its pus.
And it is that you order the weak person to put their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook or two according to your judgment and let the hook be a subtle duplication, or let there be two hooks graduated on one shaft.
Then collect those veins with subtle forceps with subtlety, and wipe away the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and dissolved, and beware lest you wound the eye with the extremities of the spatula.
And let your operation be at midday before the sun, and you should be firm in your operation indeed: so that you do not cut except those veins.
And when you have excavated, then distill into the eye either a green or a red liquid: so that its acidity eats away what remains of the cataract.
If however it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then apply a plaster to the eye with what soothes the pain of the eye: and leave it for some days, until the pain of it subsides: and it is secure from the hot aposteme: then repeat over it the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
On the reduction of the nose to its proper shape.
Chapter 19.
Physicians call it Arissati's fistula, which you will treat with a cautery, or with corrosive medicine, according to what its description has previously stated.
And if it does not heal, then there is no ingenuity in it: except that you find a gathering of pus above the aposteme at its birthplace, and extract all the humidity which is in it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
When therefore the bone is exposed, and you see in it a corruption or blackness, then scrape it with an instrument whose form is this.
And it is called the Asper of the head.
Let it be made from Indian iron, and let its head be round like a grindstone, which is dotted with the dotting of a file, or the dotting of a fine awl: and put it on the corrupted place of the bone.
Then turn it between your fingers, and press your hand for a little while, until you know that the corruption has already been scraped: and do this several times.
Then restore the place with medicines which are astringent and contracting.
If then the place is consolidated, and flesh is born in it, and the flow of blood is cut off: and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and is not apostemated, and no accident happens to it: then you should know that it is already healed.
And if not, then there is no ingenuity in it, except to reduce the fistula to the opening of the nose, according to this form.
And it is, that you scrape the bone with iron, or with sharp medicine: and when the bone is scraped, then take an instrument according to this form, let it be a metal extremity, triangular: and its wood is a pyramid extended to the extremity, as you see.
Then put that on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and stretch out your hand a little from the eye. Then turn your hand with the instrument, until it penetrates the bone, and the weak one feels it: by that which he finds the air coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Then restore the place with medicines, astringent and drying, as I have taught you.
When then flesh is born, and the place is hardened: then the matter that was flowing to the exterior, returns to the nose: it is then lighter on the weak one.
On the reduction of the prominence of the Eye.
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes with its entire mass, and no harm or diminution happens to the sight: then it is necessary that you relieve the weak one: then you bleed him: afterwards you put cups on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then put on the eye an ointment that is made from acacia, and aloe, and olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then tie the eye above with a plaster and many poultices.
And the method of poultices is that you take many soft cloths and fold each cloth four times: make many of them: then put one on top of another, a second according to the quantity that the eye needs: then tie over them with a hard cloth tightly.
And repeat the applications with suction without scarification.
Then remove the applications, and bind the eye tightly by day and night.
If however the bandage is again softened, then it is necessary that it be tied firmly: then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye already returned to its own coat, it is good: and if not, then repeat the plaster, and poultices, and binding, and applications, until it be healed.
On the incision of the conjunctiva.
Chapter 21.
When there occurs a disruption in the conjunctiva, then it protrudes and comes out from between the eyelids like a grain of the conjunctiva, and the appearance of the man is disfigured because of that, and he wants to cut it: then the operation for it is, according to what I will describe: and it is, that you insert a needle at the root of the conjunctiva from the lower part to the upper.
Then insert another needle where there is a double thread on the tear duct side, and make it penetrate: and leave the first needle according to its own position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the conjunctiva around the needle: and tie it tightly.
Then remove the needle, and put over the eye a soft woolen cloth on the white of the eye: and leave it, until the thread falls out with the conjunctiva.
Then treat the eye with what comforts it, until the wound is healed.
And when the wound is constricted on all sides with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it is perforated with a subtle spatula, which penetrates to the depth of the eye, and the white humidity flows out, and the eye sinks down, and enters at once: then constrict it, until it is healed.
On the treatment of cataract.
Chapter 22.
This disease which is called Alcomnati, is nothing but a cataract, which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not the eye itself.
And the method of operating on it is, that you make the weak one sit on a prepared seat, or a directed one.
Then take the head of it with your hands on both sides, and move it, until you see the cataract pass to the lower parts of the eye. Then it is fixed, and does not come down, and the light is freed: and the weak one sees things as he used to see: and if it does not go down below, know that it is water.
If however the descent of the cataract is not prepared with what we have said, then make the weak one sit between your hands. Then take a subtle spatula, and probe with it above the corneal membrane slightly at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the corona, until the cataract comes out.
When it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey: then let it be treated with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
On the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter 23.
We have mentioned the types of water in division, and what is suitable for perforation, as the speech declares and explains, then take that same one according to health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the weak person sit between your hands squarely, opposite to the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye, and press it firmly: then with your right hand, the eyelid of his eye, if the eye in which there is water, is the left eye: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take with your right hand the cataract needle, if the eye is the left one: or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then place the point of the needle near the corona through the thickness of the radius in the same white part of the eye apparent with tearlessness of the lesser one: then push the needle with strength: and you withdraw with it your hand, until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you feel, that the needle has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the length of the journey of the needle to the depth be the length of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness: and this is the corona of the eye. For you will see it in the same pupil of the vision of the eye, on account of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the needle up to the place where the water is. Then press it downward one after another.
If then the water descends at once, then the weak person sees that which is above what opens his vision at once, and the needle in his eye.
Then rest a little. If then the water descends, then put it down again, except that the needle should come out.
When I raise the figure, and it does not rise: then extract the arrow with ease: and you will free your hand little by little.
Then dissolve something from clear salt of gem in water: and wash the infected eye with it. Then put outside the whole eye a pledget or a cloth soaked in rose oil and eyebath white of egg: and tie it with that to the healthy eye.
We however, and those who are of our time, put on it ground cumin with egg white.
What if the arrow does not obey you to enter into the eye on account of its hardness, because it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary that you take a spatula, which is called Alberid, whose shape is this.
Make a perforation with it therefore the same conjunctiva, and do not put your hand in the opening: and it is not except that you make way to the arrow place subtle.
Then insert the arrow, according to what we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let there be prepared for the infirm a dormitory decently made, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark house: and let him be prohibited from all movements, and from coughing: and let his food be that which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its disposition until the third day.
Then undo it in that dark house, and try his vision: and make him see things. Then put back the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary that he do that at the hour of cure, or immediately after the incision: but rather it is necessary to avoid that: because water ascends quickly through violent sight.
If however a hot aposteme occurs: then it is necessary that you loosen the eye before the seventh: and straighten it with what sits on the aposteme, until it is seated: then without its strength, and put over its face an oral bandage which regulates its vision under it for some days, while it exists in that dark house.
Then let it go out of the house gradually, and let it turn to its own habits.
And know that the pupil is not excavated in the glassy body many times: for then it dares above the operation.
And now indeed it has come to us a certain one of those who are from Alayrach, who said that a perforated sink was made in Alayrach, through which water is sucked.
Therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that it is new.
And these are forms of species from a sink, so that it stands over them who are ignorant of them.
Let them be made from their own bronze: and let their extremities be with the same subtlety triangular, and the extremities pointed.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes there are born in the nose various added flesh, among which is something that resembles a scorpion of many feet: and from it is a cancerous, stony, dark-colored flesh: and from it is what is a smooth, not dark-colored flesh.
In that therefore which is of these flesh smooth, not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the weak one sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open its nostrils, and throw a hook into those flesh: then draw them to the outside, and cut what you have grasped of them with a subtle sharp spatula on one side, until you know that the whole flesh has now been removed.
What if something remains of it whose excision is not possible then scratch that with one of the subtle instruments with ease until nothing of it remains. If however blood dominates you or there occurs a hot aposteme then apply to it what is necessary or it is one of the malignant apostemes then cauterize it quickly until the blood is cut off and all flesh is removed.
Then let vinegar and water or wine be thrown into the nose after the incision. If however the nose opens and moisture proceeds from it to the palate then know that it is already healthy.
If indeed the moisture does not penetrate according to what is necessary then know that there is flesh born inside the upper bones of the nose to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then therefore it is necessary that you insert a thread of linen which has some thickness and knots on it many knots and put between each knot the quantity of a finger or less and let the weak one thrust so that he puts one end of the thread into the nose with the radius or as far as possible for him after which having made it similar to azar he draws his smelling thing until it reaches the caassus and comes out above his throat.
And many times indeed boys do something similar to this in schools and it is an easy thing for one who wants.
Then put two ends of the thread, one end which comes out above the nose and the other which remains in the nose. Then take a minister to saw the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread and put into the nose after wiping away the blood a linen cloth which you have soaked in Egyptian ointment do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then put at the end of the thing a tin pipe into the nose, for some days, until it is healed.
If however it needs a treatment that dries it out, use that.
For making a head purging, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or of brass: which should be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you want make a closed pipe, like a reed.
And the shell-shaped instrument for making a head purging, should be full, perforated: which should have a handle at its end, with which you hold it, when you heat in it oil, or whatever you want from juices and flowing things.
Chapter 25.
Often a polyp grows at the end of the nose, and it increases and grows with time, until its appearance becomes foul.
And because of that, it is necessary to cauterize it at the beginning of its appearance, and to eradicate all of it. Then put on the place, or a cauterization, or a medicine that burns which takes its place.
If therefore the cutting of it is delayed, until it becomes large, then consider if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, of little sensation: then do not touch it with iron: because it is a cancerous aposteme.
For I have often seen, that one who cuts a malignant tumor, and it happens from it great evil to the one who has it.
And if the tumor is of a soft touch, not of a dark color, and you see that cutting is possible in its entirety, then evacuate the weak part, and cut it without fear, without anxiety: and treat the place with what dries and constricts, until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
On the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
We should know when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, an operation is hardly performed on it, except in certain men.
It is necessary therefore when anything happens to someone, that you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you should bring together the lips of the wound with a suture, afterwards cure it until it heals.
And if the lips of a solution of continuity are already separated and each part has become healthy, then it is necessary that you should cut away every part from its own skin until it becomes bloody. Then bring together the two lips with a suture, and tie them, and powder over it dragon's blood and pounded olibanum: and put over the powders a piece of diafinicon plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters: and leave it tied for two days, or three. Then untie it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads separate of themselves. Then cure it with an ointment, until it heals.
The method of the suture is, that you should bring together a solution of continuity, either with needles, as we have described in the suture of the belly: or bring together the solution with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter 27.
On the extraction of tumors of the lips.
Many men have lumps occurring inside their lips, hard excrescences similar to grains of herb, and some of them are small and others large.
Therefore it is necessary that you should turn back the lip, and find the node above it, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it on every side.
Then fill the space after the incision with zegi paste until the blood stops.
Then let it be mixed with vinegar and the place where there is a styptic property be treated with it until the wounds heal.
Chapter 28.
On the incision of the flesh born above the gum.
Frequently there is a birth of flesh above the gum which the ancients call abuluss.
It is necessary therefore that you suspend it with a hook, or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
Then put on the place zegi paste, or other styptic powders, which are astringent.
For if that flesh returns after the treatment, for it often returns, then cut it a second time and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter 29.
On the scraping of teeth with iron.
Sometimes there collect on the surfaces of teeth from the inner and outer part, and between the gums rough, foul, and sometimes blackened cortices, and become citrine and green, until corruption comes from that to the gum, and the teeth are made foul by it.
It is necessary therefore that you make the weak person sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, in which the cortices appear to you, and things similar to sand: until nothing remains of them, and do likewise with the blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and other things until they are cleaned.
If therefore that which is in them from the beginning of scraping is removed, it is good: and if not, then repeat the scraping over them on one day, a second, and a third, until the utmost care is reached in what you use.
And you should know that the tooth needs scrapers of various forms and many shapes, according to which your operation is prepared: namely, because the scaper with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scaper with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of scrapers should be prepared by you, as you see.
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of toothache.
It is necessary that you cure the tooth of its pain with all ingenuity, and delay in extracting it. For it is not that the place of it is supplied when it is extracted: since it is a lofty substance: until when there is no excuse for extracting it, then it is necessary when it is weakened to extract it, so that it may be strengthened and certified by you, the aching tooth.
For often the weak pain deceives, and it seems that it is in a tooth and extracts it: then the pain does not cease, until the weak tooth is extracted.
We have already seen that from the operation of bloodletting doctors.
When therefore the aching tooth is certified by you itself, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides: then move it with your fingers, or with subtle forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in it with a good grip, and the head of the weak tooth between your knees: which you have already held well, so that it does not move: then pull the tooth according to straightness, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and introduce it under the tooth from all sides with ease, and check its movement, as you did at first.
If the dense is perforated or corroded, then you should fill that opening with a cloth and tie it with a slender thread so that it does not break in the hour of your constriction on it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act skillfully with scarification around the gum on all sides abundantly.
And preserve your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns on the weak part from that evil which is greater than its pain at first.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant bleeders do in their haste and boldness to eradicate it, beyond what we have administered.
For they often cause great weaknesses to come upon men, which are difficult to break, and all of its roots remain, or some of them: or so that they extract it with certain bones of the jaw: as we have often testified to that.
Then let it be cleansed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
What if a flow of blood happens from the places (for it often happens), then take something of gum and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if the gum does not work for you.
The shape of the slender forceps which move the dense thing at first, should be of long extremities, with a short arm, thick, so that they do not double when pressed on the tooth: they should be as you see of thick arms, until when you squeeze with them, they do not displace themselves, nor double: of short extremities, and they should be made from hardened iron or well-made steel, of straight extremities: and in their extremities there should be teeth, some of which enter into others, and squeeze with a decent constriction, strong.
And sometimes the extremities are made in the shape of a parrot's beak which is called ciconia, and they are strong for constriction.
Chapter 31
On the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of broken jaw bones.
When something remains after the eradication of a tooth that is broken, then it is necessary to put on the spot a plaster with butter for one or two days, until the spot softens: then insert into it a spit or forceps: whose extremities are similar to a bird's beak which is called ciconia. The shape of this is a pair of forceps.
Let their extremities be made like a file for teeth, or like a tusk of ivory.
If then it does not come out for you with these forceps, then it is necessary that you cut above the root, and remove all the flesh, and insert an instrument which is called a small lever, whose shape is this.
Let one extremity be short, a little thick: and let it not be hollow, so that it does not break.
If then the root comes out through that, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to use with these other instruments whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first has a triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
Shape of a triangular extremity that is subtle.
And with other instruments and iron tools which were previously mentioned in the scraping of teeth.
And also let there be a using of this instrument which is similar to a large hook, of this shape, of triangular extremity twisted, in which there is a certain thickness, so that it does not break: and let it not be hollow.
And know that the instruments of teeth are many: and similarly the remaining instruments are not counted, and the skilled artisan, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument, according to which he signifies the operation and the ailments themselves.
Since there are some ailments for which the ancients did not mention an instrument, because of the diversity of their species.
If the bone breaks from the jaw or from other bones of the mouth or becomes putrid: then probe over it in its own place with that which suits it from the instruments and forceps which I mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with gestures, the form of which is this.
Let there be in it something of thickness gradually, so that it may be tightened, or the bone may be held with it, and it does not flee and is steady, until the bone goes out: and restore the place with medicines suitable for that.
If however there is a small amount of rot in the bone, then scrape it from its own rot, and cure it, until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the sawing of prominent teeth above the others.
When the teeth are prominent not according to their natural course, a foul shape is made by it: and especially when it happens in women, and girls, namely those with veins.
It is necessary therefore that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth, and does not have its place, or it is possible to saw it, nor to file it: then extract it: and if it is connected with another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, the form of which is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made from Indian iron, of sharp extremity, strongly.
And let your cutting be that which you cut with for many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, so that another of the teeth is not disturbed.
If however it is prominent and exposed to its own filing: then file it with a file made from Indian iron, the form of which is this.
Let it be entirely made from Indian iron, and its handle be of a lesser thickness, strongly pointed, like a file with which they make needles: with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not disturbed and falls: then smooth it, finally scrape it with certain scrapers.
What if it is dense, from which a part will be broken and injures the tongue: then it is necessary to smooth it also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is made even, and flattened, and does not injure the tongue, nor corrupt the speech.
Chapter 33.
Of the gold wire for filing the teeth.
When the front teeth are subject to concussion and commotion from a blow or a fall: and the weak one cannot chew on what he eats so that it does not fall: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment was of no use: then the idea for them is to be fastened with a gold wire: because silver becomes greenish and putrefies after days: but gold never changes.
And let the thread be moderate in thinness and thickness according to the quantity that it can hold between the teeth.
And the shape of the filing indeed is, that you take the thread and insert double of it between two healthy teeth: then weave with both ends of the thread between the loose teeth, whether one or several, until you reach with the web to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the texture until the part from which you began: and pull your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is not moved outwardly: and let your constriction with which you tighten the thread be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not protrude.
Then cut the overflowing ends of the thread with scissors: and gather both, and shape them with forceps: and hide them between a healthy tooth and a loose tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue: then let it be tightened thus while it remains.
But if it becomes undone, or is cut, tighten them with another thread, and it will stay with that for the whole time.
And this is the shape of teeth, and the figure of the extraction of two healthy teeth, and of two movable teeth, as you see.
And when it happens, that one tooth or two teeth return to their places after a fall, and are retained as we have described, and remain: and this is not done except by an artisan who is prudent and experienced.
And when something is fitted from the bones of the cheeks: and it is made from it in the form of a tooth, and it is put in the place, from which the tooth is strengthened and tightened, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Sometimes the ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already consolidated.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the weak person in your lap, and elevate their tongue: then cut that nervous ligament in width, until the tongue is freed from its retention.
What if in it there is something of hardness and knotting, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then introduce an instrument into that, and cut it in width, until you cut the ligament, and the knotting is loosened.
And beware, lest there be a fissure in the depth of the flesh, and you cut the artery there, and there occurs a flow of blood.
Then let the area of the incision be colluted with rose water, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then put under the tongue a plaster made of flax, which the weak person holds during the whole night: so that it does not consolidate again.
If however there occurs a flow of blood, then put on the place a poultice of toasted barley. If the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a sluggish cauterization iron, which suits that purpose: afterwards cure it with the remaining cure, until it is healed.
