Chapter 35.
Sometimes there occurs under the tongue an apostema resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from performing its natural function: and perhaps it is so enlarged, until it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is, that you open the mouth of the infirm in front of the sun: and examine the apostema.
If you see it to be of a dark or black color, hard, which the infirm person does not feel: then do not touch it, because it is cancer.
And if it leans to whiteness, where there is humidity, then cast a hook into it, and cut it with a subtle spatula, and free it from every part. What if the blood overcomes you during the time of your operation, then put above it pounded zegi, until the blood is stopped: afterwards return to your operation, until you extract it with its completeness.
Then let it be glued with vinegar and salt: and cure it with the remaining cure suitable for that, until it is healed.
Of the cure of the apostema of the two tonsils.
Chapter 36.
Sometimes there occur in the throat glands, that is, glandules, which resemble almonds, which occur in the form of nuts: and are named the two Amygdala: which when you cure with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If it is a hard apostema, of dark color, of little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it also with iron, fearing the flow of blood, but rather leave it, until it matures. And then you will either perforate it or it will rupture by itself.
And if it is of a white color, round, and its root is subtle: then it should be cut.
And the operation on it is, that you consider before its operation, if its aposteme has cooled quiet completion, or is diminished by some diminution: then make the weak sit in front of the sun, and its head be in your bosom: and open its mouth, and let the servant receive between his hands, and press its tongue downwards with an instrument, of this form.
Let it be made from silver, or copper, which is subtle like a knife.
When you press its tongue with it, and the aposteme is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the amygdala, and extract it to the outside as much as you can, except that you pull with it something of the tunics.
Then cut it with an instrument, of which the shape is as follows. It is similar to forceps: nevertheless its extremities are curved, so that the orifice of each of them is directed towards the tip of the other: whence take care that it be made from iron, or steel that has been tempered.
If however this instrument is not available, then cut it with a spatulum, of which the shape is as follows. Let one side be sharp, and the other side not sharp.
And after you have cut one amygdala, cut the other according to the same pattern of cutting.
Then after the cutting, let the weak person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
And if a flow of blood happens to him, let him gargle with water in which the bark of pomegranates have been boiled: or leaves of myrtle, and similar things among astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped: then take care of it, until it is healed.
And when abscesses appear in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut it therefore equally on both sides as I said in the incision of the two tonsils.
And I indeed have already treated a woman for an abscess that had developed in her throat, which was pulling towards the trachea, with little sensation, which had nearly closed her throat: and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and it had already prohibited her from eating, and drinking water: and she would have reached death, if she had remained one day, or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches, until they came out above the two nostrils.
I forced it therefore hastily, and I fixed one of them with a hook. Then I pulled it, and a large portion was pulled out from it.
Then I cut it off, where it was grasped coming from the nostril.
Then I did to it what proceeded from the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth, and pressed her tongue: and afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess: then I cut off from it a part of it, and there did not flow from it except a little blood: therefore the throat of the woman was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then she took some food.
I did not therefore cease to cut into that abscess many times for a long time: and the abscess followed in the place of what I was cutting, until it was prolonged too much and with that.
I cauterized therefore, and I burned the abscess in the throat.
It stood therefore from increasing: then I walked away from the area, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
Of the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter 37
When the cold descends to the ulcers and becomes inflamed and is long: then it itself is called perpendicular: and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it itself is called a UAV or haw, however,  more properly translated as "ulcer" as uva.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not work: and you see that the hot aposteme has quieted from it, and is subtle: then it is necessary to cut it.
And that which is gathered from it round, and is not long, and is bloody, or of a dark reddish color, or black, and has no sensation: then it is necessary to avoid cutting it. For in it there is fear for the weak.
It is necessary therefore when you see it according to the measure which I spoke of from its whiteness and its length, that you make the weak one sit before the sun: and press his tongue with an instrument, whose description was given before: then tie a hook in the mouth, and pull it downwards: and cut it with one of the two instruments which I mentioned in the cutting of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you do not cut from it except that which exceeds the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will injure the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting use that which we described in the cutting of the two tonsils: and treat it, until it is healed.
If however the weak one trembles at the cutting: then it is necessary that you use ingenuity in the cauterization of it without fear and trembling.
And the method of cauterization on it is not, except with sharp water: and it is, that you put the head of the weak one on your shoulder. Then press his tongue with the instrument which we mentioned. Then take from the water which I mentioned to you in the chapter on cauterization, and mix with it quicklime, and put it not too thick, nor too subtle, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, whose form is this.
Let the extremities of it in which the medicine is put be hollow, such as the hollow of a spoon or a radius.
And put the instrument with medicine on the wound itself: and let the weak person be lying on his side, so that saliva runs out of his mouth from the inner medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat, and harms him.
Then hold your hand with medicine. And you apply it to the wound for the space of half an hour, until you see that it is already blackened and the biting of the medicine has settled.
And if you want to take a piece of cloth, and wrap it around the extremity of the radius, and dip the cloth with medicine, and insert the radius with the cloth into the tube from above, until the cloth adheres to the wound.
Do that many times, until you achieve what you want concerning the cauterization of the wound. Then leave it alone: for it will dry and fall off after three days, or four.
What if you judge that you should repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization cleanse around the wound with cloth soaked in butter, and clean what is around it from medicine: and wash with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with poultices, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This therefore is the most wholesome method of treatment, surpassing incision and remote from fear.
And sometimes wounds are also treated with what is more subtle than cauterization and incision: for they are treated with fumigation, in this manner. Take calamine, and hyssop, and origan, and rue, and sseah, and chamomile, and abrotan, and similar things from herbs: and gather all of them, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and boil: and the pot should be coated with plaster; and let there be an opening in the middle of the pot, upon which the instrument called concave is placed in this manner.
Let it be made from silver or brass: and the extremity should be inserted, in which there is a grain, into the mouth of the infirm, until the vapor ascends to the palate above the tube, until the palate becomes very obscured.
Then rub over it many times, until it is scraped away.
And beware, that you do not make this cure at the beginning of an apostema. For a plaster adds to an apostema: and it is not proper to make that unless at the decline of the apostema when it has become cool.
If however this instrument is not present to you, then take a cane, and place the bark of an oak at its extremity: so that the mouth of the infirm does not burn: for the bark of the oak prohibits vapor from burning the mouth: and this is from good cure with health.
On the extraction of spines, and bones from the jaw.
Chapter 38.
Often a bone or a fish spine, or something else sticks to the throat.
It is necessary therefore to extract that which of them appears visible upon which the sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument at the base, and it becomes clear to you what is in the throat.
And that which does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you make the infirm vomit before the food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps the thing attached will come out through vomiting.
Let the weak person swallow a piece of nap, or a root of lettuce, or let him swallow a morsel of dry bread: or let him take a piece of dried marine sponge: and tie it in a thread, then let him swallow it. When it reaches the place where there are spines, or bones, and is extracted, then it comes out.
If it does not come out with what we have said: then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a Rod for a little while, and at its end let there be a curvature, which it may introduce into the throat with ease: and let it have its head elevated upwards.
And let it beware of the touch of its epiglottis, so that coughing does not befall it, and let it push the mouth, or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that which the weak person introduces, is better: on account of its knowledge of the place of the thing annexed: and it pushes below, or draws his hand upwards with the instrument, all that according to the quantity that is prepared for it, until it comes out.
Of the extraction of bloodsuckers.
Chapter 39.
When a bloodsucker is treated with what I have said in the division on curation with medicines, and it does not come out, then examine in the throat of the weak person before the sun, or at the window after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument that I described to you.
If your sight then falls upon the bloodsucker, extract it with a small hook, or with gentle gestures.
If it is not possible with these: then take a concave tube, and introduce it into the throat of the weak person until near the throat: then introduce into the concavity of the tube ignited iron with fire. Let him do that many times: and let the weak man abstain from water for the whole day.
Then let him receive a full drink of cold water, and open his mouth in it: and let him gargle his mouth with it, and not swallow anything of it: and let him move the water with one hand after another. For a leech sticks to the skin when it feels the water falling.
And if it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the weak person with cumin seeds, or with asphalt with an instrument that I have described in the fumigation of the ulcer.
Let him do that many times, for it will fall out by itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which there are burning prunes or laurel with fire: and the pot is covered with a cover, in the middle of which there is a hole: and let the end of the instrument be placed in that hole.
Then throw in the fumigation, and let the weak person put his mouth on the end of the tube, and let him close his mouth, so that the fumigation does not come out, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall out.
For if it does not fall out, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the weak person endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
It is necessary therefore that it comes out with this treatment.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls on the same thing seen.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is introduced into the throat. And its end is similar to a bird's beak, which is called ciconia, in which there is roughness of lime: when it seizes something, it does not release it at all.
On the opening of abscesses, and their cutting, and treatment in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many modes, according to which the treatment of them is remembered, namely one and only one in this book: and they are diverse in the perforation of them, and the cutting of them from two modes.
Of which one is from the species of abscess by itself, and that which it includes from humors.
And the second mode is on account of the places, in which it occurs in the body.
For an abscess that occurs on the head is different from an abscess occurring on the anus: and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints: and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses there are some which should not be perforated, unless after the maturation of pus in them and its completion: and of them there are some which should be perforated while they are raw and do not mature according to completion.
Like abscesses that are near the joints.
For when an abscess occurs near a joint and its existence is prolonged until it putrefies what is in its surroundings, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: which is a cause closer to the contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess near a principal member: for if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either it or through that principal member.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it while it is immature: and it is not proper to perforate a raw abscess, of incomplete digestion, unless it is near the anus, so that it does not putrefy deeply and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become in its end what does not heal.
It is necessary that you know the time of the perforation of the abscess, which has matured according to its completion: and it is at the sedation of pain, the recession of fever, and the diminution of redness, and pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess: and the remaining signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lowest place of the abscess, and of the most prominent elevation: and let the perforation be going in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and the places of the arms, and tendons, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places, to which there is not a reflexion.
In those however which reflect, let it go with the perforation according to that membrane.
When indeed the abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better that you omit the perforation of it, until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to its completion.
For if you perforate it before that, the course of the pus is prolonged from it, and there is much filth and foulness, and perhaps the lips of it will become calloused, and its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body, by necessity: or according to that which the membrane needs.
And it is necessary that you administer to small abscesses a small perforation, and to large abscesses a large perforation: or many sections by similarity of the magnitude of the abscess.
And there are abscesses, in which it is necessary that the skin be concave: and an incision be made when already it has been made like a cloth, and it has been made to its limit, which already is dead: as that which happens in many, such as buboes.
And of them there are those which cut with a section having three angles. And from them is that which is cut like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
Like an abscess in the groin.
And from them are those for which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar ones from the incisions.
That one indeed which has no head, as are the lenient superficial abscesses, should be perforated with a superficial perforation, or merely a flat one.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is thick, and has already collected much pus, and you perforate it, that you not hurry and extract all the pus at that hour: but rather extract a part of it from it. Then dress the abscess until another day. Then extract something from the pus again.
Do that many times according to the condition, until it goes out: especially if the person is weak and lacking in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or a decrepit old man: for the vital spirit of an animal is often resolved with the discharge of pus: and perhaps the weak person dies, and you do not perceive.
Beware therefore this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, by which you perforate the abscesses, it is necessary that you clean the wound: and take care. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then administer dressings made from linen, or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and there are many openings of the incision, then it is necessary that you insert a dressing into every opening, until they meet in the middle.
And if you have cut away a part of the skin in the abscess, or have hollowed it out around the edges: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth, or as when a fabric is woven, or wool from linen without moisture: and tie it up until the third day.
Then anoint it, and treat it with what is suitable from the ointment, until it is healed.
If however a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then administer cold water and vinegar, after which the cloth is soaked in them: and you put it on the place of the flow of blood many times.
If however the flow of blood persists, then it is necessary that you administer a treatment and a cure and powders, which I have described in many places in this our book, and from division.
If however it is in the time of winter, and the place of the abscess is of many nerves: then it is necessary that you pour in powders with wine, and hot oil: and you put them on the places.
And if it is in summer, and the places are fleshy: then it is necessary that you put powders soaked in water, and oil, or wine and oil, and all of that is cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary that you loosen the abscess, and wipe it clean, and administer in its cure what is suitable for it, until it is healed.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the cure of abscesses in general.
According to the path of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess, in what way it is a route to its cure, chapter by chapter, individually.
Chapter 41.
Of the section on abscesses that occur on the skin of the head.
There occur on the skin of the head small abscesses, and they are of the species of Knots, which are contained in sacs, which are attached to them: as if they themselves were the gizzard's husks.
And there are many species of them.
Some of them are fatty: and some of them contain a moisture similar to slime: and some of them contain a moisture similar to that of roasted and boiled things: and similar things, and some of them are hard and stony.
And in the cutting of all, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as the arterial cut does not cause a contradiction.
And the operation in the cutting of them is, that you should try it with an instrument, whose form will be described after this, which is called Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is that which contains moisture, then dry it by a cut according to the length of the superficial cut: according to this form.
And begin with the cut from line b to line g. When therefore the moisture is evacuated, then scratch out the cyst which contains that moisture, and do not leave any of it inside.
For it returns many times, if something of it remains.
Then submerge the wound in Egyptian ointment, if it is present: and if not, in water and salt: and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it corrodes what remains of the cyst.
Then repeat over the wound with the Egyptian ointment a second and third time, and if you apply it to it, until you are confident that nothing remains of the cyst, then treat the wound with an ointment, until it is healed.
Which if the abscess contains a fatty knot: then cut it according to this form, having the shape of a cross: and put unguents between the lips of the cut, and scratch it out from every side: and test your skill in the extraction of the tunic which contains it.
If however the artery causes a complication for you, then do what I will tell you afterwards.
And do similarly in the case of a stone, from cutting and curing, the same as we have said.
And the cutting above a stone abscess is easier: since there is little blood and moisture. And there are sometimes certain apostemata which are on the head in which there is not humidity within.
Which is: because I cut into an apostema on the head of an old woman, and I found the apostema like a hard and solid stone, rough, white, which could not be broken: and if someone were to strike it by throwing, it would wound.
In other accidental apostemata on the head, besides these, namely the apostemata which occur on the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the section of all of them should be a superficial section.
Or make a perforation in them always from the lower part, so that the flow of blood may be alleviated downwards.
Then treat them with what is suitable for them from the treatment.
On the cutting of Strangulations in the neck.
Chapter 42.
These apostemata often occur on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groin: and sometimes they occur on the rest of the body.
And above those that occur on the neck, there are one or many: and they generate from a cause: and every Strangulation of them is within its own proper tunic, as it is in knots and apostemata of the head, as we have described.
And there are many kinds of these Strangulations. And of them some are stony: and of them some contain humidities: and of them some are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that one which you see of them with a good disposition to the touch, whose manifest appearance is near to the color of the skin, and moves to every part, and is not attached with the nerves of the neck, nor with the gullet, nor with the artery, nor is it deep: it is necessary that you cut it with a superficial section from the upper part to the lower part of the body in a line, and remove it from every part: and stretch the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if it is necessary for it, as we have said in the case of apostemata of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is above the caution, that the vein or nerve is not cut.
And let the spatula be not sharp, lest you add your hand with the incision, or disturb the weak part, and cut what is not necessary.
If you cut the vein, or artery, it prohibits you from operation, then put on the wound a poultice of zeolite, or some powders that staunch blood, and constrict the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the inflammation subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins to putrefy.
For blood is staunched by a tunic of flesh, therefore return to your operation, until you complete.
Then probe with your index finger, if any of the small fragments of the strophules remain: therefore cut them, and clean them.
What if there is in the root of the strophule a large vein: then it is necessary that you do not cut that strophule from its root: but rather it is necessary that you tie it with a double strong thread, and let it go until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton, which you have already steeped in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it.
If the whole strophule is cut, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound: and sew them up at once, after you see that nothing remains superfluous.
If you see that something remains superfluous on account of the size of the strophule, then it is necessary that you proceed to cut its root, and extract it: and administer the suture which we mentioned.
And which of the strophules contains humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation: that the place of its maturation may appear to you: and put the perforation from that which follows below the body, as we have said.
Then administer a dressing with ointment after the perforation, and similar things to it, so that it corrodes what remains from the corruption, until when you see the wound is already clean: then treat it with a generating ointment of flesh, until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Of the cutting of the epiglottis from an abscess that happens inside the throat.
The ancients said this incision on the epiglottis, and I did not see anyone in our land who has done it: and this is the writing of their speech.
In those having a synanche, it is necessary that the incision of the epiglottis be made: since there is no passage through it: because all the veins and lung are weak.
In those indeed who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no illness in the canal, it is necessary to use the incision of the epiglottis, to flee from the destruction which is made by the suffocation.
It is necessary therefore when the need arises, that we cut the epiglottis under three circles of the epiglottis, or four, a small incision in width, in that which is between two circles in quantity, so that it is an incision in the sifac, not in the cartilage: and this place indeed is suitable for the incision, because it is deprived of flesh, and the vessels of blood are far from it.
What if the doctor is timid, then it is necessary that he stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him pierce the skin, until when he reaches the canal: let him divert the vessels of blood, if he sees any of them.
Then let him cut the sifac which we have mentioned: and it is indicated above the incision of the canal from the phlegm that exits from it, with what disrupts it, and from the cutting off of the voice.
And leave the open wound for some time.
When the hour arrives in which the suppuration is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and suture it by itself without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that make flesh grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The sum of this sermon which we have narrated is not an exposition, except when they see that one of these apostemata has already strangled the throat of the infirm, and the infirm has been lifted up from death, and his breaths are about to be cut off: they proceed to cut the Epiglottis, so that the infirm may breathe over the place of the wound with a certain respiration, and may be saved from death: and for this reason they have ordered to leave the wound open, until the malignity of the disease is consumed, and its malignity is for three days, and as it were; then therefore they have ordered to suture the wound, and to cure it, until it is healed.
And that which I have seen by myself is, that a certain servant girl took a knife, and thrust it into her throat, and cut with it a part of the canal of the lung.
And I was called to the cure of it, and I found her vomiting, as is the vomiting of the dead.
I probed therefore at the wound, and I found blood which had come out from the wound a little. I was confounded therefore that she had not cut the vein, nor the windpipe: and air came out from the wound.
I treated therefore the wound, and I cured it, until it was healed: and nothing happened to the servant girl, except hoarseness of voice: and she returned after days to her better dispositions.
Therefore I say: that in the cutting of the Epiglottis there is no fear.
On the surgical cutting of the throat hernia.
Chapter 44
This condition called Hernia of the throat is a large abscess, secondary to the color of the body, and it occurs frequently in women.
And it is secondary to two modes: for it is either natural or it is accidental.
In the natural one certainly there is no trickery.
The accidental one, indeed, is secondary to two modes: of which one is similar to fatty knots. And the other mode is similar to an abscess that occurs from a knotting of the artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not suitable that a presumptuous action be taken on it with iron, unless it is small: and you will test it, and investigate it with an introducer. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty knot, and it is not suspended with any of the veins: then cut it, as knots are cut: and extract it with what it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst: and if not, then thoroughly examine all of it. Then treat the place with what is suitable for the cure, until it heals.
Chapter 45
On the cutting of the species of Knots.
There are many species of knots, and I have already mentioned all their species in the division: and it is necessary that I explain here the difference between a Knot and an Excrescence: since it causes doubt.
I say therefore, that when there is an Excrescence, there is heat, and fever, and violent pains, until the ebullition of the overflowing is settled, and putrefaction is complete: then indeed the fever and heat subside.
With a Knot, however, there is no heat, nor fever, nor pains, and it contains them in cysts of the flesh, which is their proper sac, and it is secondary to the color of the body: and its inception is like a seed, and it forms like a lump, and is greater and lesser.
And they are divided into two types: either fatty or containing moisture. And the colors of moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
It is necessary therefore, when you come to the cure of a Knot, that you examine it, and inquire into it first with an instrument which is called Intromissum, according to which its form will appear in the chapter which is after this.
And the method of inquiring into abscesses and all Knots is that you take this instrument and insert it into the moist place which you find in the abscess, and you move your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then put your hand last according to the measure of the size of the abscess.
Then extract the Intromissum, and consider that which comes out in its track.
If therefore a running moisture comes out, of whatever color it may be: then dry it with a superficial incision, according to what I said concerning other abscesses.
And if moisture does not come out in the track of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty: dry therefore above it with an incision having the form of a cross, according to this form, as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and scratch the skin from every part with ease: and guard the cyst if you can above it, so that you extract it healthy with the Knot.
If the cyst however is ruptured during the operation, and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then extract it fragment by fragment, until nothing of it remains.
For if a little or much of it remains, the Knot returns according to the greatest part.
If it conquers you, and a little of it remains: then fill the wound at the completion with certain corrosive powders: and bind the wound, and put on top of it what sits on the abscess, a warm application, and cure it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
If then it is a large knot, then sew its two lips, and cure it with what flesh grows.
If however the contrary vein is pulsating or not pulsating: then treat it, and fill the place of the zegi with trito, and leave it tightened for two days, or three, until the wound putrefies, and the ebullition of blood quiets down: then return to cutting that which remains of the knot.
Chapter 46.
Of the forms of instruments for cutting and boring.
There are forms of the introdgers, and they are of three kinds.
For there is one large, and there is one medium, and there is one small.
Let them be made from iron of Al-fuld quadratus of the extremities decent, so that they hold fast the entrance in the abscess.
And these are the forms of the EXPERIENCIA, and that is to say, the perforators: and one of them is named Beris, that is, the perforator: and they are of three kinds.
For there is one large, and one medium, and one small.
They convene, that they investigate through them the abscesses, and the exit wounds, and the fistulas, and the bubos from what is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and blunt, like large needles with which sutures are sewn, namely of citrin, or of copper, or of iron, or of silver.
And the better ones of them are those which are made from citrin.
And the EXPERIENCIA are also made from black lead, which convene that they experiment with fistulas, whose concavities are reflections, so that they reflect with gentleness with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. For there are some of them long, some of them medium, and some of them short, according to the quantity of the width of the fistula and the tightness of it.
The shape of wounds, and they are of many species.
Since some of them are simple, namely those which have one treatment, and they are of three species, as you see, large, medium, and small.
And some of them are blind wounds, and they are of three species.
And some of them are wounds having two treatments, and they are of three species: and some of them are wounds having three treatments, and they are of three species: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of surgical knives, with which they cut and excavate knots and abscesses: and they are of three species: since some of them are large, medium, and small.
Their extremities with which the cutting is done are sharp, the other extremities are not sharp: and they are not placed thus, except so that with them a juncture is made in the excavation of knots near the fear of cutting a vein, or nerve: and so that it may be healed through this, and find tranquility for a time from the burning which it found near the excavation of an abscess.
They are made of brass, and are similar to a file, with which is made a fine powder: and on the broad extremity there is a hidden point of a spatula: and it goes inside and outside when you wish, as you see.
The shape of spatulas which are hidden between the fingers near the perforation of abscesses: and the weak do not perceive them: and they are of three species, large, medium, and small.
The shapes of cauteries with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three species, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of brass or copper, round, a little long, as you see: and let them be subtle.
And it is necessary that these species of bleedings are great and small with you, so that you cut blood with them quickly when necessary, and then when medicine is not present with you.
However, you will not use them for cutting blood in every part of the body, and you will not use them except in fleshy places: such as the thighs, and hips, and arms, and breasts, and belly, and groin, and similar fleshy and moist ones.
And sometimes small instruments similar to the skins of whips are made from them, according to their shape: with which blood is cut when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at the cutting of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter 47.
On the treatment of men's breasts which resemble women's breasts.
Sometimes the breasts of certain men become inflated during a fullness of sleep (that is, when they begin to be polluted in sleep) until they resemble women's breasts: and they remain as foul apostemas.
He who therefore abhors this, should cut above the breasts a lunar section, according to this shape from line b to line g. Then scrape out all the fat. Afterwards fill the wound with an incarnative medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture, and cure it, until it is healed.
If however the breast declines to the lower parts and becomes soft because of its size, as it happens with women: then it is necessary that two sections similar in shape to a crescent moon be made on its upper sides, each of which is connected to the other at their edges, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this shape from line b to line g. Afterwards scrape off the skin which is between the two lips, and remove the fat, and administer what we said about sutures and powders, which is necessary for the cure, until it is healed.
If you do not close the incision of what you need to cut, because of the unease of the weak or because of the flow of blood: then you should fill the wound with a submersed corrosive ointment, and leave it, until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then cure it, until it is healed.
On the perforation of an aposteme, which occurs under the tongue.
Chapter 48.
These apostemes which occur under the tongue, are of the hard species of Strophulons, which contain cysts, and of them are those which contain humors.
Which therefore are of those which contain humors, it is necessary that they be cut with a lunar section, according to this form, from line b. to line g., when therefore all that is in the aposteme is evacuated of blood, then fill it with old oil, and leave it until another day.
Then let the plaster be removed, and let it be cured with an ointment suitable for that, until it is healed.
Which if the aposteme was of the species of nodes of Strophulons, then cut above it, as we have said in the section on Strophulons equally.
If however the wound extends in this so that it emits pus, then administer to it a cautery, according to what we have said in what has been previously mentioned.
Chapter 49.
On the cutting of an aposteme which occurs because of the artery and vein.
When the artery is torn and the skin which is above it becomes consolidated, then often an aposteme occurs from it: and similarly it happens to the veins also, so that an aposteme and inflammation occur in them.
And the signs by which it is known that an aposteme and inflammation are because of the artery and vein, are: since when the aposteme is because of the artery, it is long, collected in the depth of the body: and when you press the aposteme with your fingers, you feel as if there were a hissing.
And that which is on account of the Vein, is round in the manifest body.
And cutting above this swelling is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and the groin, and the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is a great fault. It is necessary therefore that its treatment be undertaken with iron, and what of them is also in the extremities, or in the head, it is necessary that it be undertaken.
Above that therefore which of them is from the inflation of the opening of the artery, a cut is made in the skin in length. Then open the cut with forceps, afterwards the artery: and free it from the tunics, until it is exposed.
Then insert beneath it a needle, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the artery with double thread at two places, according to that which I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then prick the place, which is between the two ligaments with a spatula, until all the blood that is in it comes out, and the swelling is relieved.
Then administer a treatment that generates pus, until the ligaments fall away: afterwards treat it with ointments suitable to it, until it is healed.
What if the swelling is on account of a cut of the vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand what you can from the swelling with the skin. Then insert the needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate: and let there be double thread in it, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie the swelling with a good ligature: according to what I said to you concerning the ligature of the vein, from two parts with two extremities of thread.
If however you fear that the thread will be extracted, then insert another needle with another thread under the whole swelling at the point of the incision of the needle: press, and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the aposteme in its middle, until when what is in it comes out: then cut the excess of the skin, and release what was tied from it.
Then put on it a poultice which you have submerged in wine, and oil. Then administer the cure, which is done with plasters and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L
Concerning the aposteme that occurs from a twisting of the nerves.
Just as an aposteme occurs in the artery and vein, similarly an aposteme occurs in the nerves: which occurs from a blow, or occurs from excessive labor, and similar things: and it happens most to those in the places of joints, or heel, and in every place where the joints move: and it is a grave aposteme.
Its color resembles the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain: and when it is constricted with inflammation, one feels in it a weakness similar to numbness.
And it is not an aposteme collected in the depth of the body, but rather it is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not proceed to the front, nor forward, nor backward to the rear.
What therefore is in the joints, ought not to be entered with iron. For perhaps a contraction will occur.
In that which of them is on the head, or on the forehead, cut the skin with a spatula. If therefore it is a small aposteme, then take hold of it with forceps, and cut it off from the root.
And if it is large, then hang it with a hook, and scratch it: then remove it, and close the wound with a suture: and treat it, until it is healed.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the cutting of an aposteme that occurs in the belly.
Pains occur to certain men many times in their bellies and in the rest of their bodies, which are called Fungal infections, because of their similarity with mushrooms.
Of which the root is subtle, and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And there is one of them that is small: and there is one of them that is greatly swollen.
And I have seen a man, and in his belly were two fungal infections, which were more similar to mushrooms than the others: there was no difference between them, they were white, with small roots, and their lips were inverted, and ended, and moisture flowed from them always.
I cut them out, and I found one of them weighed about 8 ounces and the other about 6 ounces. And the operation for cutting them out is that you should consider then, if the patient is weak and the color of the fungal infection is white, moist, with a subtle root: then cut it out with a broad spatula, and have your cauterizing irons ready at hand.
For much blood is often expelled in the cutting of it: therefore if the blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
If you see the patient is weak, pale and timid of the cutting with iron, then take a thread made from lead and tie it around the suture: whose method is what we have described, and leave it for two days.
Then put on the suture a lead dressing. Do not therefore cease to bind with lead, as many times as it impresses on the fungal infection, until it is cut off and falls away by itself without effort.
If the fungal infection is thick of root, then in the cutting of it there is something to be feared and dreaded: especially if it is in the belly.
It is possible to cut through the middle of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it, until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not cut into the black bile which is of a dark color, of little sensation, and foul appearance: for it is a cancerous aposteme: and I call it with remembrance of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of the prominence of the navel.
The prominence of the navel occurs from many causes. Or from a tear of the diaphragm, which is above the belly: and there comes out of it either gut or intestine, according to which there occurs in the remainder a rupture. Or from blood that flows from a vein, or from an artery, according to what has been previously stated. Or from flatulence confined within it.
If however it is on account of a tear of the diaphragm, and an issue of the gut: then the color of the tumor is similar to the color of the body: and it is soft, without pain: and appears in different places. And if it is on account of a protrusion of the intestine, then its location is with that which we have narrated, of more vehement diversity. When you press it with your fingers, it is hidden: then it returns, and perhaps there is a gurgling sound: and it is greatly increased at the entrance of the bath, and vehement labor.
If indeed it is on account of humidity, it is soft, then it is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor is it added to.
For if it is on account of blood, then with these signs the tumor appears as a blackness on the navel.
If however it is on account of flesh that has grown: then the tumor is serious, thick, hard, and fixed according to a single quantity.
If the wound is on account of obesity, then the touch of it is gentle.
And the operation on it is, that you should consider then, if the prominence of the navel is due to blood of the Artery, or Vein, or obesity: then it is necessary that you should abstain from the cure of it. For there is fear and dread in it, as I have taught you in the chapter, in which I said that apostems are due to the artery and vein.
What if the prominence of the navel is due to the intestine, or a cyst: then it is necessary that you should instruct the weak person, so that he may hold his breath, and stand standing upright. Then mark with a cauterizing agent around the entire navel. Afterwards instruct him to breathe again between your hands over his back. Then apply a plaster with a broad spatula around the navel above the place which you marked with the cauterizing agent. Afterwards extend the middle of the apostema upwards with a large hook. Then tie it around the strong part with a thread, or with a cord of silk of firm binding: and let the knot of the binding be secure.
Then open the middle of the extended apostema above the binding. If therefore you find that it is already caught by the binding: then pull out the secure one, and insert the index finger into it, and push the intestine to the interior of the belly: and if you find a cyst, then take it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
If on the other hand the artery or vein is opposite to you: then apply a bandage around it plentifully. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads: and insert the two needles around the area which you made around the apostema in the form of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then tie the aposteme in four places above the needle, and if it is necessary, remove the needle, and leave the place until the flesh putrefies and extends and falls off by itself, or cut it when it becomes subtle and putrefies.
Then treat it with what is necessary from ointments, until it is healed.
What if there is an elevation of the navel from the flesh or from humidity, then it is necessary to concave the aposteme, as we have said. And extract the blood, or humidity, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what solidifies the place.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of cancer, and how it is the way to cure it with medicines: and to be cautious with the treatment of it with iron, so that it does not become ulcerated: and we have spoken of cancer generated in the matrix, and the caution in its treatment.
And the ancients said that whenever cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts, or in the hip, and similar members located for extraction of all of it: especially when it is beginning and small.
When, however, it becomes old and is large, it is not necessary to approach it.
For I was not able to heal anyone from it, nor have I seen another before me who has approached that.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is to proceed and dissolve the weak part with black bile several times. Then perform bloodletting on it if you find it with a clear fullness.
Then prepare the weak part with a preparation, in which it will be located with the operation.
Then throw onto the cancer acidic juices that agree with it. Afterward, cut it away from every part with the skin, according to the examination, until nothing of its roots remains: and let the blood flow, and do not cut it quickly, but express the place, and wipe away all the gross blood with your hand, or with whatever instrument is possible there.
If however the flow of gross blood causes a contradiction to you from the incision of an artery, or a vein: then cauterize the vein, until the blood is stopped. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
On the treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter 44. We have now described in the division of the species of Dropsy, and how the collection of water is made: and the signs of each species, and its cure with medicines.
And that which is treated with iron, is not but the species of ascites alone: and it does not come near to the species of tympanites with iron: nor to the species of hyposarca deeply: for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this species of Dropsy, tympanites, with medicines, and your medication has not been effective: then consider. For if weakness has already befallen the patient, or there is with it another infirmity besides Dropsy: such as if there is with it a cough, or a loosening of the stomach, and things similar to these: then beware lest you treat him with iron: for that is to be feared.
If however you see the weak one of overflowing strength, and there is not with it a disease besides Dropsy alone: and it is neither a child, nor an old man: then the method of operation on it is, that you lift the weak one standing between your hands, and a servant behind him expresses his stomach with his hand pushing water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a spiney splint, second to this shape. It is a sharp instrument of two parts, long, at the acute extremity like a Spatula. It has in it something of shortness gradually, so that you do not pass through with it during the operation to the intestine, and wound it.
Then consider. For if there is a generation of dropsy from the part of the intestines, then it is necessary that you should extend with the incision from the navel the quantity of three fingers, downwards directly above the breast.
If indeed the generation of dropsy is because of sickness of the liver, then let your incision be to the left from the navel three fingers.
And if the generation of it is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side: and it is not necessary also that the incision be on the side above which the weak person wishes to lie, so that the superfluities do not run to that weak place.
Then pierce with an instrument the whole skin. Then introduce into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and scrape, as if you were excavating it: and let the quantity which is excavated be the quantity of a nail, or around it.
Then scrape pierce, until the spatula reaches the empty place: and it is the place of water. Then extract it, and introduce into the opening an instrument, whose form is this. And it is similar to a cannula: made from silver, or copper, or gold, smooth: which has in its lower part a small opening, and on its sides are three openings, two on one side and one on the other side: and the end of it is cut, according to the mode of cutting of a reed, as you see: on its upper end is an armilla.
When the instrument penetrates the water, then it itself descends immediately on top of the instrument, and thus evacuates from the water half the quantity in an hour.
For if you evacuate from it more than is proper, the weak perhaps dies in an hour through the resolution of its vital spirit, or a fainting fit that approaches death befalls him.
Evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the disposition of the weak signifies to you from the virtue of his pulse, and from his goodness.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is, because it is retained immediately on account of the skin which retains the opening that is above the navel: concerning which I have spoken, that you should perforate it according to that method.
Then insert the instrument on the second day, if you see the weak person tolerate it: and withdraw also from the water a small quantity.
Do that for some days without fear, and guard yourself from error: until there does not remain from the water except a little.
If however you fear for the weak person, and you let remain from the water a large amount: then treat it with wrapping in hot sand, and with much sweating in a bath and sun, and waiting upon thirst: and treat it with evacuating medicines, until it is healed: and cauterize on the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the evacuation of the water: according to what I have narrated in what has preceded.
Concerning the treatment of infants who are born with their urine places not perforated.
Chapter 45.
When any infant proceeds from the womb of its mother, and the head of its bladder is not perforated, or the opening is constricted, or it is not in its proper place.
It is necessary therefore to help with the perforation of it at once when it is born with a sharp needle, according to this method.
Then put in the opening a subtle key made of lead, and tie it; and hold it for three days, or four.
When the bladder is raised to emit urine, it should be removed, and micturated. Then reduce it.
And if you do not put in the lead, it is possible that the urine which enters over the place will not allow it to close.
He, indeed, whose opening is narrow, is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is enlarged.
Those, however, in whom the opening is not in its proper place, are injured. Which is, that in those who are born with it, the opening is at the end of the head of the penis: wherefore they cannot urinate to the front, unless they lift the penis with their hand to the top. And there is not for them generation, because the sperm cannot reach the womb according to the right direction: and it is a foul and loathsome disease.
And the method of treatment for this is that the weak one should lie on his back. Then stretch out the head of his penis with your left hand with vigorous extension, and cut the head of the head of the penis from its place at the foreskin with a sharp needle, or a sharp scalpel: just as is the cutting of a reed, or just as you would pare something, so that the middle of it comes to resemble the head of the penis: and so that the opening falls in the middle according to what is fitting.
And beware for yourself during the operation of a flow of blood. For that thing has happened many times. Apply to it therefore something that stops the blood, and cure the wound, until it is healed.
Of Albucasis concerning what happens to the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter 6.
These often occur to that Albather on a branch, and it is a prominence of foul flesh.
And some of them are malignant, and some are not malignant.
You should therefore hang the non-malignant one with a fine hook, and cut it off, until you cleanse it completely. Then put on it a plaster submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with the diaphatic ointment, until it is healed.
Which if the Albather is malignant, of foul color, then you should administer to it a cautery after its incision and shaving.
If the Albather is on the foreskin of one who is not circumcised, and part of the Albather is under the foreskin, and part of it outside: then you should remove the Albather that is inside first, until when it has consolidated, then treat the outside one: for when you treat them both at the same time, the foreskin does not heal unless it is perforated.
And they also occur on the testicles, and a blackening and corruption of the foreskin. You should therefore cauterize all that has already turned black, and what is about to decay, or has already decayed.
Then apply on it after that a honey which has already been mixed with flour of roasted grains and crushed herbs.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
If there occurs a flow of blood, then administer a cautery with lunar cautery, according to this method.
For cautery is useful for two conditions at the same time, namely for a flow of blood, and for an ulcer when it becomes decayed.
Which if the head of the branch is eaten away, and it is destroyed entirely in an hour: then you should insert into the mouth of the penis a tube made of lead, so that the weak man urinates over it.
In the adhesion of the skin of the foreskin to the head of the penis, and this adhesion does not occur except in one whose foreskin is healthy and does not require being cut around it; and when adhesion happens because of a wound or an abscess, it is necessary to cauterize it with a blunt cautery until the ligament is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
What if it is difficult to discern these things exactly, then it is necessary to cauterize something from the head of the penis and from the foreskin. What is, that the foreskin, when it is delicate, is perhaps perforated quickly because of its delicacy.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a fine linen cloth, which has already been soaked in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then let it be treated with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter 57.
On the purification of infants, and the treatment of errors that occur to them by chance.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds: nevertheless since it is from our operation, our will, and its use is properly in children, it is necessary that we indicate in it the better operation, and the lighter way leading to health.
I say therefore, that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, since they do not use it in their laws, and it is not except what we have acquired through experimentation.
What is, that I have found the greater part of artisans and bloodletters using purification by circumcision with a razor, and with scissors: and they use poultices, and ligatures with thread, and incisions with a nail.
And I have already tried all these methods, and I did not find a better purification than with scissors, and ligature with thread.
Since in cleansing with a razor, the skin often slips away. For the skin of the prepuce has two layers: therefore perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower layer remains: hence it is forced to a cutting in order to cut the other, and future pain. And with the cleansing that is done by means of a digital dilator, which surgeons use, the cutting of the extremity of the member is not secured: because perhaps it will enter into its opening.
In cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron will slip and spoil your operation: and indeed the skin of a child is brief by nature, and often such things are born, which do not require cleansing: and I have already seen it.
Cleansing with forceps and ligation with a thread has shown me a good test: because the forceps are proportional to the incision: therefore, since the sharpness that is above is as the sharpness that is below. When I press with my hand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they cut according to one operation at one time: and a compression of similar figure is made, containing the skin of the member from all parts, with which error does not fall at all.
And the method of its operation is that you should cause to extenuate, especially a child, if it is one of those who understand for a little while, what you do not do, except that you tie a thread on its member only: and leave it until another day.
Then make it rejoice and be happy in every way that is possible for you, from the fact that it receives its intelligence. Then make it stand between your prepared hands, and let it not be sitting: and hide the forceps in your sleeve or under your foot, so that the child does not see them at all, nor any of the instruments.
Then insert your hand into her vagina, and blow into the wound, and pull it upwards, until the head of the vagina comes out. Then cleanse it from the filth that has accumulated in its dirtiness.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards tie below it for a short space a second ligature. Then hold with your index finger, and thumb the place of the lower ligature with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the wound upwards quickly, and extract the head of the vagina. Then let the blood flow out gradually (for it is better) and put powder on top: and over the powder a cloth in which the yolk of an egg has been spread, mixed with oil of fresh rose, and let it stay on until the next day: and treat it with the remainder of the treatment, until it is healed.
The shape of a fork is for this cleansing.
Let the two extremities be blunt, and let them be cutting: so that a branch of a nail does not come out in them: and let the length of the two sharp edges be equal to the length of the two armlets.
The error falling in the cleansing is: that perhaps the whole inner skin escapes, or a part of it at the place of the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you stretch it out with your nail before the postulation of the place: and cut it according to equality.
What if you cannot hold it with your nail, then extract it with a hook, and cut it.
If however three days have passed and what is under the vagina remains inflated, a postulate: then let it be, until a warm postulate settles, and remove it with ease, and cut it according to what is prepared for you: and beware of the head of the vagina. For it will not harm him.
Treatment is thus with what consolidates the wound from powders, which we described in the treatment of powders: and if it cuts into the skin above the measure, and proceeds to the upper parts, it will not harm him either.
Treatment is thus with that which we mentioned, until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
On the extraction of retained urine from the bladder.
The retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction made from stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh that has grown, and similar things to these which we mentioned as methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine does not flow, and you see that its retention is on account of a stone, which is already in the neck of the bladder: then it is necessary that he should be made to sit bending over his knees. Then his feet should be placed on his back, and he should stretch out his belly from the upper part of his back, and the man should constrict his breath as much as possible for him. It is thus pressed with the neck of the bladder to expel the stone backwards, wherefore the urine will flow.
What if it is not solved with what we mentioned, and the thing is severe for the man: then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: It should be of silver, and should be subtle, thin, concave like the quill of a feather in subtlety of a radius, long in likeness of one and a half palms: to which there should be a thin tube at its head.
And the method of exit of urine through it is, that you take a double thread, and tie at its end a wool or a string, with a good knot: and introduce the end of the thread into the lower part of the Syringe: and cut with the forceps if something overflows from the wool, truly introduce into the tube with vehemence, like alzer.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg: and make the weak sit upon a seat, and embrocate the vessel, and its own virge with moist oils, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert the syringe into the virge with ease, until it reaches to the root of the virge.
Then fold it over double from the upper part near the navel.
Then push the syringe into it, until it reaches to the bladder, and let the weak feel that it has already reached to the empty space: and this is not done according to this order, unless the passage, in which it goes, is where there is a tortuosity.
Then extend the thread with a woolen yarn little by little. For the vein will follow the wool.
Then extract it, and make the vein run through the way of urine: and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do that, until the bladder is evacuated, and the weak feels relief.
On the quality of evacuating the bladder with a Clyster.
Chapter 59.
When there happens to be an ulcer in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus is confined in it, and one wishes to distill waters and medicines into it: then let it be done with an instrument which is called a Clyster, and this is its form.
For it is closed, that is, shut. And in it are three openings, two from one side and one from the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which pushes, should be according to the quantity that fills it without addition, until when something is pulled with it, it attracts from humidities: and when it is pushed, it expels far off: according to which the clyster does, which projects naphtha, that is, Greek fire in the mart's bellows.
When I insert the instrument to project moistures into the bladder, I introduce the extremity of the catheter into the moisture, and pull with it what pushes upwards, for moisture is pulled into the concavity of the catheter.
Then I introduce its extremity into the vein, according to what we have narrated concerning the syringe. Then I push moisture with that which pushes it, for moisture immediately penetrates to the bladder, so that the weak person feels it.
The shape of a thin enema instrument by which the bladder is enemad.
Let it be made from silver or gold: whose head is higher, similar to a small spoon, and under that a sinus in which the ligament falls.
Then take the bladder of a goat, and put in it moisture, with which you will enema the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature and double thread: and heat that moisture a little over the fire.
Then introduce the extremity of the enema instrument into the vein, afterwards press your hand over the bladder with moisture with a suitable pressure, until the weak person feels that the moisture has already penetrated to the bladder.
If however the bladder is not present for you, then take a piece of parchment, and make from it a circle, and perforate it throughout its entire roundness. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes, as they are turned. Then gather the circle, as the sponge is gathered: after which you put in it that which you use from moistures, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it in the instrument: and do with it as you did with the bladder from expression with your hand, until moisture penetrates to the bladder.
Chapter 60
On the extraction of a stone.
We have spoken in the division of the species of Stone, and its cure with medicines: and I have said the difference between the Stone generated in the kidneys, and bladder, and the signs of all that: and I have said the Stone in which the operation falls with the hand and cutting: and it is a Stone generated in the bladder properly: and that which is fixed in the rod: and I am narrating that with clear exposition and abbreviation.
I say therefore, that the Stone generated in the bladder, happens most to boys.
And of its signs is that urine comes out from the bladder similar to water in its thinness: and appears in it white sand, and the weak man rubs his rod, and labors with it, and many times it hangs. Then it is erected, and comes out with that the bottom in many of them.
And the healing is easy for boys, as long as they are up to fourteen years old. And it is difficult in the elderly. In youths, however, it is moderate, in that which is between the two: and of him whose Stone is larger, the cure is easier: and if small, the care is the opposite.
When therefore we arrive at the cure, it is necessary first to clean the weak man with a clyster, which will extract all the dung that is in his intestines: for this sometimes prevents the discovery of the Stone during the examination.
Then let the weak man be taken by the feet, and shaken, and moved downwards: so that the Stone may descend to the bottom of the bladder, or jump from a high place many times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and his hands are under his hips, so that the bladder is entirely inclined downwards.
Then examine him, and touch him externally.
If I feel a stone in the space, then immediately with a cut above it.
What if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe the index finger of the left hand with oil, if he is a weak child: or the middle finger if he is a young adult: and insert it into his anus, and feel for the stone, until it stands under your finger: and turn it little by little to the neck of the bladder.
Then press above it with your finger, and push it to the outside opposite the place of which you wish to make the incision: and instruct one attendant to press the bladder with his hand, and instruct another attendant to stretch the testicles with his right hand upwards: and with his other hand, to remove the skin which is under the testicles on the part away from the place in which the incision is.
Then insert the spatula of the incision, whose shape is this. And find in that which is between the anus and testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock: and let the incision be above the stone, and your finger be in the anus: and let the incision be transverse, so that it be externally ample and internally strict, according to the quantity that it is possible for the exit of the stone from it not being larger.
For perhaps the finger which is in the anus compresses the stone at the incision, and it goes out without difficulty.
And know that the stone which has angles and edges makes its exit difficult because of that.
And it is smooth, similar to a gland, and round: and its exit is easy.
Therefore for those which have angles and edges, add to the incision.
What if it does not go out thus, then it is necessary that you ingeniate above it: or bind above it with a decent instrument, whose extremity is like the Moon, which binds above the stone and does not slip from it: or that you insert under it an subtle instrument of curved extremity.
If you cannot sew above it, then enlarge the opening a little. What if something of blood conquers you, cut it off with a ligature.
If there is more than one stone in the bladder, then first push the large one to the mouth of the bladder. Then cut above it. Afterwards push a small one after that, and do likewise if there are more than two.
What if the valve is very large, then it is ignorance to cut above it with a large incision. Because it happens to the weak one of two things, either that he dies, or that there occurs to him a continuous dripping of urine: therefore, because the place is not consolidated at all.
Direct the expulsion of it, until it comes out: or instruments on the fracture with forceps, until you extract it bit by bit.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with resin, and aloe, and dragon's blood: and bind it, and put over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that the hot aposteme may be assuaged.
Then let the weak one rest on his neck, and do not loosen the bandage, until the third day. When you then loosen it, bathe the place with water, and much oil.
Then treat it with the ointment called diaphenicum, and the ointment called basilicum, until it is healed.
If however there occurs in the wound a hot and corroding aposteme, and similar things: as if blood coagulates in the bladder, and urine is prohibited from it: and this is recognized by the discharge of blood with urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, and water, and salt: and apply all the species with what is suitable from the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the hours of treatment of the whole, that you bind two hips, and gather, the causes of the medicines which are placed on the spot.
What if the stone is small, and it is in the flesh of the rod, and it is fixed in it, and the exit of the urine is prohibited: then treat it with what I will narrate before you proceed to the cutting. For many times indeed you will be excused from cutting by this treatment, for I have already tested that.
And there is, that you receive the perforator made of iron, whose form this is, a triangular extremity, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread, and bind the rod with it under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the iron perforator into the rod with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand in the stone little by little: and complete the perforation of it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For the urine will be freed at once.
Then press your hand on that which remains of the stone from the external part of the rod. For it itself breaks and comes out with the urine, and the weak man is healed.
If however this treatment is not prepared for you, on account of something which prohibits it: then bind a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the rod itself between the two bindings. Then extract it. Afterwards loosen the bandage and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And it is not necessary to tie the thread under the stone, except that it does not return to the bladder; and another bandage above, except that when the thread is loosened after the removal of the stone, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound: and for that reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the upper thread, to elevate the skin upwards, so that it returns to its complement and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
A small stone is generated in women.
If however it happens that a stone forms in any of them, then the treatment is difficult and is forbidden because of many methods.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, that you do not find a woman who reveals herself to the physician, when she is chaste, or among those having husbands.
The third is, that you do not find a woman well skilled in this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the remoteness of the place of the stone: where it requires a deep incision, and there is fear in that.
What if necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary that you receive a woman physician, well skilled: and she is rarely found.
If indeed you are deprived of them, then seek a chaste physician, subtle, and present a woman midwife well taught in the matter of women, or a woman who has acquired a part of the art: make her therefore present, and especially instruct her, that she do all that you prescribe to her from the inquiry concerning the stone.
At first what is, to examine if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If then it finds it, it constricts it under its finger, and then orders the midwife to insert her finger into the weak womb, and feels above the stone, after which she places her left hand over the bladder, and presses it with a good compression.
If then it finds the stone, then it is necessary that she moves it gradually from the opening of the bladder to the lower areas with her greatest strength, until she reaches with it to the root of the hip.
Then she cuts above it at the opposite of the middle of the womb at the root of the hip, from whichever side it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone on that side: and her finger does not move away from the stone, constricting under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then she inserts the rasp over that incision.
Then she inserts the rasp over that incision. If then she feels the stone, then she adds to the incision a second cut proportional in quantity to what the stone protrudes from it.
And know that there are many kinds of stones. For from these is the small and the large, the smooth and the rough, the long, and the round with branches.
Know therefore its kinds, so that it may be indicated to you through this above what you use.
If then blood overcomes you, then add to the place of the wound some pounded tragacanth, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is stopped.
Then return to your operation, until the stone comes out: and make that you prepare with you from the instruments, which I spoke of in the extraction of the stone from the bladder, so that you may help yourself by them in your operation.
For if the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery which has been cut: then put powder on the place, and constrict it with decent strictures: and do not touch it: and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For a weak woman will surely die. Then treat the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood has sat after some days and the place has become putrid, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
On the cutting of a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not a rupture, but an aggregation of humidity in the white membrane, which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg: and it is named a membrane.
And sometimes it is in its own proper membrane, which nature has prepared for it in the part of the egg, until it becomes putrid: which is another egg, and is the skin of the testicle between the white membrane, which we mentioned: and that does not happen except rarely.
And the generation of this hernia is from weakness which happens to the testicles: for which reason this material flows to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow on the testicles.
And this humidity has many colors. For it is either a color leaning to yellow, or it is a bloody red, or a filthy black, or it is a watery white, and that which is most.
