Then when you penetrate with the needle these four ends, draw back the needle from the part where you began, so that the thread tied on the upper part of the wound falls: so that it is a suture, like the suture of cloth which clothes are sewn with, and put between every suture the quantity of the thickness of a small digit.
These men know this suture.
And with this suture I have sewn a wound that occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and there was a large disruption of the wound the size of one palm, and about two palms worth of intestines had come out of the middle intestine, and there was a disruption in the middle of the abdomen.
I put it back after his intestine had stayed outside the wound for 24 hours.
The wound was then consolidated in a period of about 15 days: and I treated it, and it healed: and he lived many years after that, acting in his own affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not heal.
And it was a miracle that I did not treat it with an ointment. For I was in a place where no ointment was to be found, nor any of the medicines. I put therefore an old cloth over the wound, twice a day, and I persisted in washing it with honey water until it was healed.
I will describe two proper kinds of suture according to the account of my discourse.
And it is that you begin with a sharp suture, and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When the needle thus penetrates the skin, and into the flesh that is cut according to the length and width of the whole body: leave the thread end on the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate from the inner part to the outer in the other thread end at the other side of the wound.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate thus in the same thread end of the wound from the outer part to the inner: and leave the thread end that is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other thread end from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration that you make it penetrate in the thread end of the wound, which is on its part, until it penetrates into it entirely.
Then take hold of it also from this same side, and pull it out with the extremity that is from the sifac on the other side, and extract the needle from the skin which it has penetrated.
Then reduce it to that skin, and pull out the extremity of the sifac that is on the other side with this extremity of the mirach: and extract it from the skin which is on its own part, and do one side and the other: and do that side after the other side, until you have extracted all of it according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you should sew up the pellets which sew up the skins: just as you should put the sifac on one side with one extremity of the skin: and that you should let it go on the other side with another, until you have finished.
The second type of its own suture, which Galenus called.
This speech of his has been related thus, that they should sew it up according to the likeness, which some physicians sew them up, so that they join every part to its proper part by nature: and apply the extremity of the sifac to its other extremity, and the extremity of the mirach to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I relate to you.
It is necessary that you should fasten the needle in the extremity of the mirach nearest to you from the outer part, and make it penetrate to the interior in that alone: and let go two extremities of the sifac. Then turn the needle around, and make it penetrate from the outer part to the interior in the two extremities of the sifac, namely on both sides.
Then turn both around, and make both penetrate from the interior to the exterior, into the two other extremities of the mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common suture and it is a suture whose success precedes memory.
This is therefore the treatment of Galen also.
And some have said. The suture of the belly is tightened with two fingers. That is, so that you insert into them both one thread: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from what is at your hands, and make it penetrate up to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew leathers equally.
And know that when a disruption is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than that of the other places of the belly.
The judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
That is, because when you treat a fresh wound with its blood, before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and sew it, and adapt it: then put on top an incarnative powder.
That if the air has already altered the wound: then put on it certain ointments, which are present to you, until they make pus: and the threads fall, and consolidate the tissue, and form a scar. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it heals.
If however the medicines are not present to you, then put on it immediately, from which it begins to emit pus, old coturn: and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.
For you do not use other medications in many treatments, when the wounds are simple.
If however you fear that the wound communicates with the principal members in a lesion, then it is necessary that you soak woolen threads in tempered oil of moderate warmth, or in rose oil: and put them on the circumference of the places which are between the groin, and under the armpits.
What if he feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction in his intestines (for that often happens) then a clyster with styptic wine, black, and warm: especially if the putrefaction has already entered the intestine, and a penetrating fistula has been made up to its concavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine, is of easier healing: and what is from them is from the subtle, of more difficult healing.
The intestine which is called Jejunum, does not receive healing from a wound that happens in it at all: and this is because of its multitude, which is from the veins, and their greatness, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If what proceeds from the wound is a flow, and you treat it freshly: reduce it according to your reduction, whereby you reduce the intestines equally.
If indeed it has passed the space, and already has become green or black: then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place which has been discolored from it, so that there does not happen a flow of blood. For in the flow are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and put two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside of it: so that the attraction of it may be alleviated over you, and the extraction of it at the time of flow, and emission of pus of the wound.
A remembering of the wound which happens in the intestine.
When a disruption happens in the intestine and is small: then it is possible that it be restored in certain men: because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with the thrust of a spear, and the wound was on the right stomach, and the wound became old, and a fistula was made, and there came out from it an egestion and flatulence.
I applied a treatment, until what I was not confident of in its healing: nor did I cease to refine it, until it was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the weak spot, that a weak accident would happen on the weak spot, an accident in its belly: and no accident happened to it from that accident. And it remained in its better dispositions, eating and drinking, and farting, and it entered the bath, and was exercised in its diets.
And now certain testers have said that when an injury happens in the intestine and it is small: then it should be bound in this manner. And it is, that you take poultices of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and put one poultice of those which have an open mouth over the two lips of the wound. When it seizes upon them, and closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres, and is not loosened.
Then put another poultice near the first, and do not cease to do that with a poultice after a poultice according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it, and heal its wound. For those heads remain attached in the intestine, until the intestine is digested and healed, and no harm happens to the weak person at all.
And now it is possible, that the intestine be bound with a thin thread: which is taken from the intestine of an animal, and attached to it after it is inserted on a needle.
And it is, that you take the end of this thread from the intestine, and much flesh is scraped away. Then tie on its end a thread of linen wrapped around. Then insert that thread into the needle, and sew with it the intestine. Then reduce it in a fracture.
And this method of suturing with ants and intestine is not unless according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is great, large, especially if it is in one of the slender intestines: then there is no ingenuity except subtlety, and its healing is not at all.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids and fistulas.
Know that every wound, or abscess when it becomes old and aged, and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and always discharges pus, and does not separate: then it is called universally in whichever member it may be a Fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a Fistula indeed is according to truth a hard, white, calloused nodulation: with which there is no pain, to which is attached the feather of a bird: and on account of that some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And it is in certain hours humid, which discharges pus. And perhaps the humidity is discharged at certain hours. And sometimes this humidity is much, and it is little: and it is thick, and it is subtle.
And I have already mentioned in the division, then when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor is born in it flesh, is not that except on account of one of nine causes.
One of which is, or on account of the smallness of blood in the body: or on account of its malice: or because between it and above its lips there is hard flesh, preventing the birth of flesh: or because it is of much filth and foulness: or because the ulcer in itself is putrid, and the matter which is applied to it is of bad quality: or because the medicine is inconvenient for its cure: or because the corruption falling in the region is a kind of pestilence, or by the property of the region, as it happens in Caesaraugusta: in which difficult digestion of ailments occurs, and the healing of abscesses is delayed in it by nature: or because in it there is one mouth, or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division; and it is necessary here that I make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore, that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For some of them are Fistulas that pass into the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spleen, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the composite joints of a composition: such as the hand, and foot: and some of them are which reach to the proximity of the principal member, and similar things.
Which therefore of these Fistulas is according to this method, is from diseases of difficult healing, and does not receive cure, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
That if it is one of the Fistulas whose depth does not reach to these places which I mentioned: and you hope, and trust in its cure, and you use to reach the knowledge of it, then take a probe made of bronze, or iron, if the Fistula tends according to straightness, and investigate it with it.
If however there is tortuousness in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe of refined lead. For lead, with gentleness of its body, is lifted and turned around the tortuousness.
That if the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to examine it with a probe. Therefore close one of its openings from its openings.
For the moisture that you close it with, goes back to the other openings, and flows out from them.
Then carefully inquire according to which method it is possible for you: to know what that place is, or a nerve, and whether it is a Fistula with a long or short concavity: or if it is one Fistula which has many openings, and stands on the whole of that, with the greatest fortitude of yours: with your interrogation, which you interrogate the weak, and the invention of pain upon compression with your hand over the place, and similar signs and causes of accidents found.
When you stand over all those stations of that place, then proceed to cure according to confidence: and it is, as you consider then, if there is a fistula appearing near or in a healthy place far from the joint, or from a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places I told you about: then cut the Fistula according to what was previously mentioned from my narration, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupt flesh, which grows in its opening from flesh, or pus, and similar things: and cure it, until it is healed.
If the fistula is of deep and distant length, and it is according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it as deeply as is possible for you. Then clean it out from all its corrupted flesh.
Then administer the unguents in acute medicines: and introduce them up to the concavity of the fistula which you grasped with the iron: do that several times, until the acute medicine corrodes all that remained in the concavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If it does not heal by that method, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
When the cause of the fistula is a hole, and it is confirmed to you: then investigate it, and cut it according to what I have narrated to you: if nothing prohibits you from the vein, or nerve, or principal member, as we have said.
When a hole is detected by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then scrape it, until all its corruption is removed. Then cure it with what will make flesh on it, until it is healed.
What if it does not heal, and emits pus as it was doing: then know that you did not reach the complete eradication of the corruption.
Remove that thing from above it in the second place and make a cut of it and cleansing of it with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If then it is healed, it is good: and if not, then you should know that the corruption is in the depth of the remote body part, which you have not reached. There is therefore no ingenuity for you in it, except to entrust it to nature.
What if the small mouth is corrupted and it is possible for you, draw it with subtle forceps that fit onto it.
If however there are many bones, then make a careful pulling of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If indeed something among the moistures causes opposition to you, and there is no fear of incision or error: then cut those moistures and flesh, if they are there, and free the bones.
If indeed you do not have preparation to remove the bones from your hour, then put on the wound what will rot it: and leave it, until it rots what is around those bones of flesh and moistures.
And guard the wound that it does not consolidate. And let the section which you cut in your operation be bound with a ligature. Below put on it, if you wish, a plaster submerged in sulphur beaten with oil: or a plaster submerged in Egyptian ointment, or some of the green ointments.
For when those moistures rot, and the bones are separated from them, the removal of them will be easy, and their attraction.
If however there is one large bone, such as the bone of the thigh or the hip bone, and similar to these, and what of it is corrupted is a large part: then scrape it with a final scraping, until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
If the corrupted part which is in it is a large portion, and the corruption has already affected the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it entirely up to the place where the corruption penetrates: then treat it, until it becomes consolidated.
And I will tell you a story about a man who had an injury on his thigh: so that you may consider it as an example and aid for your operation.
There was a man of youthful age, about thirty years old, to whom pain occurred in his thigh, from a cause which moved upon it within the body, that is, from the interior, until the matter reached the thigh and became an abscess, a large abscess: and there was no external cause for him.
It was extended therefore with him the time when there was an error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and many matters ran together with it. And his treatment was put off, until his thigh became fistulous, and many openings were made in it: which all emitted pus, and the moistures of the body.
A multitude of physicians therefore treated him for about two years, and there was not in it a wise hand in the art, until I was called, and I saw his thigh, and the matters which ran out of those openings with a great flow: also the whole body of the man had already wasted away, and its color was yellow.
I then inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached to the bone.
Then I examined all the openings, and I found that they connected to each other from all parts of the thigh.
I cut therefore and I separated above one of those openings, until I exposed a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted, which had already been gnawed away, and was blackened, and rotten, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I will restore what has been lost to me, and prepared from a corrupted bone.
And I have cut away what was not on the bone except for that corruption which I excised and sawed. And I will now eradicate it.
Then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it has not been consolidated.
Then I probed and examined the bone a second time above the first detection, and I found continuous corruption with the bone, and I sawed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
I examined therefore the bone again, and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut around one palm from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and this repetition in the operation and cutting of it would not have been necessary, except because of the disposition of weakness and infirmity, and scarcity of tolerance of it, and my fear concerning it of death: since there occurred to him in every hour from an excess of evacuation of bad syncope.
He was healed therefore with complete healing: and hard flesh was born in the place of the bone, and a good arrangement was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he turned to his habits: and walking did not cause him any harm at all.
If the prominent bone in the body is already broken, then it is necessary to saw it in this manner: and it is done by taking a bandage, and tying it at the extremity of the prominent bone: and instruct someone, who should pull it upwards: and put another bandage made of coarse wool on top of the first bandage. Then tie it above the flesh that is under the bone and extend the extremity so you pull the flesh to the lower parts and you will dissect the flesh above the place you want to saw, so the saw does not wound the flesh.
Put a stick or a plate under the lower bone decently: for when you do that, it is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be above the corrupted place for a little while, so the corruption is not in the cavity of the bone, and it does not appear in its appearance to the sense, and forces itself to be sawn instead of another.
For if the bone is corrupted and it is not prominent, namely the part of it continues with a part, and the corruption is in the middle of it or in a part of it, then dissect the flesh from all parts entirely. Then put a stick below.
Afterwards saw it from the first side, where the corruption is, until the sawing continues from the other side: and let the sawing be according to the length a little away from the corruption, as we have said.
For if the corruption is in the joint, cut out the corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it continues from the other side.
If indeed the corruption is in the connection of two joints, then there is no ingenuity in it except scraping.
For if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or in the sole of the foot, then the matter in it is very difficult. It is necessary that you investigate the corruption as it appears to you. And scrape it and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever ingenuity is right for you: when the vein or nerve does not make a contradiction for you.
And you should know that the incisions and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and fineness, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Wherefore it is necessary that you prepare every kind of instrumental apparatus suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations signify to you the type of instrument which you need when you have a lengthy study with it. And knowledge of the modes of this art is testimony of the modes of ailments then indeed you will find by yourself that which is suitable for it from instruments for every weakness.
And I affirm to you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will give as examples upon which you will stumble and a trial with which you will test other things.
The shape of another saw.
The shape of a razor for joints. Let the head of this razor be according to the shape of the head of a star-shaped key and its points be according to the shape of the points of an awl.
And it does not fit unless the joints are rubbed with it when they become corroded or the mouth is wide and large.
The shape of a larger razor.
The shape of a broad razor.
The shape of a razor in which there is a concavity.
The shape of another delicate razor.
The shape of a razor with a curved end.
The shape of a small razor, delicate handle.
This is similar to a file, and its outer edge is like a saw: it is exhibited in many places for the scraping of bones.
The form of a rasp which is suited for the scraping of that which is perforated by bones.
Its outer edge is triangular, and it is sharp at the edges.
It is made from Indian steel. And it is similarly necessary that all the things we have spoken of should be made from rasps, and incisors, and saws.
On the incision of edges, and the sawing of bones.
The extremities rot, either from an external cause or an intrinsic one.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with medical cures, and the treatment does not work: and you see the corruption spreading to the member, which nothing can penetrate: then it is necessary to cut into that member until where the corruption has reached, so that he may be saved from death, or from the evil which is a greater defect of the member.
And the sign to which it happens is that the member blackens, until it becomes putrid: as if fire had burned it, or it had rotted after blackening: until the putrefaction is cut into, up to that which follows that member, and spreads through the whole body.
It is proper therefore with the incision of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous things: such as the sea scorpion, and viper, and rue, and similar things.
If therefore the corruption or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then cut into the finger, and do not give space to the corruption, lest it spread up to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut into it from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption, until it takes hold in the forearm.
And if it invades into it, then cut the arm at the elbow at its joint.
If however corruption passes through and you see it around the elbow then in that is death of the weak. And to administer beyond that from the cure, is a quantity of strength.
And do similarly for the foot, when corruption invades into the toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it reaches to the body of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
What if corruption has already gone beyond the knee, then there is not in it ingenuity, except abandonment, and to leave the weak to death.
And the method of cutting a limb, or sawing it, is that you tie a ligature below, which you want to cut: and tie another ligature above the place. And let one assistant stretch a lower ligature downwards: and let another assistant stretch an upper ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures, with a broad spatula, until the whole flesh is detached. Then let it be cut or sawn.
And it is fitting, that you put a linen cloth on all parts, and the saw does not touch the healthy place: and there happens to the weak a lesion added, and a hot abscess.
If however there happens an internal flow of blood while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or put on it some powders that staunch blood. Then return to your medication, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a bandage suitable for it, and treat it, until it is healed.
And I will tell you what happened to a man with his foot, this same thing that I am telling you.
What is, that a blackening occurs in his foot, with a burning which resembles that of fire; and this corruption first occurs in his toe, until it begins to affect the entire foot.
It purified a man by itself, after it saw the corruption befalling the member with its swelling, which it found from the pain and burning of it: and it befall the joint, and it was healed.
And after a long time had passed for him, the same corruption occurred to him in the index finger of his hand.
He came therefore to me, and I attempted to recover that excess with what I placed on the hand from medicines after the purification of his body: and the excess did not push back, and it began to enter into another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
He called therefore for me to cut into his hand: and I was unwilling to do it, hoping from me for the pushing back of that excess; and I feared also for him regarding death at the cutting of his hand. For the man's strength was on the decline.
After he therefore had given up on me, he returned to his own land: and he came to me about it, because he had hastened, and he cut his entire hand: and he was healed.
And I have not added this narration, except as an aid regarding that which falls under the type of this illness: and as a signification which is signified, and according to which it is acted.
On the treatment of abscesses, and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it makes a collection of pus: then it bursts or is perforated, and there exits what is in it of pus, and the empty place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it like cloth which has already been worn thin: and it does not arrive at the end in its corruption, nor does it press into the bone, nor into the nerve, nor into the ligament: it deserves to be named Sinus: and it is not named Fistula: nevertheless if its space is prolonged until the corruption presses into one of these members, then it is named Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is that you cut the entire wound as it was previously, and especially when it has been subtle and made like a cloth, and you are confident that it will not adhere to the place because of its own corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and there is no corruption from that last consequence, and if there is a thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster, and it is so that you consider regarding the Anus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it is of foul odor: then give a clyster to it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, mixed together in a vessel: and decocted over fire, until the medicine reddens: and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it what you need, and dissolve it with water and honey, and give a clyster to the Anus with it: and constrict the orifice of it, and let the medicine stay in it for the quantity of two hours.
Then extract it with expression: do this for as many days as until the Anus is cleansed, and the foul smell is removed.
And sometimes give a clyster with water of ashes, when this medicine is not present to you: and it is that you take the ash of vine wood, or the ash of holm oak wood: and throw water upon it. Then strain it, and give a clyster with it, until you are confident that the Anus is now cleaned.
What if the weak one is not able to tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor the water of ashes, then give a clyster with water and honey, in which something has already been mixed from crushed ziniar. Or give a clyster with honey and wine mixed, since it is a property of honey to cleanse and wash: and the wine makes the Anus adhere, especially when there is an excess of dryness and astringency in the wine.
When you do that several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus has already been removed: then insert a suppository with what makes flesh grow in it.
And it is, that you take from the plaster dyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil and styptic wine, or insert a suppository with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I wrote about in the treatise on ointments.
What if the orifice of the sinus is narrowed, in which the cannula of the clyster does not fit: then widen it with iron for a short time: or put in it a small amount of lichen wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in royal ointment, until it is widened.
And similarly if the orifice is also too wide, then bring its lips together with a suture: and reduce from it the quantity in which the clyster can enter without addition.
And similarly if the orifice from which the pus runs is elevated upwards: then make an incision in the lower place that is in it, so that the pus runs out from it downwards: for pus when it is confined in the depth of the sinus, prevents flesh from being generated in it.
What if it is not possible for you to make an incision in the sinus around its lower part according to what you want: then try, that you prepare a membrane with a preparation so that the pus runs out from it with ease according to what is prepared for you. Try, that nothing of the pus is retained in it at all: and take consolidative medicines which are applied to the wound, suitable for what you want. And spread them on a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it makes contact with the entire sinus. Then cut with forceps directly at the orifice of the sinus a hole, slightly larger than the orifice of the sinus. Then spread a lenient unguent over another cloth according to the quantity of the wound and put it on the wound in a long shape from the orifice of the sinus, so that the pus can flow out: and do not remove the large cloth unless after some days once.
That one, however, which is over the orifice of the Sinus, is that which you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it from pus can flow out: and so that you know, whether what flows out from it is a lot, or a little: or digested, or undigested.
And with this again consider the location of the Sinus itself, whether the master of it feels pain in it, or if there is not pain in it, and whether there is an abscess in it, or if there is not an abscess in it.
When therefore your medication is thus extended, and you see the matter that flows out over the orifice of the Sinus, then know that the Sinus already adheres, or approximates adherence: then therefore dissolve it on the next morning, or on the third day: and when you dress the wound, then change the cloth to another, and renew the unguent.
If however a subtle pus flows out from the Sinus after a long interval, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, since there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
If however undigested pus flows out from the Sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adherence of the Sinus is delayed, and its matter is prolonged: then add to the drying of medicines, true ones made by doctors, so that they are humid in their substance and dry in their virtue: such as the unguentum dyafinicon, when a surplus addition from zegi is added to it.
And from the most extreme of what is cured, it is that you take the ointment called diyafinicon, and dissolve it with rose oil, and spread over it an equal old wine in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And now do similar to this action, when the ointment diyafinicon is not present to you, honey, when you have cooked it, until it becomes thick, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind the whole, and pulverize it over the honey while it is over the fire. Then smear over it a cloth, and tie it over the wound: and smear with the honey that you have cooked, until it has thickened: and sprinkle the powder over it, and put over it a cloth, and tie it.
And when administered to it Aristolochia longa, and the roots of heavenly lily, and it is a lily: and flour of the herb, and centaury. Use them simple, or combined, grind them, and sieve them: and sprinkle over the honey that I have mentioned, and use it.
What if the matter of the wound is prolonged, and it does not heal with this cure: then know that there is corruption in its depth: or now it makes an impression on the bones, or on the remaining hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the cure for fistulas, according to what has been previously stated.
Of the treatment of a wounded or worn away nail, and the cutting of a toe, and the division.
A panaritium is a tumor that grows under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot: and perhaps it grows on the other toes.
When indeed it has prolonged its matter, and the treatment of it is neglected, and it suppurates with a hot tumor: or it becomes rotten, and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it rots the whole thing, and perhaps the corruption reaches to the bone, until it is foul smelling: and it makes the end of the toe thick, and its color becomes dark.
When you raise your treatment with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not work: then it is necessary to cut with iron all the excess that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cauterization is a strong helper.
If the bone is indeed healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the additions of vacant nail now press the flesh inward, and begin to pierce it, and wound it: then it is necessary that you put a subtle pumice under the angle of the nail which pierces the flesh, and push it upward: and cut that flesh with ease, and put on top of what remains of the flesh from medications burning, corrosive, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
If the corruption has already made an impression on the bone, then it is necessary to cut that bone and extract it: for it will not be healed as long as the corrupted bone remains.
What if you see corrosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to which a reminder of its former state has been preserved. Then treat it, until it is healed.
If however nail attrition or a blow occurs, and there is severe pain in it, then it is necessary to perform bloodletting on the weak one first. Then cut the nail with a sharp razor, a cross-section from above to the root.
And be careful, lest the cut penetrate to the flesh that is under the nail, for you will cause severe pain by it on the weak one, and it will be a cause of inflammation of the added flesh in the place.
Then treat the place with what calms pains.
The finger that is added to the ones that grow on certain hands of men is perhaps entirely fleshy and perhaps there are bones in some of them and perhaps there is a nail in it and some of them grow at the root of the joint or its growth is in certain nodes of the finger. That one which grows on certain nodes of the finger does not move and that one which grows at the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
The cutting of that one which is fleshy among them is easy. Which is to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
The treatment of that one whose growth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore refrain from cutting it.
In that one which grows on the finger near the last node, then it is necessary to cut first its flesh with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws that are suitable for it. Then treat it until it is healed.
The joining together of fingers which occurs, often happens either from the birth of the man, that is, it grows with him, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from burns of fire, and similar things.
It is necessary therefore to separate that joining, until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then put between them ligatures or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not join quickly, and separate them, or put between them a subtle sheet of lead, until the place is consolidated according to what is necessary.
And similarly if a joining occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then separate that joining according to what prepares and suits the shape of the member.
Of the cutting of defects.
There are varices, which are swollen veins, full of melancholic superfluities that occur in many parts of the body: and they most often happen in the thighs, especially in the thighs of travelers and laborers.
It is necessary therefore that you administer a purging of the body from black bile by strong purging many times.
Then perform bloodletting on the patient with a basilica vein.
Treatment of them indeed is with iron, and it is in two methods. The treatment of them is that they should be cut, and black blood is extracted. And the second method is that the vein is evacuated, and everything is extracted.
The cutting of it indeed is according to this method. First immerse the thigh in warm water many times, until the thick bloody filth is dissolved. Then bind the weak thigh from the upper part of its hip to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then evacuate, that is, extract the blood with your hand from the lower part of the thigh upwards, and it is necessary that you perform bloodletting on the weak one first. Then cut the vein until below, until there comes out from the blood a quantity that you see is sufficient, and which the strength of the weak one can tolerate.
Afterwards bind it: and order him to abstain from foods that generate black bile: and prohibit evacuation and bloodletting, when the veins are full, and it harms the weak one.
The evacuation of it is according to this method. Scrape the weak thigh, if there are many hairs on it. Then insert it into a bath, or immerse it in its wound with hot water, until it becomes red and the veins become swollen, or let it be exercised with strong exercise, if a bath is not available to it, until the member becomes warm.
Then cut the skin in the direction of the vein's section along its length, or at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and strip the vein from every side, until it becomes apparent to sensation: and at its appearance, that which you see it turning from redness to blackness declining. When therefore it comes out from the skin, you see it as white as if it were a tendon.
Then insert beneath it a metal rod, until when it is raised and comes out from the skin: suspend it with a blunt hook, gently.
Then cut another section near that section, three fingers apart. Then strip the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it becomes apparent. Then raise it with rods as you have done, and suspend it with another hook, as you have done at first.
Then cut another section, or several sections, if you need to. Then raise and cut it at the last section near the heel.
Then pull it out to the section which is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the section which is above, or higher than all the sections, until when it comes out entirely: then cut it.
If it does not obey you to pull out and raise, then insert a needle with strong thread double, and tie it, and pull it out: or insert beneath it a rod: and move your hand to every part, until it comes out. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to heal it, and a defect from it will increase over the weakened part.
When you have extracted it entirely, then put on the place of the wound a wool cloth soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, and treat it until it is healed.
What if the vein is contracted, having a twisted condition with an involution to the parts, and it is not as we have said according to rectitude: then it is necessary that you find on it at every part of its twist, and the place of its involution.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you elevate it entirely: and beware at your dissection above it lest you fall into the vein, or wound: for it will be difficult for you to elevate, or extract it. Preserve therefore your prudence.
The figure of the incision by which the vein is healed.
The figure of the blind hook.
It does not have curvature like the other hooks, nor is it of sharp extremity, so that it does not wound the vein: and it is of coarse flexibility, gentle: for if it were subtle, it would fall into the vein by its subtlety: indeed let there be superfluous grossness to it, as we have said.
On the extraction of the saphena vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot climates, such as Alizegi, and the land of Arabia, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness, that is, in which no herb or tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, just as occur within bodies of snakes and worms, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
The sign of the inception of this vein is a severe inflammation which occurs in the thigh. Then the place should be warmed. Then the vein begins to come out from the place of that warming, as if it were a root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore the extremity of it appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead, the weight of which is one or two drachms, and tie it, and suspend the lead from the thigh.
As often as anything comes out from it to the outside, wrap that in lead and tie it. What if it is prolonged a great deal, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root until the whole thing has come out. For if you cut it, it contracts and returns into the flesh, and causes an abscess to occur, and decay in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Wherefore it is necessary that it be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it comes out from the farthest part of itself, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein come out in certain men what is five palms and ten in length. And it has already happened to me that a man came out with it twenty palms long.
If however it is cut during the time of your treatment, then introduce the needle into its opening, and perforate it with a long needle, with a thread, until all that is in it of material comes out: and administer putrefaction of the place with medicines for some days. Then treat it with the cure for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself: and there occur to it many openings, and a branch comes out from each opening.
Heal the wound itself, as we have said in the division, and with what has been prescribed beforehand.
On the excision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called a cattle disease.
This disease is called among us cattle disease; because it often happens to oxen, and there is a small worm which is born between the skin and flesh.
And it walks throughout the body, going up and down: and it is shown to sensation while walking from limb to limb, until it breaks out wherever it breaks out on the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the rot of certain humors. As worms and snakes happen, that is, intestinal worms, and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared because of its wickedness. For when it walks in the body and rises to the head, and arrives at the place: then perhaps it opens in it, and emerges, and destroys the eye: and this happens many times.
When therefore you want to cure it, and extract it, then it is not done unless by its movement, and the appearance of itself to sensation.
It is necessary therefore, that you bind what is above it, and what is below it with a strong constraining bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
Which if it is deep in the flesh, and you do not find it: then apply to the place a cautery with fire, until it dies.
And what is feared most, is its corruption, which corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If however you see it having already arrived at the head near the eye, then bind beneath it the eyebrow with a good tight bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is fitting that the weak person should use purification of their body with medicines that dissolve putrid foul humors.
And let him beware of foods which generate putrefaction.
On the excision regarding the illness of Nakir.
This illness is called in our land Nakir, and it is a pain which occurs in certain members, then it moves from member to member.
And I indeed have already seen it, according to what I relate to you. I was called to a weak woman in certain villages. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw one swelling increase with a gurgling sound, as a worm increases, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than it is able to be: so that it is like living silver, when it runs from place to place.
The pain therefore receded from that place, and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran into the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it wandered throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
I was amazed therefore at the swiftness of its change from member to member. And I indeed had not seen this illness with my eyes in this manner before. However I saw many who find the pain changing from member to member, and I did not see it with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is, except that it is because the woman was one of those who are from villages of dry bodies and detected veins, hence therefore it appears to the sense that the migratory condition is changed.
And it is necessary that they do not appear according to this method in those who are delicate and have moist bodies of hidden veins.
When you apply the treatment to it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to your eye, as we have said, then bind above it and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that constricted bloodiness goes out: and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with bodily evacuation, and with that which expels bloodiness, and purges them with foul-smelling pills, and pills of serapion, and similar things.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are differentiated according to their types and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is this: that among them are those which have large and small heads: and are concave, and convex. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those which have barbs. And among them are those which have stars.
Those indeed which are according to the members in which they fall, are according to two modes. Or the members are from the principal hollow members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and their likes. When therefore it falls into any one of these members, and the signs of death appear to you, which I will tell you after, then it is necessary that you should desist from extracting that arrow from them, for death follows it in him in whom it is, in most dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For indeed when an arrow falls into the brain and penetrates the skull, and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, it happens that there occur: soda uehemens, and scotomia, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflation, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and mixing up of the intellect, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And a white humidity similar to poultice exits from the place of the wound. And there exits from it something similar to the suppuration of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left breast: and he feels as if it is fixed in a hard thing, not in a soft thing. And perhaps the arrow has a motion similar to the motion of a pulse: and black blood runs out from the wound. And it is followed by coldness of the extremities, and a cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death descends without doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung, is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck become swollen, and the color of the sick person changes. And he breathes out a great, high breath: and he seeks an attraction of cold air.
For if the arrow falls into the membrane that is in the breast: then it is near the small ribs: and there is made a great panting with vehement pain, with sighing: and all the members of the shoulders are moved.
And if an arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps it will come out of the wound with some undigested food.
And if an arrow falls into the belly and is fixed in it, something of feces will come out of the wound, or a piece of intestine, or gut which is already torn. There is therefore no hope for its cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
What if an arrow falls into the bladder and urine comes out, and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger above the weakened area: then you should know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder, and arm, and the vertebrae of the back, and the elbows, and the hips, and the legs, and their like, are saved from the members according to many dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery or a nerve: and the arrow is not venomous.
And I tell you what I have seen regarding the removal of arrows, so that you may understand by it about your cure.
Which is, that to a certain man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. I therefore extracted it from him from the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye below the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not take out of it except a small extremity, with which it adhered to his wooden arrow, and it was a large arrow among the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no notches.
The Jew was therefore healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted an arrowhead from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabian arrow, and is that which has barbs. I sewed over it between two guidings: and now it was submerged in his throat. I was careful with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man into whom it had fallen in his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
After which thirty days passed for him, and nothing was changed regarding his condition, I sewed over the arrow, and I applied a poultice to it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no evil accident happened to him.
And I treated a man into whom an arrow had fallen in his back, and the wound was consolidated over it. After which it was, after seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I treated a woman into whom an arrow fell in her belly, and the wound was consolidated over it, and the arrow remained inside her: and nothing was changed regarding her condition, and the woman did not find any harm in any of her natural functions.
And I treated another man, into whom an arrow fell in his face: and the wound was consolidated, and it remained, not finding much pain for him: and many things similar to these.
And I extracted an arrow from one of the King's princes, which had fallen into him in the middle of his nose, and already it had declined to the right side a little: and the whole arrow was hidden.
I was called therefore to his treatment after the accident of the arrow, for three days. I found the wound of the arrow constricted strongly.
I searched for it with a subtle probe, and I did not feel it: and he himself felt a puncture and pain under his right ear. The spear was removed, which was that puncture from the extremity of the arrow.
I applied a plaster to the place with a plaster in which there was an attractive and digestive virtue, trusting with me that it would bring forth the place to a head and the sign of the arrow would appear to me, so I could cut above it: and no accident happened in the place indicating that the arrow had struck the place.
I treated it diligently with a plaster over it for many days: and no accident occurred, and the wound was consolidated meanwhile: and he remained weak from the extraction of that space for some days, until he felt the arrow on a certain day inside his nose. And he told me about it.
And he told me about it. I applied therefore a sharp medicine, corroding for many days, until it was opened: and when I probed I probed it, and I felt the extremity of the arrow subtle which adhered to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us: and a space of time passed for me with it for about four months.
Then after the wound had been enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it, and move it, and it did not obey to come out: then I did not cease to contrive, and devise over it with methods of devices, until I began on it on a certain day with well-made forceps, according to which the shape of them will be found at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And doctors indeed judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I restored it therefore, and the wound was consolidated, and the weak one was healed with complete health: and nothing harmed him at all.
And I will tell you the quality of extracting certain arrows: so that you put that rule and meaning on that which I did not say.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with narration: nor does the book comprehend it, and the skillful artisan compares a little with much: and with what is present, with that which is absent: and he finds a new operation, and a new instrument according to the descent of extraneous things of this art when they occur.
I say therefore, that arrows do not come out of the members in which they are fixed, except according to two modes. Or by traction from the place into which it entered. Or by the opposite part of another.
And that which comes out from where it entered, is an arrow proceeding into a fleshy place: and is drawn, and comes out.
For if it does not obey to exit at the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you leave it for some days, until the flesh which is around it putrefies: and its attraction will be alleviated, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in a bone, and does not obey you to exit, then leave it also for some days: and move it with attraction and motion every day, until it comes out.
For if it does not obey you to exit after days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the bone on every side with a subtle drill, until the arrows are freed. Then draw it out, and extract it.
If the arrow is already hidden in a location of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sensation: then investigate it with probing. If you feel it, then attract it with certain instruments that are suited to its attraction.
If you cannot reach it, on account of the constriction of the wound, and on account of the length of the arrow in the depth: and there is not a bone, nor a nerve, nor a vein: then probe above it, until the wound is enlarged, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
What if there are to him nerves that hold it, then free the flesh fixed in them on all sides, with every device that you can, and devices, if it cannot overcome the swelling of the flesh in the fracture of the ears, and their distortion, until it comes out.
And you when you administer the extraction of the arrow, in whatever place it is, then use distortion of your hand, with forceps on all sides, until you make it come out: and proceed with ultimate subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its attraction and extraction become difficult for you.
What if you cannot overcome it in one hour, then leave it for some days, until what are around it putrefy. Then repeat it: for the tunic will be loosened, if indeed a contrary condition makes for you an efflux of blood, then administer what we said concerning treatment in its own chapter: and preserve your strength from the incision of a vein, or nerve, or cord.
And administer the arrow's extraction in every way that is possible for you, and let it be with facility, and tranquility, or slowness, and firmness, as I have narrated to you.
And it is fitting, that you do when you draw out the arrow, that the weak part be according to the figure, according to which it was in the case of the arrow itself. For it is a greater assistance. If however it is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
The arrow which comes out from the opposite part of another, or already proceeds from it something to the exterior: or makes a gibbosity of the extremity of the arrow by sensation from the upper part of the adjacent skin, and you see it prominent: make an incision over it, and let the incision be according to the quantity that the forceps can grasp in it.
Then draw it out: for it becomes easy to come out.
If indeed it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to its roundness, until the arrow impresses itself in the bone, and enlarges itself: then draw it out.
And if not: leave it for some days: then repeat it, until it comes out.
What if the wood of the arrow is in it, then push it with that.
If however the wood has fallen, and you want to administer a push: then introduce to it a hollow instrument, so that its hollow enters into the tail of the arrow: then push it with it.
If however the arrow head is barbed then push it out with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is indeed pulled out through that.
What if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary, that the barbed flesh, in which is the venom, be cut out: if it is possible for you. Then treat it with what is suitable for that.
What if the arrow falls in the breast, or in the stomach, or in the bladder, or in the side: and is near to that which you touch with your finger, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting the vein, or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it, and treat it, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let there be similar extremities like a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp above the arrow, they do not release it.
And many types are made from them, large and small and medium, according to the quantity of the size of the arrow, and its thinness, and the width of the wound, and its constriction.
The form of a pusher for barbed things.
And the form of a pusher for pointed things.
This is the extremity of the rod, like a radius, so that its entrance is pulled out from the barbed arrow, and it is pushed with it.
Of the venesection of veins.
The veins for bloodletting, whose cutting custom has run in the body, are thirty.
From which there are in the head sixteen: two pulsating Veins, which are behind the ears, called two Herbae; and two Arteries, which are on both temples apparent; and two Veins which are in both tear ducts of the eyes, which are called Aspicientes; and a Vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The Veins however which are bled in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalica, and it is from the left side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Fusca. And it is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilica, and it is placed on the domestic side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the cord of the arm, and it is placed above the elbow: and it is that in which bloodletting is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb with a clear manifestation.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and there are two branches of it.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is which is under the knee joint on the left side. And the second is the Saphena: and its location is near the heel on the inner side. And the third is the Vena Nesse: and its location is near the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins.
On the bloodletting of two veins behind the Ears.
The bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the Ears is a remedy for ancient catarrhs and hemicrania and sahafati and old bad head ulcers.
And the quality of bloodletting it is according to what I will narrate. And it is that the head of the weak person should be rubbed: and his occiput should be rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth and good friction. Then the weak person should bind his neck with a cord, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the Ears in two depressed areas of the head. Seek them therefore with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, there mark with a cautery.
Then take the lancet for bloodletting, and it is that which is called the Incisor. Then insert it under the vein in the skin, until the bloodletter reaches the opening. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin with an incising cut. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers, or about that, and make come out from the blood as much as you want.
Then bind it with dressings, and leave it, until it heals.
And now the cutting of them has been dealt with at the beginning of the book, and also their cauterization.
Of the two arterial veins that are in the two temples for bloodletting, the remedy is for old migraine: and foul sore, and constant eye disease: and the running of overflowing acrid humors to the eyes.
And the quality of bloodletting them is according to what I relate to you. The weak one will press his neck strongly, until two veins appear with a clear visible appearance: and the pulse of them will be shown under your finger. Then mark with cauterization.
Then raise the skin above the vein with your index finger, and insert the bloodletting needle underneath, and raise the vein to the upper parts. And cut it, as you have done with the two other veins: and make the blood flow according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then release the constriction of the weak one, and put your finger on the vein for one hour. Afterwards put a plaster and poultices on it, and bind it strongly above with a tight bandage: and leave it, until it is healed.
And now the recollection of them has passed, and their cutting, and their extraction at the beginning of the book.
The remedy of the bloodletting of the vein of the forehead is after the bloodletting of the head, for old facial pains: and for migraine, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its bloodletting is, according to what I relate to you. The weak one constricts his neck with a band, until the vein appears. Then take the instrument that is called a Fossorium.
Place a prominent needle which is on the head of the vein, and strike above it with the pectoral or with another tool in its manner, and let blood flow according to the quantity that you want. Then loosen the weak perforation, and constrict it, until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad phlebotome. But truly it is not suitable, that the phlebotome should be of sharp extremity, as are the rest of the phlebotomes, but rather of broad extremity. And let the phlebotome be according to the crossing, because the bone is nearby, and perhaps the phlebotome will break when it is subtle.
The treatment of the two veins which are in the two lachrymals of the two eyes, is for an eye affected by itch and redness, and dirt in the eye, and facial pains.
And the quality of the venesection of them is, that the weak neck constricts itself with a thread. Then perform venesection on them, and you should stand above its head.
And let the venesection be according to the crossing for a small length, with a small phlebotome, slightly broad, for there is no flesh in the location. For if the phlebotome is of subtle extremity, perhaps it will break.
Then make the blood flow as much as is necessary for you. And put a plaster on them, and constrict them for one night, and then loosen them.
The venesection of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent sweating, and facial pains, such as half-headache and redness which occurs on the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of his bloodletting is that it constricts the weak part of the neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a subtle, long bloodletting instrument: and insert it into the middle of the lip, between that which distinguishes the nose according to its straightness. Since a vein does not appear to sensation there, and because blood begins to flow at once.
And it is necessary that you withdraw your hand with the bloodletting instrument for a short time: and make the blood flow according to your necessity. Then bind it with one bandage at night. For it is restored quickly.
The instrument of Guidetti's bloodletting is for constriction of asthma, and for the interception of leprosy, and melancholic diseases, and for bad impetigo and ulcers, and corroding, that is, herpes and itching.
And the quality of their bloodletting is that it constricts the weak under both with a bandage on the neck: and let the practitioner stand over the head of the weak person, and the weak person be sitting on a seat.
Then let the bloodletting instrument pierce the vein to the length of the broad bloodletting for a short time. Then extract from the blood a moderate quantity, or according to that which you see is necessary for it. Then do likewise with another vein.
Afterwards loosen the bandage, and constrict the two veins in the middle tightly: so that the weak person is not suffocated. And leave it until morning. For the wound will be healed.
Of the bloodletting of the veins according to Algeberich. The instrument of Algeberich's bloodletting of the veins is because it helps against bad breath in the mouth, and corruption of the gums: and bad ulcers: and cracks of the lips, and bad ulcers, also those which are in the nose, and in its surroundings.
The quality of bloodletting of the veins is that you should make the weak sit before you and stretch their neck with a towel. Then uncover the lip of it, and look at the two veins: one of which is to the right of the lip, and the second to the left of it: and the demonstration of both is through the blackness of the gums. And that vein is because around the edges of them there are small, black veins. Cut therefore both with an incision of the falling.
If however there is doubt about you, and you do not know which of them it is: then proceed to the cutting of the larger one, and its more manifest.
And do likewise concerning the two veins which are in the upper lip: and mostly custom runs with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Of bloodletting of the vein under the tongue after the bloodletting of the head. The aid of the two veins which are under the tongue for bloodletting is, that the bloodletting of the head is for the afflictions which are in the throat, and the sickness of the uvula, and the sicknesses of the mouth.
And the quality of bloodletting them is that the weak should sit between your hands before you in the sun: and raise their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side a vein, and on the other side a vein: and the color of both declines to blackness.
Bloodlet therefore both: and beware, lest you wound your hand in bloodletting them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps it happens that there is a flow of blood from those arteries.
The three veins which are bloodlet in the elbow, are where bloodletting custom runs in all men.
And bloodletting of the ears is done in two ways. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle-shaped lancet, or with an olive-shaped one for subtlety, or by cutting with a knife-shaped lancet, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and subtle types according to the width of the veins and their tightness The form of the myrtle-shaped lancet is broad, as you see. And it suits for opening hollow veins, full ones, protruding ones, visible ones, thick ones, and those which contain coarse, turbid blood.
And this lancet is of lesser breadth, and of more subtle extremity.
And it suits for bloodletting of subtle veins, which contain subtle yellowish blood.
The royal vein, which is one of these three veins for bloodletting, is a privilege. For it extracts blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, following the chest and stomach: and it suits the bloodletter to bloodlet with it, so that he may beware, and be above fear of it, for beneath it is an artery. If therefore he errs, and adds to the prick of the lancet, he will cut that artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Wherefore it is necessary that the bloodletter does not bloodlet with the lancet, but rather cuts with Alnesil.
Which if it does not appear with a clear royal appearance, then it is necessary that you displace it, and change to another, or seek some branch of it: or in place of the bloodletting, use a ligature on the arm. It itself is obvious, and cut it with a lancet, Anesil, as we have said.
But if you perform phlebotomy on the same, then it is necessary before the tightening of the arm, so that you touch the place, until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark on the place with a cauterizing agent, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave section using a lancet, Alnesil, as we have said. And beware that the blow does not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you tie the ligature, an inflation in the place that you marked with a cauterizing agent, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: avoid it therefore.
For if you see blood in phlebotomy jumping, like the urine of a child, and it is subtle, red blood: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then press closely, and put your finger on it over a long space, then remove your finger. If the blood is then cut off (for it is often cut off), then tie the arm, and let it be: and let the weak one beware of its motion: and let him be above its fear.
And do not move it for some days, until it is healed.
But if the blood does not stop, and overcomes you: and your medicine is not present at the moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For the extremities of it contract, and the blood is cut off.
Take the bark of a plant, and bind it: take one half, and tie it over the place of the vein with a proper tightening, with a ligature and poultices until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then the cure itself with that which preceded it, a remembrance of the place of powders for cutting off the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, on account of the smallness of the place of the wound, and the possibility of binding from the arm.
The bloodletting of the dark vein is a remedy, which draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body: because it is composed of the branch of the Royal and the branch of the Head, as we have said.
And it is suitable for the bloodletter to be cautious with it, that he should be above fear from its bloodletting, for beneath it is a nerve. If therefore he adds to the puncture of the bloodletting and finds the nerve, there occurs in it numbness, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not be completely healed.
And this nerve often appears to sensation. If however it is hidden in some men, and is subtle, and does not show itself: then it is necessary that you place your bloodletting where you will bloodlet with a cut with Alnesil, and divert the nerve with your strength.
Which if the vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in its length.
Of the vein of the head. The bloodletting of the vein of the head is a remedy, because it attracts blood from the head and applies to diseases of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish, that you bloodlet it by pricking with an olive bloodletter, or with a myrtle bloodletter: because it is the savior of all veins, because there is not beneath it an artery, nor a nerve.
The vein that suits you for bloodletting is the one where you find the head of the elbow, and you look for a thinner spot: and it does not harm if it is not found with the first puncture, so that it is repeated over it several times with the bloodletting instrument.
However a vein perhaps forms an abscess in some men when it is not bloodletted at the first puncture, but that abscess does not harm it in any way.
On the usefulness of Bloodletting, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of the bloodletting however, and its accidents that arise from it, are things in which you should consider correction.
First therefore it is that which you ought to know, that bloodletting is not administered except in the conservation of health and its preservation and caution against the approach of diseases.
And that bloodletting is done on one of the three veins that are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilic. And that bloodletting is done at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, and in summer, and autumn, and winter after digestion, when signs of fullness appear.
And bloodletting is done on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed from the day.
Bloodletting which is administered in diseases does not have a determined hour, but when convenience and necessity provoke it, at night or day, and every hour, and every time.
And it is not suitable for children to be bloodletted until fourteen years are upon them. Nor should a pregnant woman be bloodletted, except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor should old men be bloodletted, who have already passed sixty years.
When someone wants to undergo bloodletting for any condition, it is necessary that they cleanse their intestines before bloodletting with a mild enema, if there is a lot of retained feces in them, so that the veins do not attract putrid and harmful superfluities from the intestines during bloodletting to the principal members.
Nor should bloodletting be performed on a patient who is vomiting, that is, with nausea, nor one who is drunk or vomiting from intoxication, until that is removed from them. Nor one who is weak with dropsy.
And bloodletting should be avoided again after a colic passion, and vomiting, and flux of the belly, and frequent intercourse, and labor, and exercise, and wakefulness, and fasting, and anything that dissolves virtue from the bodily or animal substance.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines, if it is possible for him.
Then let him take a bath, if nothing prevents it, or let him be exercised with some exercise, so that the blood is made subtle.
And place bloodletting on his chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said: and take care, that his chest is evacuated that day from all bad animal accidents, such as anxiety, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and punishment, and excessive evacuations, and similar things.
And let him have present things with which usage has created a habit in men from aromatics, and perfumes, and incense and similar things to the quantity of possibility.
Then let the one performing bloodletting sit on a cushion, which is higher than the cushion of the one undergoing bloodletting: afterwards let him draw out his arm, and rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then he should tie the ligature with a bandage, and wrap it twice.
And the tightening should be moderate, because when the tightening is uneven, or excessive, it prevents the flow of blood; and similarly in leniency, it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the tightening it is necessary that the one performing the bloodletting should rub his hands together until the veins become swollen and declare themselves to the sensation.
Then the one performing the bloodletting should wipe the vein with a little old oil, properly.
Then he should place his index finger of the left hand on the vein under the spot where he wants to perform the bloodletting, so that the vein does not flee and the puncture is made.
