For the vein is like a fleeing string when it comes to bloodletting: and of them there are those which are full of wind: and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, and the one performing the bloodletting is deceived, and the vein does not open: and if it does open, it is constricted: therefore it is necessary that the one performing the bloodletting should be steady, and be careful in all these things.
Then he should apply the lancet. If then the vein is opened from one side, then it is good. And if not, then he should try the other side under that same spot a little, or above it with speed, if the spot is not damaged.
What if the spot becomes inflamed, or becomes weak, then let it be for a day, or two days: and do not tie the ligature. For perhaps the warm poultice will attract the inflammation. And let him not enter the bath.
Then he should repeat the bloodletting if it is loved.
What if the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly and it happens that the amount does not come out of the blood as the force: then insert the lancet in the same opening according to the straightness, and add a little in the operation.
And do that with speed, before the part becomes inflamed, for an inflamed part is in many men near a small operation.
If therefore you see it already inflamed, then do not go over it at all, for it does not excuse anything with regard to you. And put on it something from the dregs of oil: since it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your bloodletting, which you perform on veins to prohibit the flow of blood: and they call it tyrical alfarach, and sagzenea, when it is put from one of them on the place.
For the blood is refined and resolved, when it is thick.
What if there happens a great inflammation in the place of bloodletting, for it often happens, especially to him who is not accustomed to bloodletting except by chance, or is the opening of the vein small, then hasten and put on the place a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed a little: and bind it for one hour: since it loosens.
And it is necessary that you do that after the blood has come out from the same vein, when its flow is complete, or from another vein.
What if there remains in the place after days something blackish or greenish, it does not harm: and if you want, put on it something of aloes, or a solution of myrrh: or something from the juice of mint, that is, calament or something similar.
And many times there occur apostems and enlargements with phlebotomy of the Basilica.
Therefore put your hand on it, then if you find softness with compression of it, then that enlargement is bad. Beware therefore, that you put on it something of what we have said. For perhaps blood flows from the arterial vessel.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place is hardened. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man blood according to the quantity of his virtue. And that which appears from the color conquering over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it exit, until it becomes red: and similarly if you see it thick: then let it exit, until it becomes subtle: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs extraction of blood twice, that the phlebotomy of the vein be amplified, and the phlebotomist be warned: and in him who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in the veins, and that the opening be narrow: and better that which is administered in phlebotomy of the vein, is that it be a cross-section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood, and from the cutting of the nerve: and it is more praiseworthy, and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And to whom it is accustomed to faint at phlebotomy of the syncopis, it is necessary that he eat before phlebotomy moderate bread infused in water of pomegranate or secaniabin, if it is warmed: and extract from him blood in three veins, or four.
And if it is of a cold complexion, then let it receive before phlebotomy bread soaked in juice of myrrh or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in syrup aromatized with fragrant odors.
What if it happens to faint at phlebotomy, and the cause of this is the exit of much blood: then it should take in drink water of flesh, and subtle fragrant syrup: and make use of aromatics with gum, and let its chest be bound with it: and make use of the remainder which we said in the division on the chapter of fainting from evacuation.
Whoever wishes to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose phlebotomy is because of much evacuation, and the virtue of which is weak, that he release blood little by little according to the quantity of virtue in continuous days.
Whoever indeed wishes to evacuate his arm and release his blood a second time, and his body is strong: then let him do that after seven hours, or nine from his first phlebotomy.
Whoever wishes to draw blood from his body from the opposite part to which it inclines: it is necessary that he be evacuated on the second day, or on the third.
In whose body there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and it causes fever to happen: it is necessary that blood go out from it at once, and a large quantity be extracted from it: and the opening be enlarged until fainting happens: after you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy: and that you put your hand on his pulse during the flow of blood, so that death does not happen in place of fainting. For it often happens, when the phlebotomy practitioner is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary when the vein has been opened to release the arm and release blood a second time, since the opening of the vein has already been closed, and the exit of blood becomes difficult, so that it is pressed upon with force, and the curved [needle] is extended quickly. For that is a malignant ulcer, or it diminishes itself, until it is blooded a second time.
Remove it with the sharpness of bloodletting that which has coagulated from blood in the opening of the vein, and put upon it something of salt dissolved in water. And put upon it something from theriac of Alfaroch, or Sagzenea, and apply to it a gentle compression, until the blood comes out.
If then the vein is an apostema, leave it, and do not touch it, until the apostema subsides.
What if necessity demands to release the blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either bloodlet it above that place, or in another arm in another vein.
The veins of the arm are bloodlet at the place of the Fuscus and the Basilicus, when they are not found, and are hidden, because it is composed from them.
And the quality of its bloodletting is, that the weak hand is inserted into hot water, until it becomes red and the vein appears manifestly.
Then let it be bound above it with a ligature of moderate constriction. Then bloodlet across a little at a time, not in width, nor in length: and let the opening be wide, and your bloodletting be that which you bloodlet upon the joint of the hand for a short while.
What if the exit of blood is forbidden, then put the hand again in hot water, and let the blood flow into the water, until you obtain your necessity.
If however you are in the days of summer, then you are excused from putting the hand again in hot water. And the flow of blood into hot water is used a lot in the time of winter.
And the bloodletting of this vein is a savior to all veins, because there is not under it a pulsating vein, nor a nerve.
Venesection of the right hand is conducive to ailments of the liver.
And the quality of its venesection is that you bind the veins of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you have inserted it in warm water until the vein becomes inflamed and is demonstrated to sensation strongly. Then venesect the vein by making a cross incision. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm it in anything.
And beware, lest you venesect your own hand. For under it are the nerves of the fingers stripped of flesh.
Then put the hand in warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not repeat it in warm water, the blood congeals in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
When you have extracted the amount of blood necessary, then put oil and salt on the vein: so that it does not close quickly.
And similarly it is necessary to do with every constricted branch.
The remedy of venesection on the left hand is: because it is conducive to ailments of the spleen: and do similarly in its venesection as you did in the second equally.
The remedy of venesection of the saphena vein is for ailments that are in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the uterus, and retention of menses, and weaknesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and thighs, and similar ailments.
And the quality of its venesection is that the weak person inserts their foot into warm water: and makes friction on it until the veins become swollen: and binds above the joint of the foot with a ligature. And the place of the vein is near the heel, visible towards the thumb: and many branches ramify from it on the sole of the foot.
Bloodletting is performed on the larger branch of it, or at the heel in its connection, because it is better and more beneficial for health.
What if bloodletting is performed on the face of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are under it above the face of the foot.
And put your bloodletting where you perform it from a transverse position, as if you wish to cut it, and let the bloodletter be Nesil.
If the flow of blood is stopped, then immerse the foot in warm water, and let the blood flow into it, until it is evacuated.
What if the bloodletter misses the vein at first, then repeat the bloodletting towards the upper parts for a little while, for the place is safe, and you will not get harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot equally.
The location of the sciatic veins, as we have said, is at the heel on the wooded side.
And the indication for bloodletting of it is, for pain of the hip, when it is due to warm blood.
And the quality of its bloodletting is, that the weak enter the bath and be bound, and let the leg be constricted on the side of the hip up to above the heel four fingers, with a thin long bandage, for it does not appear except because of this.
When it appears, then perform bloodletting according to whatever method is possible for you, or according to a crosswise position, and it is better, either that you cut it with an incision, or that you cut it with a section. For its location is safe, and it is hidden in many men to a great extent.
For its location is safe, and it is hidden in many men to a great extent. What if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the senses at all, then perform bloodletting on some branch of it, and it is that which appears on the back of the foot, and opposite the ear, and beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow in quantity as you wish.
Then loosen the tightening, and put a piece of leather over the place of bloodletting, and constrict the place. For it will heal quickly.
Of Purging, and the quality of its use.
Clysters are made from horns, and from copper, and from glass.
And Purging is done in two ways. With scarification, and the extraction of blood: and the other is purging without scarification.
And this Purgation which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the purgings that are administered with scarification and the extraction of blood, are in fourteen places of the body. Of which one is the purging of Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and the purgings of Alhagdahim, and they are two sides of the neck from both parts together: and the purgings of the jaw, and it is under the jaw in the lower mouth: and the purgings of the two shoulders: and the purgings of Alachosos above the end of the tail: and the purgings of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and the purgings of the two thighs: and the purgings of the two hams.
And this Purgation does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that, virtue does not come out from them, as is the case with bloodletting. Nor is it necessary to administer purging, either with fire or without fire, in any of the illnesses that occur from overfilling, until the whole body is emptied.
What if necessity calls for Purgation from illness, or because of habit, we administer it at every hour, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
What is it, that which is from humans who when the blood in them multiplies to the point that it requires extraction through venesection, finds in its head a heaviness and swooning.
And there is from them one who finds fullness and redness in his face, his head, and his neck. And there is from them one who finds itching in his face and his forehead, and darkness and dimness in his eyes.
And there is from them one who scratches the place of his venesection. And there is from them one whose sneezing is multiplied. And there is from them one who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or it collects on his tongue and he spits up blood. And there is from them one whose sleep is multiplied. And there is from them one who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things that are similar to those.
When therefore we see any of these things, and it is properly so in the third day of the month: we then order venesection to be done, after two or three hours have passed.
The juice of the Venesection of the nut, is: because it brings about heaviness in the head, and that which flows to the eyes. But indeed it should be that it is after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this Venesection is in place of the phlebotomy of the head.
And it is not permitted that one who is of a cold complexion, or in whom there is a catarrh, should use it. For it brings to them great harm.
And because of that it is not fitting that the old use it, and those in whose head there are cold agues.
He who does it frequently over it, is overcome by forgetfulness.
And because of that, it is necessary to instruct the one performing the surgery, that he should put his hand with the cannula for a short time towards the lower parts, fearing the generation of an obstruction.
Ventosatio or Alkehel is in place of phlebotomy of the spleen and phlebotomy of Basilic, and because of that it treats asthma and constriction of breathing and fissure of the instrument of breathing and coughing and replenishment.
And it is necessary that the cannula of Alkehel be pushed in for a short time, because if it is placed below, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Ventosatio indeed is a treatment for accidental pains in the head, and diseases of the eyes, and migraines, and perforations, and pain in the roots of the teeth: and it is in place of phlebotomy of Basilic.
And it is necessary to instruct the one performing the surgery, that he should not press his hand with the incision: so that he does not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
Ventosatio under the chin treats ulcers in the mouth and corruption of the gums and similar things from diseases which are in the mouth: and it is in place of phlebotomy of the two lips.
Ventosatio of the spatula treats the alchafacham, which is caused by retention and heat.
Ventosatio indeed of the interior parts of the two armpits treats the place of phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilic, and Splenic, and Cephalic: because it itself draws blood from all those tiny little veins which are in the flesh: and those tiny little veins draw from other thicker ones until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary to instruct the one performing the surgery, that he should not proceed far in the incision: because the area is bare of flesh, and under it are nerves and arteries.
A vein that is cauterized on the alchausos constricts hemorrhoids and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary to warn the cauterizer that the cautery should be large and made of copper: because the location requires strong suction and subtle cauterizations are likely to break.
Cauteries of the hips diminish with obvious reduction: because they attract blood from the whole body and increase old pains in the kidneys, bladder, and uterus: they provoke menstruation, and cause bothor and ademenul: and they take the place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause syncope to occur in many men.
And the benefit of cauterizing the alhorcobene is similar to the benefit of cauterizing both hips.
The quality of the position of cauterizations is that you should place a cautery in a vacant space, and apply moderate suction: and do not prolong the application of cauterizations, but rather put them on quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the area equally: and do not cease to repeat and continue this, until you see the area already red and inflamed, and an appearance of blood appears: then scarify, and repeat the suction little by little. Then consider the disposition of bodies.
For one who is of tender flesh and rare pores among men, it is necessary to scarify him only once, not more: so that the location does not become ulcers.
And instruct the cauterizer to enlarge the scarification, and make it a little deeper: and moderate the suction with facility, velocity, and his own movement.
For if there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. In this case, what is first considered is the subtlety of the blood and its thinness, and secondly the scrutiny of the exact extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very foul, then let there be a third incision, so that it attains the ultimate.
And finally, when we want to extract blood gradually, or for a short time, one incision is sufficient for us. What if we want the extraction of much blood, we make many incisions.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that there be a deep incision: and the limit of equality in the incision is the depth of the skin.
What should be administered from oils at the application of poultices, and what the poultice itself will absorb.
In that case in which the skin is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are constricted, it is necessary that the places of the poultice be anointed with opening, softening, resolving oils: if it is in the summer season, such as is oil of almonds, or oil of violet, or oil of sweet almond, or oil of gourd seed.
And if it is in the winter season, then oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or sabbatical oil: and their similars.
If the surplus is thick and cold, then let the oil be bitter, or oil of balsam, or oil of albene, or oil of anetina: and their similars.
If the poultice is of large pores, of tender flesh: then it is necessary that it be prohibited from oil, and it is necessary that these places of the poultice be washed after the poulticing with rose water, or with cold water, or with solative water, or with gourd water, or with purslane water: and their similars.
He whose blood is of much humidity, should wash the vents with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumach: and similar things.
Whose superfluities are gross, should wash his vents with old wine, or with water of samsuc, or decoction of anet, or camomile: and similar things.
And it is fitting that the ventization should be done in a bath, or in a dresssed chamber of a bath, and it is necessary that it should be administered after exiting the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not fitting that anyone should sleep after ventization.
What is it that one should be governed by, either the one to be purged or the bloodletter before purging.
And after that it is fitting that you consider first: then if the one to be purged or the bloodletter is choleric, and his blood is acute with inflammation: then it is necessary that he receive cooling things, such as cold granules: and also an enema with vinegar and lettuce, and secancabin, and iuleb: and similar things.
And let his food be soft-boiled, and the flesh of boiled eggs, and seasoned foods with verjuice: and similar things.
And whose complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in a drink syrup of honey, or of almond, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take fragrant wine moderate, which is between old and new: and let him be prescribed to use food in small quantity. And let his food be soft-boiled, and cabbage: and pease, that is, small birds: and boiled or cooked meat of pigeons.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of purging and bloodletting be more than the food: and it is necessary that there be given in a drink to certain men at the third hour alfaroch, or medicine from musk, that is, muscat, or alesselithe before purging: and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal members be comforted, and the blood be refined. And it is not suitable for them to take it in a hot drink.
On inflammation of the blood vessels without scarification. Inflammations of the blood vessels which are without scarification, are those that are applied to the liver, and spleen, and gout, and on the navel, and umbilicus, and the region of the kidneys, and the hump of the ankle: because these members do not suffer scarification upon themselves.
And it is not sought through them, unless there is an attraction of blood from one member to another member: as we apply a plaster on the breasts in the disease of flowing nosebleed. And we use them to resolve a cold and viscous inflammation from a member to another member: as we apply a plaster on the abdomen, and navel. For it rarefies the member, and heats it, and removes the pain for this reason, because it resolves that inflammation.
And it is also applied on the kidneys, when there occurs in them a blockage or stone. For by virtue of its attractive power it perhaps opens up the blockage, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when it is applied on the liver and spleen for a third type of inflammation in both of them.
And these plasters should be administered with the juice alone, without cutting. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water for diseases of the pleura: and that is, so that the plaster may be filled, and be large, with hot water alone, or water in which herbs that are suitable have been decocted.
The form of a plaster administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two fingers, according to which we form it: and its quantity in depth be half a palm: and let there be on its edge, in the place which is around the middle of it, a small hole, according to the quantity that the needle enters: let it be made from bronze or citrus, with thick, smooth, equal, polished edges: so that it does not harm the member at its application upon it: and let there be in its middle a transverse rod of bronze or iron, where the candle is placed with fire.
And now this large spongy vein is made, greater than we have described, and a little less, according to the illnesses and age of the one who uses it.
For the spongy veins of children and thin people are different from those of men and fat bodies.
The quality of placing this spongy vein with fire on a membrane is to light a linen thread with fire from a candle or a small wax taper and place it above the middle of the rod that is across in the middle of the spongy vein, so that the fire is lit around the lower part of the spongy vein, so that the body of the weak person does not get burned.
Then it should be placed on the membrane, and the finger should be above the opening that I mentioned, until when we hold the spongy vein: while we press, we remove the finger, and vapor will exit above that opening, and the spongy vein will dissolve immediately.
Then light a thread according to its measure and apply it, if you press on it.
In the spongy vein that is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no cross rod nor opening: and it is not administered except that it may fill with water and is placed on the membrane only.
And this is its form.
And the larger this spongy vein is, so that it may hold much water, the better it is.
Of the hanging of bloodletting cups.
Bloodletting cups are not administered on many parts, except on those members where it is not possible to place spongy veins, because of their smallness, such as the lip and gums, and similar things. Or on a member bare of flesh, such as the finger and nose, and similar things.
And the quality of using them is: that they are taken from bloodletting cups which are in sweet waters, clean from impurities.
Then they should be immersed day and night in fresh water, until they are softened, and nothing remains in their stomach. Then the body should be treated at first with bloodletting, and with applications to the abdomen. Then the weak member should be cleaned, until it becomes red. Afterwards they will be placed on top of it.
When they fill and fall, and it is possible to suck the place with an application, then it is most useful for healing: and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and rubbed, and squeezed out.
What if the flow of blood is prolonged after a fall of blood vessels, and it is that which is flowing back: then soak a linen cloth in cold water, and put it on top, until the flowing back is stopped.
What if the blood increases, then powder over it with a powdered zeolite, or gall, and similar things from astringents: until the blood is stopped.
Or they will be placed on the place, crushed broad beans, and left until the beans stick to the spot, for the blood is stopped.
And it is necessary if a repetition of bleeding is needed, when it is possible, to change them.
If however the blood vessels are forbidden from suspension, then the place will be cleaned with fresh blood. Or a needle is thrust into the places, until something comes out of the blood. Then they will be placed, for as soon as they feel something from the blood, they stick at once.
When they are used so that they fall, powder over them with something from powdered aloe, or salt, or ash: and they will fall at once.
This chapter is also many times necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures, and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O sons, that the foolish among the physicians and the common people throw themselves into this chapter, stupidly, and who have never inspected the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it happens that this method is deprived of the sciences in our land. And I did not find anything good in it at all, and I would not have attained what I attained except for the length of reading the books of the ancients, and study above understanding them, until I extracted the knowledge from them.
Then I adhered to experimentation and study throughout the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this Chapter all that my science contains: and on which my experimentation has passed, after I had abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of length, and abbreviated it at the end of abbreviation, and shown it at the end of declination: and formed in it many forms of the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
It is fitting that we say in the prologue of this Chapter, the sums of speech, and chapters, to whose understanding is compelled at first the artisan to stand on the truth of it: namely that you, and who are prepared, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or fall happens to someone, then it is proper first to perform bloodletting on him and to administer a purging treatment, or both at the same time, if nothing prevents it, such as lack of strength, or if he is one of those persons who has a condition in childhood, or an old decrepit person, or if it is a time of intense heat or intense cold.
Then he should be content with his food being cool, and with fowl that is chilled, and with birds' meat, and with fish: and he should prohibit wine, and fatty meats, and filling foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until he is safe from the hot abscess, and does not bring about an outflow of matter to the member: then the weak person should return to his first regime with which the habit of his body was running.
When, therefore, the fractured bone begins to be restored, then it is proper that the weak person should be fed with nutritious foods of much nourishment, thick, heavy, in which there is viscosity: such as dishes made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and cabbage, and feet and innards and livers of beasts, and eggs, and fresh fish: and rough wine, and similar things. For with this regime the clotting of the fracture happens more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in persons who are in early old age and decrepit, it is not possible that they should knit together and consolidate according to their nature always, on account of the dryness of their membranes: and they do consolidate and knit together which ones of them are at the end of tenderness, such as bones of small children.
However, nature attaches to the fractured bone from every part something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds itself, until they knit together and bind together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until something does not hinder its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you put nourishing food on the weak person, in which there are thickness, viscosity, and grossness, as we said.
And you should know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the members, since a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the head bone, and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the back bone, and similarly all the remaining members are differently joined, and I came with remembrance of every type of fracture exhibited in its distinct Chapter, separate from another. And the types of fractures of bones are diverse even within themselves.
Since there occurs a fracture of it that is curved, besides what happens to it in terms of dislocations. And sometimes there occurs a fracture of it along the length of the bone, and there are fractures of it that are displaced, that is, dislocations, and additions that proceed, as they are in a reed, when it is adapted to a knife for writing. And not proceeding, and there is a fracture with a wound and a disruption in the head, and there is a small splitting fracture.
And every type has a device, or its own proper instrument for restoration, according to which comes the remembrance of it, distinct in its own places.
And from that which the fracture of the bone is recognized, is its twisting, and its own protrusion, and the appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When therefore there is not a visible twisting in the place, nor a sound upon touch with your hand when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the weak one find great pain, then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there is a twisting or a small split, and therefore it is not necessary to move it with the hand and compression at all, but rather put on top of it from the medicines, whose remembrance came after the moment that which suits the place, then let it be bound with a subtle bandage.
And you should know that the place when it is curved and split into two, besides what dislocations happen to it, nevertheless every part is moved from its comparison, then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to rectify it, equalizing it before a hot abscess happens to it.
If a hot abscess happens to him, then leave it for some days, until the abscess subsides. Then treat it equally according to whatever method is possible from gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration of it and its equalization is easier than the bones, in which there occur displacements.
Then bind it, according to what the memory of it suggests to you.
If there are displacements in the bone, then extension of this broken member is necessary from two parts, if it is the hand or foot: or with your hand, if it is a small member: or with two cords, or with a cord and hand.
And let your positioning be where you place the member on an equal spot, according to its natural figure, until when the parts of the broken member are extended: then try, that you make those additions return with all skill, and according to whatever method you can in their places: and beware according to your strength, lest pain or injury happen to the weak one through your operation: and try according to your strength, that you apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to the better forms.
And it is necessary at that time, that you feel and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the quantity of your ability.
And beware violent extension and strong compression, as many foolish ones do. For they often cause a hot abscess to happen through their operation, or contraction in the member: as we have already seen that from their operations many times.
Then connect after equalization, and decent operation, and the binding of that member, and quiet, and tranquility: and warn the weak one concerning it. And let it rest, so that it does not move during its hours of watchfulness, and its sleep according to its revolution, and its agitation, and at its evacuation, and all its movements by its final strength. And let it rest, so that the position of the limb is not a position in which pain follows. Which is, that when it feels pain or a lesion in the disposition of the limb's position, then it is necessary to change it to another disposition from the one with which it does not feel pain.
And let it rest with that, so that its position is equal and straight, so that no twisting occurs in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the broken limb is according to which I will describe.
Know that fractured limbs are differentiated in their smallness and largeness, and their shape.
Those therefore which are small among them, such as the arm, and finger, and elbow, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, fine cloth. Those however which are thick among them, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be on them a wrapping of broad, strong cloth.
For a wide bandage connects the large limb and binds it from every side with equal tension, so that it does not press upon it unevenly.
Then after your dressing, put on a plaster suitable to it, over the linen cloth, on the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap a bandage over the place of the fracture itself with three or four wraps, according to what the limb deserves, and tighten your hand for a short time with the bandage.
Then go up to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than your previous tension, with which you tightened the fractured place. Then stretch yourself with an involution from the place of the fracture for a short time, and gradually soften the stiffness, until you take a large thing from the healthy place.
Then take another poultice, and involution it again over the fractured place with involutions. Afterwards dress with an involution to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be on the tightening of the involution and its softening, according to what we said in the first involution above.
Then put between the involutions a dressing of linen or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture is straightened, if there is crookedness in it: and if not, you do not put anything in it.
Then involution another poultice over it. Afterwards place equal to these involutions stars made from plaster immediately, if there is not in the place inflammation, nor hot aposteme.
If there is however in it inflammation or an aposteme, then put on it what settles that aposteme and takes away the inflammation: and leave it for some days. Then bind over it then the plasters.
And let the plasters be from the middle of a split cane, shaved, wisely formed: or let them be from wood from which cribs are made, which are made from pine or gerah, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and let the width of all plasters be according to this form, and this same figure.
Truly it is necessary, that the plaster which is put over the fracture be thicker and wider for a while, than the other plasters: and the length of the plaster be according to what is suitable for the member from its size and smallness.
Then bind over the plasters with another poultice, according to the first tightening, the same. Then bind from above with twisted threads, according to what we have said concerning the bandage: and it is, that your bandage should be above the place of the fracture by more: and the more it is elongated from the fracture, the less the bandage.
And it is fitting, that the threads should be moderate in thickness and fineness, and should be made from soft flax.
For if the threads are thick, such as I have seen from the work of foolish surgeons placing their threads made from twisted flax cords, it is a great mistake: for the bandage falls with them beyond the quality.
And very fine threads are also not suitable: for you will not achieve through the bandage with them that which you wish.
And it is not fitting, that there should be between one star and another less than a finger.
What if a weak person is injured by the ends of the stars after the bandage on healthy places: then put under them from soft lint or woolen cloth, until nothing is injured from that.
When, however, there is a fracture with a wound, and a disruption occurs in the skin, I will discuss it with consideration of its specifics in the second chapter.
And know that not every broken member needs to be tied with stars from the first day. Which is, that when a member is large, it is not fitting to put stars on it, unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your security from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored, which are put on a fracture and dislocation.
The method of a common plaster abbreviated, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially for women and children: since it is from that which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is, that you take from the dust of the millstone, and it is the inner part of the flour which hangs in the sides of the millstone near the motion of the mill: and knead it as it is, except that you sift it with the whites of an egg: and put its confection not thick, nor fine. Then use it.
Another method of plaster for restoring a fracture and a dislocation and a twist. Take mess, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath, and juice of all in equal amount X myrrh, and aloes of each an equal amount V, and alethil XX, let all be pounded, and sifted, and mixed with water of alethil: or with the white of an egg, if the application to the weak person is warmed. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it restores broken bones quickly, and confirms many men.
Another method of plaster, which also confirms fractures and twists. Take mugath, and mess, and white alteae, of all in equal amount X myrrh, and aloes of each an equal amount V acacia VI, and boli armeni XX, let all be pounded with much rubbing, and sifted, and mixed with water, or the white of an egg: and administer.
Another method of plaster for joints, and bones separated from their places: and it calms the accidental pain to them, and the division of bone, and the fracture. Let a woolen cloth be dipped, and submerged in vinegar, and oil cooked, and put on the place.
And in this plaster there is not a virtue of restoration, but it is good for calming hot abscesses: and it drives out pains properly.
Another method of plaster, which restores the broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig tree, and the leaves of the papaver of the forest: and pound all, and make a plaster from both.
Another method of short plaster, administered for the restoration of a fracture of bone: and when you use it for the resolution of the remainder of an abscess. Take the root of marshmallow and chamomile and flowers of violets and powder of herb, all in one portion: let them all be ground and mixed with wine, if the member is not heated.
If the member is heated, then mix it with water of moist coriander or with water and apply it thereto.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution than this first one, when there occurs a hard aposteme during the restoration of a member. Take the root of marshmallow and seed of flax, and pomegranate, and melilot, and samsuch, and flowers of violets and chamomile, all in one portion: let them all be ground and mixed with willow water, or sweet water, or wine. All that according to the heat of the member, and the quietness of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not say in his book that anything should be applied on a fractured member for its restoration, except a cerate made from wax and oil: none other: and he said it is a mean between grossness and subtlety.
To Galen however it seemed, that things which have an exciting of heat, such as myrrh and aloe and frankincense, and the like, should be applied on a fractured member for its restoration.
A method of plaster that relieves lesion and pain. Take mugwort and cress and cut human hair or bird's feathers and marshmallow and salt in equal parts: let them be ground and sifted and mixed: and make from it a plaster.
The quantity however according to which it is necessary that the bandage remains, then is solved, is that you consider then, if there does not occur weakness or pain, nor itching, nor is the fractured bone moved from its place: then do not resolve it for many days. If it happens to him in the place a severe itching, or an annoying pain, or a swelling: then apply and dissolve it at that hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft marine sponge, moist: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the spot with it, until the itching calms, and the pain subsides.
If it happens to him in the place a severe itching, or an annoying pain, or a swelling: then apply and dissolve it at that hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft marine sponge, moist: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the spot with it, until the itching calms, and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then put on it a piece of cloth soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it over the place for one night, until you are secure from a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is relieved.
Then repeat it to a subtle tightening, and a small plaster: and do not bind it with your first constriction, but subtly, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all the accidents have been entirely removed, and you apply plaster and binding, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
If nothing happens to the limb from those things that we have said, then do not dissolve it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, all that according to what appears to you from the solution of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres, and the coagulation of the flesh approaches over it: then add also at that time in the binding more than your entire first binding: and also add in the nourishment of the weak body, according to what the previous recollection of it was.
If you see the place of the fracture already becoming dry and thin more than it should: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore moisten it with warm water at every turn where you dissolve it, and let it be for three days, and loosen the binding a little.
Through this I perform the operation, nourishment runs to the member, and it heals quickly.
What foolish restorers do from a fracture of a member, when it is not restored at first, according to what is proper, and is restored according to the twisting, is an error from their operation and a great fear: and if it were correct, the ancients would have said that in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any trace of them at all in that, and it is more correct that it should not be done.
On the making of an accident on the head.
The species of fractures are diverse, and its figures are many: and the causes of it are distinct.
For there is a fracture which occurs from a blow with a sword: and the blow takes away the bone as far as it reaches to the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of that this species of fracture is named Axial.
Or the sword strikes a part of the bone, and strikes its surface, and the cut does not penetrate to the utmost of it: and this species of fracture is named Kalan, that is, Absolute Removal.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a crushing or an attrition: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which now approaches the periosteum, which is under the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and there is a wound of these two fractures also, either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone to the thinness of a hair: and it is a small crack, and on account of that this species of fracture is named Capillary.
And from them is a fracture that occurs due to a fall or a blow with a stone, and similar things, which cause the splitting of a bone to go inside, and it happens in the place of the concavity: just as it happens to the brewers of brass, when a blow occurs to them, and a part of them goes inside.
And this happens most often in the heads of moist bones, such as are the heads of children.
And there occur to all these kinds of fractures separate displacements, and I will discuss with recollection the entirety of it in its own place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures through detection over them, and inquiry of them with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh that is on them.
The species of the hair-like fracture is recognized by this, that it is detected over the bone, and wiped away, and anointed over it with a plaster: for the fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the weak person: then if you see from the accidents that which signifies manifest signs of fear, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of intellect, and loss of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar things to these from the accidents in many dispositions, without doubt it will not be cured: and if you see accidents not terrifying you, and you hope for health: then begin the cure of it.
That is, if the wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded: and it happens in winter days, then it is necessary that you should strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every disposition.
And if it is in summer days, then it is necessary that you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it becomes corrupted what is under the bone from the padding, and those accidents occur which we have described.
What if the fracture of the bone has now penetrated to the membrane touching the brain, and is with bruising and attraction then it is necessary that you cut the comminuted and attracted part according to what I will tell you.
And it is that you shave the head of the wounded and uncover the bone according to whatever mode is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what is weak above the infirm.
What if it happens to you upon the uncovering of the bone a flow of blood or a hot aposteme then apply to it what is necessary. And it is that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil and make the wound rest until the aposteme settles and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the cure of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is, that you cut the bone with a strict edge incisor and this is its form.
Then administer another incisor wider than the first one for a short while and this is its form.
Then administer also another incisor even wider also than the second.
And the second method is, that you have with you a number of different incisors, of which some are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others, and let them be at the end sharpened to a point, and made from iron or steel, and use facility in striking on the incisor, so that it does not strike the head and harm it.
If the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary that you perforate around it before you administer the incisors, with awls which are called non-penetrating awls, and they are not called thus except that they themselves do not penetrate beyond the end of the bony term into what is behind it, because the awl has a round extremity above what is under the head of its point, similar to an edge, and a small circle prevents sinking and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is suitable for you to take from these trephines a large number of which each one is suitable for the quantity of the thickness of the bone, until there is present to you on the whole skull a trephine, whose quantity of sharpness in length and width is according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of trephines, or trepans.
The quality of making a hole around the broken bone is: that you put the trephine on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone has already been penetrated. Then change the trephine to another place, and put what is around the entire hole the quantity of the thickness of the radius, or around it.
Then cut with the incisors what is around the entire hole in the bone: and make it thinner than you can on the surface of the fineness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, either with your hand or with some other of those instruments which you have prepared for that: such as tenailles and subtle forceps.
And it is necessary that you proceed with all caution, lest the trephine or incisor touch anything of the brain.
When you have cut around the bone by making a hole, and it has been separated from the brain, if it was continuous with it, and you extract it, it is necessary that you scrape, and equal the edge of what remains in the bone with another instrument similar to an incisor. Truly it is necessary, that it be thin and more subtle than other incisors.
What if something remains of the small bones, and fragments: then take them with ease, with that which is with you of instruments. Then treat the wound afterwards with linen bandages, and ointments which we have said.
And another method of the operation is easy, remote from fear, which Galen said and praised it with great praise, and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin to expose the part of the bone in the location where the fracture is most violent and most apparent, until when you have exposed that part and placed under it the extremity of this lentiform incision, whose shape is this.
And the lentiform part does not cut anything gently, and its pointed part enters on both sides of it in length, as you see, so that the lentiform part is applied to the saw, with its pointed face cutting into the bone.
Then strike on the incisor from one side with a small hammer, until you cut the whole bone with ease, as it turns out, and you will be secure from the accident that you fear.
And if the physician were not the most foolish of men and more ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the pannicle in certain places, he would cut it away from it with the extremity of the lentiform incisor, and free it from it with ease. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the pannicle, and that which is cut away from the bone is its face, and there remains in it roughness and subtle scratches: then it is necessary, that you scrape away that roughness, and eradicate those scratches with subtle rasps, which you have prepared in a number of different quantities, so that it is possible for you to administer from them in every place the most convenient of them, and the most correct, according to what the operation leads you to, and the bone itself, fractured, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your scraping, which first scrapes the bone, wider than those of the rasps. Then use a finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you administer in your scraping, which first scrapes the bone, wider than those of the rasps. Then use a finer one after it. And do not stop doing that according to continuity, until you reach the thinnest and finest of all.
In each of the remaining small fissures and fine fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a thing which is not hidden above all, to whom in this art there is any study, and it stands on what we have written, and we show in great fractures.
If indeed the periosteum is detected and retained, when you extract the bone: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and put it on the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and put it on the first cloth: and make that as easily as you can over it, so that it does not press on the spleen.
Then administer over it a broad ligature: and do not bind it, except by the quantity that the wound holds so much: and leave it with this care for one day, or two days, until you are secure from a hot abscess. Then undo it, and administer after that medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as the roots of lily, and flour of herb, and powder of frankincense, and aristolochia, and similar to these: and it is, that you make from these medicines a powder, which is powdered over the wounds as they are dry.
And universally administer on it every medicine of a property whose is that it cleanses and does not bite. And beware according to your virtue, that the wound in your cure is clear: and is not in it dirt of ointment, nor filth.
And beware according to your virtue, that the wound in your cure is clear: and is not in it dirt of ointment, nor filth. And do not allow the pus to accumulate in it at all, since when pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and a great malice befalls the weak after that; and now it befalls the cerebral siphrac, when the mouth is cleansed from it, especially when the care of it is neglected, blackness on its surface.
Consider then, if weaknesses befall, which we have mentioned, you should know that it is perilous without a doubt.
And if blackness does not occur, unless from the medicine that is placed on it, and it is in the virtue of that medicine, that it makes that blackness: then you should take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and anoint the cloth with them. Then place it on the siphrac: afterwards treat it with the species of cures that are suitable, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is fractured.
You should know that the nose is not fractured except when both of its upper parts are broken at the same time, or one of them: because it itself has two bones: since the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it does not fracture, and nothing happens to it except bruising, and twisting, and skewing.
If one of its two parts is fractured, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape: and this should be done with art and facility. And beware, lest pain occurs through your operation on the weak part.
For if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it: then you should straighten it with the end of a ruler, on which there is a small thickness.
If the fracture is in two parts, then make similar to that: and restore it promptly on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, in the quiet of a warm apostume.
Then insert into the nostril a pledget of linen from a cloth, if the fracture is in one part: or insert two pledgets if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be in the pledget a thickness of such quantity, which fills the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said, that the pledgets should be soaked in butter, and changed every day. That indeed does not seem to me, but rather it is necessary, that the pledget be soaked in the white of an egg, mixed with powder of meal. Then leave the pledget alone, until the bone becomes firm, and the cartilage becomes hardened.
And sometimes a feather of a goose is inserted into the nose in place of the pledget, after which a linen cloth is wrapped over it: and it is done so that it itself holds the fracture of the nose more vigorously, and so that it does not prevent the weak from inhalation.
And this thing is not necessary. If you use it, do that: and if you wish, make pledgets.
What if it happens to the nose again while you are operating with a warm apostume, plaster the nose with cerate, or with coten infused in rose oil, or with a little of diacalendine.
If however the warm apostume does not happen, then it is necessary, that you plaster it with flour of sesame, and powder of frankincense mixed with the white of an egg. Then put on top of it a linen swab, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture, or are shattered, then it is necessary, that you cut above them, and extract them with instruments which are suitable for them. Then I dissect and cure it with what promotes flesh and consolidates from suitable plasters or ointments.
What if the wound occurs between the nostrils, then you should cure it with bandages and administer a tube of lead until it is healed.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is broken apart.
When the lower jaw is broken and its fracture is not with a wound, consider then if its fracture is only on the outside and does not break into two, and is bent inwardly, then its treatment is easy.
It is necessary therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert the index finger of your left hand into the mouth of the weak person: similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand: and push with it the curvature of the fracture from within with ease to the outside with your other hand from the interior part of the bone, and straighten it with that.
What if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from both parts, according to straightness: until its equalization is possible.
If indeed a commotion or disruption has already occurred to the teeth, then bind them with threads of gold, silver, or silk. Then put a bandage over the fractured jaw. Then put over it a double cloth, and put over the cloth a large strap made from leather: or a piece of skin sole, equal in length to the jaw.
Then tie it up from above, according to the preparation of its bandage, and suitable application, until it is not destroyed: and order the weak person to rest and quiet: and give their food in gentle sips.
If you think that something has already changed in some shape or manner, then apply a poultice and dissolve it on the third day. Then correct what has been changed, and plaster it with a poultice made from the powder of fenugreek, with the white of an egg, or with flour of barley, after you have removed the dandruff: and put a soft wool over the plaster.
While therefore the poultice remains on it, and the arrangement of the bone is not changed, do not remove it, nor dissolve it, until it is healed: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
What if meanwhile a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its treatment, until the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, take care. For now a fragment is separated from the bone, or by a cut: then be subtle in this, so that you remove those fragments of the cut with what suits you in removing them with instruments.
If indeed the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the quantity of your necessity.
Then when you have removed those cuts, and none of them remain, or then the opening of the wound, if it is large: and if not, put on it a plaster that suits it, and incarnates it, until it is healed.
Of the restoration of the Jaw, when it is fractured.
The jaw is frequently fractured from the front part of the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either fractured and separated into two, besides the cuts that occur in it: and it is easier to restore.
Or that which occurs in the fracture of the jawbone: and is more difficult to restore.
Or it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation on it when there is a fracture without a wound, is that you should have two assistants present, so that one of them may hold the support that follows the fractured clavicle, and the other stretch the neck towards the affected side.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers, until its shape is as it should be, and that it is not in an elevated or depressed state.
What if you diagnose an excessive extension, then it is necessary that you place under the weak arm a sphere of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to the quantity of your need: and extend and elevate the clavicle, and constrict the sphere with your hand, until it is equal to the fracture, as it should be.
What if you cannot draw the extremity of the clavicle to the outside: because it penetrates to the depth: then it is necessary that the weak one leans upon his neck: and you place under his shoulder a moderate-sized pillow, and an assistant presses his shoulder downwards, until the bone of the clavicle which is in the depth rises upwards: and then you set right the fracture, and adjust it with your fingers.
If you feel a displacement from the clavicle, and it moves: then it is necessary that you fix above it, and extract the same displacement with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, then make an incision of it with one of the incisors that you have prepared for it, after you have placed under the clavicle an instrument that serves as a splint: and this instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It becomes similar to an ear that has no depression.
And its width is according to that which you measure for it from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length is also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it has two extremities, as you see: and one extremity is wide, and the other is narrow.
If the disruption of the wound that you cut when you extracted the sequestration of the bone is wide, and you are distant from the hot aposteme: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or there is a hot aposteme: then fill the wound with cloths, and poultices according to the quantity of the division of the wound.
What if there occurs a hot aposteme, then wet a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and put it on top.
The narrowing of the bone when it is without a wound, and without a cut, is, as you put on the bone a plaster made from wheat flour with the white of an egg. And put a soft dressing over it. Then put a ball under its nipple, if you intend that. Then take the hemp, that is, the rope which the Saracens hold, which should be long indeed: and its width should be a palm, or about that. Then put a double poultice over the dressing and plaster. Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and in length similarly. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the splint in the place of the bandage that falls on the fracture and the place. Then put the bandage on the fracture as it is wrapped, and wrap it around its neck, and under its healthy nipple, and under its weak nipple: and bind it all around, and as you see that the tightening holds the fracture with a decent tightening: and it is that which is not hidden from you, and all, so that the splint is not removed from above the broken bone. And for this reason it is necessary that you examine the weak person throughout the whole day. As often as the ligament is softened, and you see that the splint is already being removed: then correct it, and tighten the ligament.
Then put the bandage of the weak person on his back, and put under his armpit near his bandage a small pillow at night, so that his helper may be raised from his side. The fracture of the forearm is therefore elevated by elevation of the shoulder.
Or tie his arm to his neck: and do not untie the bandage, unless an accident occurs in the place from itching or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see its surface to that. And add to the tightening, and leave it alone, until it is restored and coagulated.
And a fracture of the forearm is tightened and comforted according to the majority within twenty days: and sometimes it happens in certain men in less than that.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the Shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely fractured, and in the broad place of it: and they are not fractured from it except its edges.
When therefore the place is fractured from it and is fractured in its middle, and it is not recognized except by touch: then according to what is the shape of the fracture, prepare its treatment. And reduce it to its natural figure in every way of ingenuity, which is possible for you.
Then put on the place powder of rose with white of egg, and a soft cloth, and put over it a small pillow from folded linen. Then put on it a broad splint made from thin wood according to the quantity of the whole shoulder blade, or a little larger than it.
If the ulcer is under the sole and there is a concavity in some location of the Spatula, then equal that concavity with a linen poultice, until the sole descends according to equality.
Then tie above with a long ahimemati bandage, of decent constraint and firmness of the sole, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the ligature every day. As often as the ligature is loosened and softened, tighten it, and equal the sole if it is moved from its place: and let the weak one lie on his healthy side.
And the Spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then untie the ligature: and you will be in security. For it is one of the bones whose union is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a fragment proceeds from the bone and pricks under the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said regarding the fracture of the fork from the application of a warm poultice, if anything happens from it.
On the restoration of the fracture of the Chest.
The chest is rarely broken in its middle, and it rarely happens to it. Its extremities however are most often broken and united from its accidents.
When its middle is broken, which declines to the lower parts, and there happens to it a severe pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps it vomits blood, and it is declared a concavity in the fractured bone, and it is not hidden from feeling, The restoration of it is, that the weak one should be placed on his back: and put between the Spatulas of his shoulder a pillow.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides or the sides with the hands: and shape it in its equality, according to every mode which is possible for it and is suitable, until the bone figure returns, according to what is proper.
Then put on it a plaster and a dressing, and put over it a splint made from a thin board of willow or hazel, or similar things in lightness, after you have wrapped it in a cloth. Then shape the dressing by binding it over the broken bone, so that it is not removed: and go with the binding according to the roundness of the back several times, and tighten it with a decent tightening.
Then consider the dressing every hour, and as often as it becomes loose, tighten it: and if necessity demands its solution on account of itching, or corrosion which occurs in the place, or pain, or abscess: then hasten, and loosen it, and remove the plaster, and rectify what has occurred with the method of its cure.
Then replace the plaster, if you see it to be good: and add to it a dressing, until it is healed.
On the restoration of the Ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a break of the ribs does not happen, except in thick places which follow the back, and from the ends of them on the front side. And damage does not happen to them, except because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from feeling on examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way, that you equal the break with the fingers according to the possible mode, until it equals the figure, according to what is proper. Then apply a plaster, and tighten the broken bone with a splint, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declined to the interior, then there occurs to the weak a violent pain and puncture, just like the puncture that occurs to one who has pleurisy: because the bone punctures the membrane, and also there occurs to them difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And now the ancients have contrived in it with many contrivances: and among them there are those who say, that you should apply foods to the weak person which generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly may be inflamed, and extended, and the fracture driven outward.
We indeed disapprove, that a cause for making a hot aposteme does not happen, if it does not occur. If however it has already occurred, then he adds to it.
And some of them have said: let a bandage be placed on the swollen place, then let it be sucked with force, and it is similar to reasoning. Nevertheless, it is feared that the swelling may attract superfluities to the place, on account of the disposition of weakness.
And some of them have said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been soaked in hot oil: and little balls shall be placed between the ribs, until it is filled: so that there may be an equal bandage when you wrap it around according to its roundness.
Then let the weak person be treated with a treatment for pleurisy, from food and medicine.
For if the severe thing constrains the weak person, which it does not tolerate, and the bone punctures the membrane with a painful puncture, and we fear for the weak person: then it is necessary that we cut over the place, and uncover the fractured rib. Then let us put on it an instrument that preserves the health, whose mention has preceded: and let us cut the bone with ease, and cut it out.
Then we shall bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture: and we shall treat it with plasters, until it is healed.
What if a hot aposteme happens in the meantime, then apply it, and pour in powders mixed with rose oil, and put on the place: and let the weak part be treated with what is suitable for an aposteme.
Meanwhile also a plaster shall be put on the side on which he sleeps, until it is healed.
On the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
When a fracture occurs to the bones of the neck, and rarely an attrition happens to them, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. When then this thing happens to someone, and you want to know, whether it will be healed or not healed, then consider. For if you see his hands already made soft, and numbed, and dead: and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor clench them: and when you clench them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not be healed, and in many cases it is mortal.
For if he moves them, and feels in them the clenching with fingers and the pricking: then know that the joint of the bone is safe, and that the weak person will be healed with treatment.
For if it happens to the vertebrae of the back similarly to that, and you want to know whether it will be healed or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are made soft, and there happens to them what happened to the hands: then when he lies on his back, air and flatulence come out without his will: and when he is turned on his stomach, vomiting comes out without his will: and when he is turned on his back, and wants to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. I do not impose labor in its cure.
If a wound does not become affected by something from it, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens from it is that you apply a warm dressing to the postule: thus, that you put on the affected spongy part rose oil alone: or with the whites of roasted eggs.
Put on it that same thing several times a day, until when the warm postule is applied: then put on the place one of the strengthening, drying, and constricting plasters, and bind over it with a ligature: and instruct the patient, to use quiet and tranquility. And let him not lie on the side where he finds pain, until it is healed.
What if it happens with an erosion of the bone or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary, that you cut over the skin, and remove it. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with a suture. Then treat it with some kind of ointment, from the oils, until it is healed.
If the end of the bone is fractured, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary, that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and straighten the broken bone with the other hand, according to what is possible, and according to the straightening.
Then put on it, and take it away: and treat the wound with the treatment that preceded, until it is healed.
Of the restoration of the Ankle.
Chapter 11.
The bones of the ankle are rarely fractured. What if they are fractured, then the fracture of them is not, except a crushing in their extremities: and they split along the length, and decline to the interior: and it happens to the patient pain in the place, and puncture: and the leg which is fractured becomes numb.
And its restoration is that you stretch out your hand over it, until you are above the fracture as is its shape.
If the fracture is only in its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from equalization, until it is assimilated to its natural shape.
If the fracture is in its length, or it has already declined to the interior, then make the weak one lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have equalized it, put a plaster on it, then put on it a splint of wood or of leather: and bind a bandage over it, which you do not fear to change the fracture, nor removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of them with what fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality: and instruct the weak one, that he sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
If a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from extension of it and its restoration, until the hot abscess has settled: and put on it what settles it, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration: and bind it as is necessary.
If indeed a splinter occurs in the bone, or it is comminuted, extract it.
On the restoration of the fracture of the elbow.
The elbow is that which is from the cubit to the head of the shoulder blade.
If it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat wood of medium thickness, according to this shape, which follows.
And let two bandages be tied on the two extremities.
Then let it be suspended from an elevated place: and let the weak person sit on a seat. Then he should throw his broken arm over a stick, until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which the curvature of the stick is attached.
Then let something heavy be suspended from above, so that the assistant extends it to the lower parts. Then the physician should equal the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns, according to what is necessary.
And another method is, that the weak person should be raised up on his neck: and his hand should be suspended from his neck with a bandage. Then instruct two assistants, that one of them should hold what is above the fracture with his hands: and that the other should hold the lower part of it, and each of them should stretch to their respective part.
And if you want it to be a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage: and let each of the two assistants stretch them to their two parts.
If the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a bandage be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then equal the fracture with ease without labor, until when the fracture is equal according to what is necessary, and a good application is applied: then bind it, if not a hot abscess occurs.
If indeed a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the binding until the seventh day. And put on it a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides: then bind it: and the method of binding it is, that you put a plaster on the fracture.
Then put a dressing made from new cloth over the plaster. Then he should place his arm on the helper and put his open hand on his shoulder.
And put a cloth, and bind it over the helper on the arm, so that the arm stays in place like a star: if nothing prevents you from that, nor is anything altered by you from the fractured bone.
What if you fear that something is altered by you from the fractured bone, then use a star: and it is, that you put on the fracture itself a star that is wider and stronger than the other stars.
