And put between every star the width of a finger, and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the stars a bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your binding on the place of the fracture should be tighter. And the more the fracture stretches, the less tight the binding should be.
If then you see the stars and binding as we have said, at the hour in which you restore the limb immediately, then do it: and if you fear a warm aposteme, then remove the binding and stars until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligature for all three days, so that there does not occur at the place an itch, or swelling, or is prevented food from reaching the limb, on account of the constriction caused by the binding. Set it right therefore all that, according to what we have said.
If indeed you are confident, that nothing will happen from it: then do not undo the ligature, unless after three days, or more.
And let the weak person sleep on his back, and his hands on his stomach: and put under the helper a mattress full of wool equally.
And consider at every hour of night or day that the shape of a fractured limb is not disentangled or the bandage loosened. Set it right therefore with all your strength.
And give food to the weak according to the order which we have mentioned above, so that at first it is subtle food, until the mouth begins to close: then it is necessary to thicken his food.
For it is the custom of the hip and the thigh to be constricted for forty days: then therefore it is necessary that it be loosed, and a bath administered, and treated with ointments which correct it.
If the fracture is a foul or bruised one, then you will not loosen the bandage from it and apply a plaster until fifty days, and until two months.
On the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearm bones.
Of which one is small: and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is placed under the small one from the lower side.
Perhaps therefore the larger forearm bone alone breaks, or the smaller one: and perhaps they break at the same time.
When therefore the upper forearm bone breaks, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm bone breaks, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when the two bones break at the same time.
If therefore the bone that breaks is the upper small forearm bone, then it is fitting for the doctor in restoring it to apply a small extension with ease, until it becomes equal.
If indeed the large forearm bone is the one that is broken: then it is necessary to apply a stronger bandage.
And it is necessary that he puts the shape of a hand near its restoration and its own extension extended over the pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers at the top: and let the joint be lower than all the fingers or the rest: and let the weak [person] sit squarely over itself, and let the pillow be before it, or in its direction, in height, so that the weak [person] does not incur labor.
Then the servant will stretch out the arm from the lower part, or with his hand, or with a bandage. And another servant will similarly stretch out from above.
Then the physician reduces the bone, until he brings it back to the best shape that he can.
What if in a fracture of the bone there are fragments, then you should reduce and put back every piece in its place with your strength.
If indeed there appears in it a separated fragment, and it punctures the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then sew over it, and remove it according to the method which we mentioned in those things that were previously stated.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter about that, so take the cure of it from there.
What if at the beginning of your restoration a hot aposteme occurs, then anoint a cloth with a paste made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be a medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a subtle bandage, until when the aposteme has settled: then remove the paste, and put on a prepared plaster made from powdered flour with the white of an egg. Then put on splints, and let the splint that is put over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one forearm or in two forearms at the same time.
Then put your bandage over the place of the fracture, stronger and tighter, and somewhat more with the bandage towards the upper part of the fracture, or towards the lower part of it: or put a softer bandage for a short time, according to what was mentioned in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be gentle cloths, moist: and not hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be made from linen properly, moderate between subtle and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the limb and the ligature after some days. What if something happens that needs correction, such as an itching that happens to the limb: then it is necessary that you bathe the limb with warm water, until that itching calms down, and you release the limb not constricted, until it calms down, then repeat the bandage.
What if the bandage is already softened, and the wound is already healed, and similar things to those: then correct that whole thing with your skill, and examine again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, on account of the excessiveness of the bandage, then it is necessary that you soften it for a little while, and release it for some days, until nourishment runs to it, then tie it.
If however nothing happens to the weak person among those things that we have said, then it is not necessary that it be undone, unless after twenty days, or around that time.
Then let the hand of the weak person be suspended at his neck. And let his neck be equal, and be guarded by its strength against agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. The whole of it is secondary to the disposition of the complexions, and the dispositions of the virtues.
On the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that a fracture of the bones of the hand and the joints of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not happen to them unless as a result of repeated trauma.
When therefore a fracture of the sole or a trauma occurs, then it is necessary that the weak person should sit and in front of him should be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on it extended.
Then the assistant extends the fractured bones and the physician equalizes them until they join with a good union.
Then it is necessary that he should apply a plaster and a dressing, if not a hot abscess occurs. Then place a bandage over it according to the quantity of the location, and you have already wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If the fracture is towards the lower part around the belly of the sole, then make a shape like a sphere from cloth, and order the weak person to bind it over his fractured sole, then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be from leather, in which there is gentleness, so that the leather may be applied to the body of the sole, and tie it according to what is necessary.
If the fracture is towards the outside, then it is necessary that you place a bandage over it and another bandage underneath on the sole, and let the hand be straight and open. Then apply a tightening, as the hand turns, and extend between the fingers with a bandage.
For if there is a fracture of any of the nodes of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, equalize it according to what is necessary. Then tie it with the sole, and if you love him, place for him a straight bandage, small, so that the fracture may be elevated and not moved.
If the fracture is of the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be put in place, and bound with the healthy finger that follows it: or all should be bound together according to continuity: and it is better. Or put on top of it a small star, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the application of a warm poultice: and apply with it what is necessary, when something happens from that, according to which the account of it is repeated.
On the restoration of the fracture of the thigh.
The hip bone is often fractured and is revealed to sensation: since it turns to the inner and back part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a ligament is tied above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And a plaster is spread over its surface.
Then every ministering bandage should be stretched out to its own part according to equality: and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the bandage be on the side of the chest, so that the extension falls upwards: and another bandage below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the bandage be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician balances the bone with both his hands, until he reduces it according to the likeness of its natural shape: and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary that you put on a plaster and a bandage, if an aposteme does not happen to the limb. For if an aposteme happens in it: then leave it for some days, until the warm aposteme subsides, then return to your cure.
The narrowing of it should be thus. It is necessary that you apply a hard bandage over the fracture, that is, a binding: and fold it twice or thrice: and let some of it remain as an excess. Then bind the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread emerge from the upper part of the two parts. Then wrap over the leg and thigh what remains in excess of the bandage.
Then put over the thigh at the place of the fracture a small stick, and put one of them as a stick over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the emptiness between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that it may equal the narrowing.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four windings. And the more you stretch it with a bandage away from the place of the fracture, let your binding be the less and softer and gentler.
Then tie to the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg. Bind therefore with each of the three sticks that are above. Then tie with the two ends of the thread downwards, until you reach the heel of the foot. And bind also with each of them the ends of the sticks from the other side, so that the bandage is not removed from its place.
Then leave the bandage over that, while it remains, so that there does not occur to the limb a corrosion, nor an abscess, nor an inflammation, and similar things. If anything of that should happen. Treat it therefore with its remedy, and correct what has happened there entirely, according to what we have said many times.
If there is a fragment of bone in the bone that punctures: then it is necessary, that you should reduce it, if it is possible for you. And if not, find above it and remove it: and treat with that which the aforementioned treatment has reminded, until it is healed.
And when this fracture of the hip is tightened, besides that the sides are added to it, as we have said in the case of the shoulder and arm.
Truly however in the restoration of such a fracture, lameness does not happen to the weak. And if it is restored alone, besides that the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary, that he whose it is, will always be lame.
And know that the hip is tightened in fifty days, or a little more or a little less: all that is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
On the restoration of the fracture of the knee.
Know that the knee rarely suffers a fracture, but it often suffers an injury.
If indeed a fracture happens to it, then it is not except for a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And let all of that be treated with sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that it should unite what has been disrupted from its parts with the fingers, until it aggregates and is joined according to what is possible from equality and facility and fabrication.
Then put a plaster made from the flour of sesames, or of meal, with the white of egg: and put on top of it a round piece of wood, if you apply it: and tie above it a bandage fitting to that.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have narrated concerning other fractures: such as a hot poultice, and similar things: so that you oppose every accident with what is suitable for it, until it is healed.
On the restoration of a fracture of the neck
You should know that the neck has two bones, one of which is thick and is named with the name of neck: and the other subtle, and is named gullet.
And it happens to both species of fracture that which happens to the two bones of the arm: and because of that its restoration is made, just like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
Which if the two bones are broken at the same time, the leg turns to all parts.
And if the subtle bone is broken, the leg turns to the front part.
And if the thick bone is broken, you find that below: and it is one of those things which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and binding of the stars according to that equally.
Truly it is necessary, if the fracture of the neck is a foul fracture, having many sores: that there should be less extension, and a lighter alleviation by you.
And in the neck there is something from the operation beyond the arm: and it is, when you equal the stars, and complete all your operation, then take two splints of pine wood, which is administered in the fabrication of cots that are placed under the tables: and they are made from palm splints: and choose from them those which have a small thickness, and not be among the smallest: and let their length be according to the length of the neck from the knee down below.
Then wrap over each one a cloth with two wrappings according to its length. And put one part of the neck on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot below.
Then bind two splints in three places with two extremities and the middle. For when the constriction is prohibited, the leg declines to the right and left, and it stands in a good position.
And now let a wooden splint be administered according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it conserves it from motion.
And it is very necessary, that you should do that in the fracture when there is a wound with it.
Then examine the leg every day, and study it with utmost care for signs of abscess, or inflammation, or other remainders. If therefore anything of those things happens, then oppose to it what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture indeed does not deeply affect the heel bone.
The bones of the foot are subject to fracture, and the toes also are subject to fracture; and it does not happen to them except due to trauma in many instances.
If a fracture happens to the bones of the foot, and you see those bones displaced above each other, then let the weak foot be placed on the prepared ground, as when walking.
Then stand and place your foot on what is elevated by those bones: afterwards press, or tread on it: and make it even, until it returns to its places.
Then apply a plaster, and put a dressing over it: and place under the sole of the foot a small board, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it with the foot by a suitable ligature, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things which it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed for them, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones even, whether they were fractured, or dislocated. The operation therefore is in them, as you see.
If some of the fingers are broken, then restore them, and make them equal according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then put a bar on your finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than that: then place under the foot this board which I have described to you, and tighten it with a decent strap.
If indeed that which is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then put on top of each finger a bar of its own quantity, wrapped in a linen cloth: and tighten the board under the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, that you do not forget, but consider all things that I have told you regarding the remaining fractures from accidents which we have mentioned: and apply to each accident what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the breastbone, and the rod of a man.
When the vulva of a woman is broken, then make her sit squarely. Then bend her slightly to the back part of the same, and let it be held from behind.
Then fill up the vulva with a sponge, until it fills it: and let it become in the vulva like a sphere.
Then strike the woman, and raise her back little by little. For that sponge comes out, until it becomes before the entrance of the vulva like a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then put a pillow on her back. When she gets up to urinate, remove the sponge with ease, then she urinates: and put it back according to the manner in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her first pillow.
It should be done for seven days, or thereabout, because the mouth itself is restored.
And if you need to, take a vesica of a reed, and tie a thread of papyrus over its opening, and insert the entire vesica into its vulva. Then blow into the tube with force, until the vesica is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture will be restored.
Then let it be filled with a paste, and let it stand for some days, according to what we have narrated, until it is healed.
And when the jawbone of a man or a woman is fractured, then administer its restoration, and its alignment according to what we have narrated concerning the bone of the ankle.
And let not the rectitude be hidden in these near fractures, which little touch, especially to one who has something of study, or use.
And understand my book, which is much, for the fracture itself shows you in many dispositions the right way in its restoration, and its alignment: understand therefore.
When a man's rod is fractured, take the gullet of a goose, and insert the rod into it. Then put over it a dressing made from cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or about that time, and it will heal, if God wills.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones, when there is with a wound.
It is fitting for one to whom a fracture with a wound happens, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the like, that bloodletting be applied immediately: if the conditions of bloodletting are attested to you, as we have previously stated.
For if the wound flows with blood, then it is necessary, that you proceed to the cutting of it, so that you powder over it a little powdered zeolite: if there is not present to you another thing.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if an abscess does not occur.
If a hot abscess occurs, then desist from the restoration of it until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause bad accidents to happen to it.
If the fractured bone is protruding above the skin, then it is necessary that you should gently reduce it and adjust it with your hands with ease and slight extension.
If the extension of it and its adjustment with your hands is not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And there is an instrument made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingerbreadths, and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And on account of that it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the quantity that he needs for the cure in every type of fracture.
And let it be round and let there be a small thickness in it so that it does not double at the time of compression during the operation: and let it have a sharp point, which has a margin at its extremities. And let its upper part be thick from the middle of it to the lower parts.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that the pointed curved end of it be placed over the end of the prominent bone: and drive it with one blow, until when the bone returns and is adjusted by some adjustment.
Therefore, take care to adjust the ends of the fracture to each other.
If the fractured ends are thin and do not take the instrument with a good grip, then it is necessary that you should cut the end of the bone until the instrument has power over it.
If you cannot reduce the bone with what we have described, then cut it with what is suitable for it from the incisions that we mentioned: or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape what remains in the bone from roughness and small cortices.
When therefore the bone is reduced and the weak one finds after its reduction a vehement and piercing pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do it therefore. For you confer on the weak one a great benefit, when your restoration is completed which you do to the bone.
Then submerge a cloth in black styptic wine. And especially if it is in summer. And do not apply a cerate over the wound, nor anything in which there is oil, so that it does not cause to happen in it putrefaction and corruption.
Then administer the stars at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And uncover the wound: thus, that you cut with a fork a hole in the bandages according to the quantity of the wound.
And beware with all caution, lest you bind the wound with a fracture. For many foolish doctors have done this: and have caused to happen to their patients either death, or corruption, or ichor, that is, pus. And let your dressing be soft, gentle, different from the dressings of fractures and other things.
For if the wound is bad, or it is a great wound, and you find on it certain bad accidents which we have described, and you find pain in the suspended place: then it is not fitting that you apply the stars to it. And make for them bandages from hard cloths in the place of the stars, and bind it with them.
When it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from above the dressing that you placed on it with wine. Then apply plasters and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as the four-drug ointment and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every day morning and evening, until it heals and becomes whole.
And it is necessary that the member be prepared in such a way that pus runs out of it downwards with ease.
If many days pass for the wound and it does not heal, nor is the pus separated from it: then know that there are small pieces of bone there. Therefore it is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. Whatever of those small pieces of bone are separated, remove them and extract them. And those of them that are not separated and prick the member and cause pain, try to cut around them and remove them in every way that is possible for you.
If there occurs to the wound zuchen or ekileten, that is, eschar, or another species of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you oppose each one of them with what is suitable for it from the cure, the account of which has been given in its own chapter.
And from that in which it is necessary that my speech stands, and to which you should direct your attention when the bone is fractured, and protrudes over the member, such as the hip bone, and the shoulder, and similar ones of the large members, it is that you should not attempt to reduce the fracture, for many times death occurs from this, rather leave it alone until it becomes putrid.
The dressing will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then attend to the cure of the wound, if it seems so to you: and if not, leave it alone.
On the cure of swelling in a fracture.
This swelling often occurs in the healing of a fracture. Especially one that is near the joints, and is disfigured from the shape of the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from performing its natural function.
Consider then if the swelling is recent: administer to it medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and gum arabic, and similar things.
Take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or with the whites of eggs, or with vinegar: and apply them to the swelling in a poultice, and bind them tightly around it.
And leave the dressing on, and do not remove it for many days. Then untie it, and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a strip of lead sheeting. For lead has the property of removing every swelling from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone, and is hard, and necessity urgently demands its removal: then cut into it from the top part of it, and incise the prominent excess: or shave it with certain razors, until it is removed: and treat it, until it is healed.
On the cure when restoration occurs, and a limb remains after that subtle, not according to its own nature.
When a fracture of the bone is restored, and the limb remains after that weak and subtle: then this does not happen except for many reasons. One of them is the multitude of the solution of the ligament and its ligation not according to what is necessary: either because of the excess of tightness of the ligaments, until nourishment is prohibited from running to the member: or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at not its hour: or because of the paucity of blood in the body of the infirm, and its debility.
And the cure of it is to feed the infirm, and to make tender its body, until blood is multiplied in it. And administer a bath. And bring joy and gladness upon him.
Then put pitch on the member: that it may attract much nourishment to itself. And repeat its embrocation with warm water, until nourishment runs and it returns to its natural figure.
Of the cure of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When it happens to a member already restored after its healing that crookedness and prominence of the fractured bone or knot occur, and the form of the member is disfigured by it, nevertheless the member is not prohibited from its natural operation: then it is not necessary that you take the advice of him who thinks that the member should be broken again.
And now there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is very reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, as if it were mortal.
But if the lameness and knot are recent: then it is necessary that an embrocation be made with warm water, in which are decocted softening herbs, such as the leaves of marshmallow, and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And let a plaster be made for it with a softening plaster, such as diachylon well prepared.
Let there be applied a poultice of the root of altea, and let it be mixed with hen's fat or with oil of sesanum, and let it be plastered on.
Let there be applied fat figs, and let them be rubbed with dove's dung, and similar things which are named from medicines that diminish consolidation.
And when the knot is dissolved with continuous, gentle, and soft rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And let movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
If however the twisting has already come beforehand, and it has become strong, and it has turned into a stone, and necessity has been provoked for its treatment with iron: then it is necessary that the above of it be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which exceeds from the knot or bone be cut out with subtle incisions: and let facility be administered to it with solicitude and study.
On speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of a joint from its place. For which it is prohibited from its movement, and the shape of the limb is deformed. And it causes pains to occur on the weak part, and vehement injuries.
When therefore a dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration: and let it not be delayed at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes putrid, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
On account of this therefore it is not necessary that it be moved, nor extended at the time of its putridness. Since often it causes spasms to occur, and painful injuries.
But when that occurs, then it is necessary that you proceed to bloodletting of the weak part. Then let it be applied, until the aposteme quiets.
Then let the limb be anointed with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced with ease.
And the whole limb should be treated with that which will bring back its memory to its own place.
I have already discussed the chapters on dislocation, and also according to what preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated at the same time.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. Or they are removed from their places by a slight movement, and become slightly softened. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they become softened to the part of the chest, until the saliva of the weak one runs, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When however the dislocation of it is slight, then it returns in many cases by itself with little trouble.
If indeed the dislocation is complete and entire: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is that the assistant should hold the head of the weak one: and the physician should insert the thumb of his hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between his teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert both thumbs at the same time, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the other fingers of his hands are outside, which he uses to pull.
And instruct the weak one, that he should clean his jaws, and loosen them for chewing in every direction. And the physician pulls the jaw, and pushes the jaw, until it returns to its place.
What if the reduction of it is difficult, especially if both jaws are together, then administer vapor with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When the jaws go back and become even, and the weak mouth is closed and not softened: then put over them cotton pads with cerate made from wax and rose oil.
Then it should be bound with ease with soft ligaments.
And the weak person should sleep on their back and their head should be steady between two pillows, so that it does not move to the right or left: and it should not be forced, or compelled to chew anything: instead soft food should be given to them to eat, until when the pain is relieved, and the jaw is knotted: then they should eat what they want, and it should be administered with ease.
He should not exert himself over the operation of his mouth in eating and drinking, and spitting, until the jaw is knotted and healed.
What if the reduction of two jaws is difficult, when they become dislocated at some hour, and do not return to their places: then often there occur from it fevers and constant sweating: and perhaps the weak bowel is loosened, and perhaps pure bile is vomited.
When you see that, then know that it is a loss, and often he to whom it happens dies within ten days.
On the reduction of dislocation of the elbow, and the extremity of the shoulder.
The elbow indeed is not dislocated from the inner side, on account of its continuity with the chest. And it becomes dislocated sometimes to the outside: and that is made clear by sensation.
And its restoration is, for the weak person to lie on their back, and to stretch out their arms. Then press the constricted place with your palm, for it will return to its original state.
Then put on it a plaster, and poultices, and bind it tightly.
The extremities of the bones which follow the elbow and are connected with it are not dislocated except rarely. What if it becomes dislocated on the same day: then it should be reduced and adjusted according to what we have said, and what is prepared for you.
Then put on it a plaster, and poultices, and a bandage. And instruct the weak person to adhere to stillness and quiet, until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the shoulder is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
On the reduction of dislocation of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder is not dislocated except in three ways.
Of which one is, that it is dislocated to the armpit downwards. The second is, that it is dislocated towards the breast. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the shoulder, and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, because of the blade. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerve.
And most often it is dislocated and protrudes downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is scant. For it protrudes quickly in them, and also goes back quickly.
In those, however, whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely that it protrudes with difficulty and goes back with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some men a blow or a fall, and the shoulder is struck with a warm poultice, and it is thought on account of that, that it is already dislocated.
It is necessary, therefore, that you should test that, until you are certain of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And a dislocation is recognized when it turns to the lower part of the shoulder, and you will distinguish between a dislocated shoulder and a healthy one. For you will find between them a clear difference, and you will find at the head of the shoulder a concavity, and below the shoulder joint on touching the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and the weak one cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if the shoulder is dislocated towards the chest or upwards, then you will find a palpable feeling, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this type of dislocation is easy, when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is, that one elevates the hand of the patient upwards. Then place your thumbs under the shoulder joint, and lift the joint with force upwards to its place: and the patient lifts his hand, and extends it upwards. Then he puts it down below, for he returns quickly himself.
If however it does not return by what we have said, and there are many days since the dislocation occurred: then it is necessary that the patient should bathe in warm water, and an embrocation which softens and soothes, such as if they decoct roots of alteae, and fenugreek, and melilot in water, and administer.
Then the patient should be raised on his back: and let a sphere and wool be placed under the shoulder joint, which is equal between softness and tightness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the sphere, and lift the head of the humerus with strength, and draw the patient's hand downwards.
And if you do, draw him back according to this method: and it is, that you make the strong man be present who is longer than the weak one, and stand at his side, and insert his shoulder under the armpit of the weak one: and lift up his armpit to the upper parts, until the weak one is suspended in the air. And let another person pull the hand of the weak one to the lower parts of his belly. If the weak person is lightweight: then it is necessary, that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also drawn back according to another method: and it is, that a long piece of wood be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape: like a mason's bucket: which is neither thick nor thin. Then let there be placed under the weak one, after they have been put on top of the head of the wood, soft cloths: and let the weak one stand according to the length of the wood.
Then let his hand be extended to the lower parts from the other side, and let his body be extended from the other side with strength: for the joint returns to its place with speed.
What if the reduction of it is difficult through all that we have said, then use this cure: and it is, that you take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: which has a round head, so that its entrance is alleviated into the depth of the armpit, according to this form.
Then let soft cloths be tied around the round head, so that the wood does not harm the weak one.
Then let it be placed under the armpit of the weak one, so that the hand or arm extends over the wood to the lower parts: and let the wood be tied above as an aid to the arm and extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed above the step of the ladder in width, and the hand is extended to the lower parts, and the rest of the body is suspended from the other side. For the joint immediately enters.
When I raise it completes its entrance in whatever way is possible: then it is necessary, that you put under the armpit a sphere of equal quantity made of wool. Then put a plaster prepared from powder of millet with benzoin and the white of an egg all over the shoulder, as it turns out from above.
Then bind under the armpit a tight bandage: and turn the dressing around the plaster from above: and tie his hand to his neck, and let it be, nor move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that weak food be given, until the member is comforted: for it is more speedy to its healing.
Then a larger one is put on after seven days, or five, and it is exercised with movement. If then it becomes firm and is not softened, then it is already healed.
If the joint is dislocated many times, on account of humidity which happened to it, or on account of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery be administered to it with three openings, according to what was previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
What if all this is done, and the bandage is loosed after seven days, and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and dressing are put on it many times, and it does not become firm, and it falls, and it cannot be lifted to the upper parts: then know that the nerves which are at the head of the shoulder are already cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
But dislocation which occurs towards the chest and breast, or reduction to the back, is done with pushing and extending with the hands: until it returns, and the rest of the dressing and care are administered to it, until it is healed.
What if after healing heaviness occurs in the limb and slowness in its movement then let the weak one use a bath many times until that heaviness is alleviated and it returns to its first nature.
On the cure of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty, and likewise returns with difficulty also: and it dislocates to all parts, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: since it falls under sight and under touch, according to whatever figure it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, it is shown to you plainly. For the joint is shortened and cannot double the arm, nor touch with it its own shoulder.
And it is necessary that you hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a warm aposteme happens to it.
For if a warm aposteme happens to it, its reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not healed at all, especially when it is to the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocation, and a more vehement pain is to them: and many times death descends with it.
And its restoration when it is among those things which are possible to reduce, is that the hand of it is extended with two hands: and its arm is stretched out, and the hands of the physician are above the elbow and below it, and he himself pushes the joint with his thumbs simultaneously, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he doubles his hand with his own hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
What if it does not obey dislocation to reduction, then administer a vehement extension, a strong one; and it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and two assistants should hold it too, so that it is not moved during extension.
Then the arm should be turned to any part after a double cloth or a broad bandage has been wrapped over the hand.
And when the physician touches his joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil: so that it is one of those things which help with the slipperiness of the joint and with ease. Then he should push the joint with a strong push until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed over it, in which there is astringency and excitation with the whites of eggs: and that it be bound with a decent bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the invalid, and let it be left for some days.
Then let it be loosened. If then the joint is stabilized in its place, then undo the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being well bound, then apply another plaster, and bandage, and leave it for some days, until it is bound. Then loosen it.
If however heaviness occurs to it after the reduction of the joint, and the arrangement is in motion: then administer a moistening in bathing, and subtle rubbing, and compression, until it is soothed, or place on the joint of the ilium, that is, the fat of the hog's belly. Then let it be bound, and leave it over it for a day and a night. Then remove it, and put it into a bath. When it sweats, then rub the joint with a moderate rubbing. Then anoint it again with the same ointment one time, and a second, and a third with the entrance of the bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, put on it a warm and moist poultice of dung, heated with butter, and bind it over.
Do that several times. It itself becomes soft, and returns to its own condition.
Of the cure of the dislocation of Almahassem.
The shoulder is often dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary, of the rest of the joints. But truly it is necessary, that there be speed in the reduction of its dislocation in the hour in which it becomes dislocated, before the place becomes infected: or an infection of heat occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is, that you put the dislocated shoulder on a table, and an assistant extends the hand of it: and the physician puts his palm on the prominence of the joint, and presses it, until it returns.
But truly it is necessary, that it be considered, if the palm has already receded to the thumb of the hand: then let the weak one put the back of his hand on the table at the extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let the position of the hand be turned inside on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand be put on the table, so that the hand of the physician falls on the same prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good: and if not, then bind it with a plaster to calm the infection: and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you achieve reduction of it, after the days have passed, truly the joint remains according to its deformity: and nothing hurts the weak one, except that the hand becomes softened, and cannot grasp anything: then know that the nerves are cut, or injured.
There is therefore no ingenuity in it, except that it should be seared with a cautery near the fall of it. And perhaps it does not bring together that something.
When therefore Almahassem does not cause to return, then put on it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be seared and left for five days: afterwards let it be undone and left, and the hand exercised.
If however the motion of it is deprived, and there occurs to it something heavy: then let it be soothed with warm water, and rubbed several times, until it is soothed.
On the cure of dislocations of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated to any part, or a finger to the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the finger, and push back the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then hold the head of the finger, and suspend it in the opposite direction to that in which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it becomes straight on its own day.
When therefore it has been night, bind it according to the same description: and do not cease to unbind it by day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it by night.
Do that for some days, until it becomes seared.
And do similarly with it, if it is dislocated to the palm of the hand: and bind it to the same part, and perform your first operation with it, until it is healed.
And do similarly with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
On the cure of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When there occurs to anyone a dislocation of the vertebrae of the back, or a dislocation of the neck, either complete or with many vertebrae out of their places: then there is no cure for it, because death quickly follows the infirmity.
And the sign of it is that the weak evacuation comes out without control, and it cannot hold it: and often some of its members become soft because of it, or two of its legs, or two of its arms, or one of them: or one vertebra is displaced from its place.
For often it is moved, because often a dislocation to the four parts is made.
That therefore which is dislocated to the back, is called Gibbosity.
The treatment of it is, that you should consider then, if the gibbosity occurs from youth: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any healing from it.
In that, however, which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to those, the ancients have already spoken of the methods of treatment in lengthy discourse. Whose benefit is not returned with profit.
I have already abbreviated from that which excuses little because of its great length from my closeness to the intention: and my exposition, which I have expounded it, and formed the instrument of it in a different manner than that which they showed it, and expounded.
I say therefore, that in the gibbosity which occurs from the front part, there is no device, nor healing from it.
And likewise in that which occurs to the two sides also.
And it is not treated from them, unless that which occurs in the back properly, with what I tell: and it is, that the weak person is stretched out on his face on a level platform near the wall: and a moist cloth is spread under him, so that his chest is not hurt.
Then a straight stick is fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, near his head: and another stick near his feet at the other end of the platform: and an assistant holds the stick, and it is not fixed in the hole. And one servant should hold the other.
Then tie above the head of the weak person, and under its pillows a linen bandage, firm, and extend the extremity of the bandage to the stick which is near its head, and tie it on that.
Then bind another bandage above its hips, and above its knees, and at its ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them on another stick which is near its feet.
Then each servant should pull the bandage to the part of the stick, and the two sticks should not be moved from their places where they are fixed: nevertheless they should not be tightened, as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with force, until it returns to its place, or he should place a board on it. Then he should attach his feet on the board, until it returns to its place.
If however it does not return to its place through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three cubits. And bore into the wall which we spoke of, that is near the weak person, a place for the extremity of the board to enter. Then place the middle of the board on the spine. And the physician should put his feet on the other extremity. And he should pull firmly, until the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with the lathe that turns with your hand: and it is, as if you fix in the ground near the head of the weak person at the end of the bed two sticks, whose length is one cubit: and near its head at the end of the bed two sticks.
And let the length that is between each stick be one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place where the lathe runs: and each stick should be firmly fixed in the ground, so that it does not move at all.
And a round stick is inserted: and it is turned on which the ligature revolves in two holes of two sticks.
And at its end is a hole, in which the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm with what revolves: and in two other sticks there is similarly one of them.
Then tighten the ligatures that you tightened on the chest with the turnstile that is at its head: and the ligatures that you tightened on its shins with the turnstile that is at its feet.
Then let a servant stand at each turnstile, who twists his hand with the cloth, with which the turnstile revolves: and the physician adjusts the Spondilem, according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turnstile, which follows.
Then after the Spondilis returns, and the place is adjusted, then it is necessary that you put an excitatory plaster with egg white. Then a dressing. Afterwards put on top of the plaster a splint made from a board, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length such quantity as to cover the place of the gibbosity, and over the healthy part of the Spondilem.
Then let it be tied with a ligature that we have said. And let the weak person use subtle nourishment, until he is healed.
What if something of elevation remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the administration of a board as we have described for a long time. And now let it be applied on that plate of lead.
And when there occurs an elevation in the last vertebrae of the back: and it is thought to be through that a dislocation, and it is that bone which now protrudes. It is not suitable that a supposition should be made for him with this medicine: perhaps it will cause death.
On the cure of a dislocated shoulder.
You should know that the joints of the shoulder and the joints of the arm do not undergo anything except dislocation, nor does what happens to other joints from slight movement and concavity happen to them.
And the joint of the shoulder is dislocated in four directions. That is, it dislocates to the front and to the back.
And most often it dislocates inwardly, and a little to the front and to the back.
And the treatment of its dislocation inwardly is: that when you join the weak leg to the healthy one, it is longer: and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak one cannot bend its foot at the groin: and the place which follows the groin is anointed with a warm apostema, because the head of the hip has already reached there.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation outwardly happens is that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation forwardly happens is that it stretches out the healthy leg according to its full length: nevertheless it does not bend it without pain which is in the knee: and if it tries to walk, it cannot step forward on it: and its urine is retained, and its groin is anointed, and upon walking there is a gait with the foot over the heel.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation backwardly happens is that it does not stretch out the knee, nor can it bend it before it bends the groin: and its leg is also shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the hip is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this dislocation of the species is to be considered, as you would consider then, if the dislocation is old, which has already become ancient with the one having it. Then, if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its own disposition: then there is not in it a cure at all. Not so it is fitting, that a deep probing should be made to it.
In that one however whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation: then it is proper to this, that you should move the joint, and extend it to the interior and to the exterior, and move it to the right and to the left: for perhaps it returns, and does not need another cure.
If however it does not return in this manner, then it is fitting, that you should prepare a strong minister, and he should extend the leg of it from the lower part: either with his hands, or with a ligature, which is tied above its leg above the knee: and another minister extends his hand above, so that he inserts his hands under its armpits. Then he should tie with a soft ligature above the root of the hip, and hold the end of the other ligature.
And its extension is either from the front part of the groin to the part of the armpit, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let its extension be for all of them as one, until the weak one is lifted with its body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this indeed is a species of extension which is a common species of the four species.
If then the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every species that which I will say about its own cure.
In the reduction of it indeed, when the dislocation is to the interior, it is fitting that the weak one should be placed on his healthy side. Then he should put a ligature on the root of the hip, where it is between the head of the hip and the place that is under the groin. Then he should extend the ligature upwards from the part of the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the thigh.
Then another strong servant should take his arms and cut the thick part of the weak hip and stretch it to the outer extension of the dislocation. For it has now returned to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced.
But if it protrudes above you and does not obey you at the entrance with this type of treatment altogether, then it is necessary that the two weak feet be bound together with a strong ligature, gentle, over the two ankles and over the two knees: and let the length of each be from its fellow as much as four fingers, and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the weak one should be suspended over a wooden beam that is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then instruct a strong youth to support the head of the hip: and let another youth be suspended with the weak one: and let another youth push, supporting the hip with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the reduction of it to its proper place, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the weak one lie on a couch, according to what we have described in the case of having a hump. And let a ligature be tied over his leg, weak by nature, and over his chest.
Then two wooden beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then an additional wooden beam should be placed in the middle of the couch, fixed well: over which wet cloths should be wrapped, so that it does not harm the weak one: and let the beam be between his hips, so that it is not pulled downwards at the extension.
Then the assistant should stretch out to his own side, and the physician should achieve reduction with his own hand.
If then it cuts to the turning back, it will be good.
And if not, put on it a plate, and press on it, as we said in the case of hunchback, equally. However it is suitable, that the weak one should lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is suitable that the weak leg should be stretched out quickly: and it is according to this same narrative on the couch: and the physician should put the palm of his right hand on the weak groin. Then he should hold it with his other hand, and with that holding put it extended downwards to the knee joint.
And if the dislocation is to the back: then it is not suitable that the weak one should be stretched out downwards and be raised above the ground: but rather it is suitable that he should be placed on a hard surface, as it is also suitable if his hip dislocates outwardly, according to what we said about him lying on both hips: and it is on his face, and the ligaments are extended: according to what we said just before.
And it is suitable that compression should be administered with a plate also on the place from which the joint protrudes, when the reduction is completed according to what is suitable.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is that you should stretch out the two weak legs: and when you see them equal, and the weak one draws in his leg, and stretches it out without effort: then you should know that the limb has returned according to what is suitable: then you should join the two hips, and put on a plaster, and bind with a wrap, that is, a bandage: so that it may not move the hip to any side: and let the weak one adhere to quiet for three days, or four.
Then untie the bandage, and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in condition, then you will know that the displacement has already been fixed: therefore set the weak one to walking.
And if you see something of softening: then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it for three days.
Then untie it and make it walk slowly on both for some days, until it is well comforted.
Of the Dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated to the outside, to the inside, and to the bottom, namely to the back.
And it is not dislocated to the front.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you will make the weak one apply his leg to his hip. If it does not stick to the hip, then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is, that you make the weak one sit upright, so that it now extends its leg if it can over that, and you stick your back to his face: and put his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee stick to your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with force. And another assistant extends his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of the return is, that the leg sticks to the hip with gentleness, not laboriously.
Then plaster it, and make the leg stick to the hip. Then bind them together with a bandage for three days, or four. Then loosen them.
And he should not walk at all for some days, until he is cured.
If however the reduction of it refuses you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, the description of which has preceded in the cure of hernia, until it returns.
On the cure of dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes put back with a little manipulation, and sometimes it is displaced according to its completion. And its dislocation occurs, either to the inside or to the outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, bulging out towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its reduction is easy: and it is that it should be extended with ease by the hands, and adjusted, until it returns.
In the cure of it when it is dislocated according to its completion, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright: and a strong assistant should hold him firmly from behind, in the middle of his back.
Then you should hold with your right hand his foot from the top of it, and with your left hand from the bottom of his foot at the place of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards yourself with your right hand. Then with the left hand in two places.
Then extend it with the left, and push the bottom of the foot with the right, turning the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then push the bottom of the foot to the leg in a third place, and you pull the ankle.
If indeed it returns to one place, or to two places according to this method, and you see the foot equal, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it, since it will return.
If then the reduction of it is prohibited to you with what we have narrated: then make the weak person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a strong stake in the earth, so that it falls between his hips: and you have now wrapped around it a cloth, so that it does not harm the weak person.
Then let the assistant take his thigh: afterwards the assistant should stretch the other leg, either with his hands, or with a bandage that binds above the ankle of the foot. Then let the assistant stretch everything in opposition to the stretching of his companion: and the stake should stand between the two hips of the weak person, holding it, so that his body is not drawn downwards at the bottom during the stretching.
Then the physician adjusts the dislocation with his hands, and the other assistant should hold the healthy leg below. For the dislocation returns with swiftness.
When then the dislocation returns, and the health of its return is declared to you: then put on a plaster and a poultice, and bind it with strong bandages, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or avoid the nerves which are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not tight over them and harms them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. What if the bandage becomes loosened, bind it: then untie it on the third or fourth day: and prohibit the weak person from walking for forty days. If indeed he tries to walk before that time, there will not be security unless the dislocation is dissolved over it, and becomes corrupted, and it does not receive a cure afterwards.
What if a hot abscess happens to him: then it is necessary that you administer to his sitting that which our narration previously mentioned in another place concerning the cure, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
On the dislocation of the toes and their treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated should be reduced with a few extensions without labor, and that indeed is not difficult, rather it is easy.
If the dislocation is in certain nodes of the sole of the foot, then it is necessary that the weak person sits with his foot on a level place on the ground or on a board, and he stands as if walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then tread on it with your foot with strength, until it returns, and you will see that it is already evened, and the protrusion does not appear in its place.
Then put under the sole of his foot a board which can hold the whole foot, which has two ends. Then bind it with a bandage for three days suitably. Then untie it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and secured from the dislocation.
On the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something of that kind happens, and you consider its treatment and restoration, death often occurs, and because of this it is not fitting to presume upon the treatment of such a thing, except for one who is prudent in the art with long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous: and so that he use in his treatment medicines which quieten hot apostems only: and commit the weak person to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for health from destruction with mildness of sickness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore consider the reduction of it at once in the beginning of the thing, before a hot apostem happens.
If then the member is restored, second to which the will, then administer the regime that calms the abscess: and cure the wound with that which agrees to it from dried ointments.
What if it is a dislocation with a fracture, and they occur in the separated bone of the knee, then conjoin the separation of them, and do in that which we said in simple ailments from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from the entrance into the way of fear: according to what my preceding warning has said to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting to your glory, and more healthy to your body among them in your time.
